The MUSES Sacrifice



TO THE MOST NOBLE, and no less deseruedly-renowned Ladies, as well Darlings,
as Patronesses, of the Muses; LVCY, Countess of Bedford; MARY,
Countesse-Dowager of Pembrooke; and, ELIZABETH, Lady Carry, (Wife of Sr. Henry
Carry:) Glories of Women.
THE Muses, sacrifice; I, consecrate;
They, unto Heaven; I, to you, heavenly THREE:
They, my poor Heart; I, my Loves rich Estate,
together with my Rimes, that rarer be.
But what can be more rare than richest Love,
since so rich Love is, now, so rarely found?
Yes; measur'd-words, that, out of measure, move
the Soul to Heaven, from Hell that is most profound!
A vexed Soul for Follies, that betray
the Soul to Death, some call the nether Hell:
Thence move my Measures; and, do make such way,
that they all Lets to give way, do compel.
These Rarities, which my poor Soul confines,
her treble- Zeal to you (three Graces) brings
For Grace, as glorious as the Sun that shines
(as bright, as cheerful) on inferior Things.
Such Grace you have, by Virtue, and by Fate,
as makes you Three, the Glory of these Times;
The MUSES Darlings, and their Chaires of STATE;
Shapers, and Souls of all Soule-charming Rimes!
BEDFORD, the beaming-glory of thy HOUSE
that makes it Heaven on Earth, thy Worths are such,
As all our WITS make most miraculous,
because thy WIT and WORTH do work so much.
For, WIT and SP'RIT, in Beauties Livery,
do still attend thine all-commanding EYES;
And, in the Achiuements of thine Ingenie,
the gloss thereof, like Orr, on Sable lies.
The Womb that bare thee, made thy noble Breast
abound with Bounty, yer thou knew'st thy Fate;
Where furnished was that Bounty with the best
of Honors Humors, giving Her the Mate.
For which, all Poets Plowes (their Pens) do plow
the fertil'st Grounds of ART; and, in the same,
Thy still-increasing Praises (thick) do sow,
to yield Aeternitie thy Crop of Fame!
PEMBROKE, (a Paragon of Princely PARTS,
and, of that Part that most commends the Muse,
Great Mistress of her Greatness, and the ARTS,)
Phoebus and Fate makes great, and glorious!
A Work of Art and Grace (from Head and Heart
that makes a Work of Wonder) thou have done;
Where Art, seems Nature; Nature, seems Art;
and, Grace, in both, makes all outshine the Sun.
So sweet a Descant on so sacred Ground
no Time shall cease to sing to Heavenly Lyres:
For, when the Spheres shall cease their gyring sound,
the Angels then, shall chaunt it in their Quires.
No Time can vaunt that ere it did produce
from femine Perfections, so sweet Strains
As still shall serve for Men and Angels use;
then both, past Time, shall sing thy Praise and Pains.
My Hand once sought that glorious WORK to grace;
and writ, in Gold, what thou, in Incke, had writ:
But Gold and highest Art are both too base
to Character the glory of thy Wit!
And did thou thirst for Fame. (as all Men do)
thou would, by all means, let it come to light;
But though thou cloud it, as does Envy too,
yet through both Clouds it shines, it is so bright!
Where bright DESERT fore-goes; a spur is Praise
to make it run to all that glorifies:
Of such Desert, i^ ought eclipse the Rays,
it ever shames FAMES public- Notaries.
CARRY (of whom Minerua stands in fear,
lest she, from her, should get ARTS Regencie)
Of ART so moves the great-all-mouing Sphere,
that every Orb of Science moves thereby.
Thou make Melpomen proud, and my Heart great
of such a P^pill, who, in Buskin fine,
With Feet of State, do make thy Muse to mete
the Scenes of Syracuse and Palestine.
Art, Language; yea; abstruse and holy Tongues,
thy Wit and Grace acquired thy Fame to raise;
And still to fill thine own, and others Songs;
thine, with thy Parts, and others, with thy praise*
Such neruy Limbs of Art, and Strains of Wit
Times past never knew the weaker Sex to have;
And Times to come, will hardly credit it,
if thus thou give thy Works both Birth and Grave.
Ye Heavenly Trinary, that swayes the State
of ARTS whole Monarchy, and WITS Empire,
Live long your Likes (unlike) to animate
(for all Times light) to blow at your Arts Fire.
For, Time now swells, (as with some poisonous Weed)
with Paper-Quelkchose, never smelt in Schools;
So, made for Follies Excess; for, they feed
but fatten not; if fatten, it is but Fools.
What strange Chime^aes Wit, (nay Folly) frames
in these much stranger Times, weak Wits t'affright
Besides themselves! for, Wits Celestial Flames,
now spend much Oil, yet lend but little Light!
And what they lend, is (often) as false, as small;
so (to small purpose) they great Pains do take
But to be scorned, or cursed, or loathed of all
that, by their false-light, foulely do mistake.
For, to give Light that leads light Men awry,
is Light that leads to Darkness; then such, Light
Were better out, than still be in the Eye
of Men, that (so) do, lightly, run from RIGHT.
For, while such Light does shine, the Multitude
(like Moates in Sun) with their Confusion plays;
Not weighing, over their Heads, how Errors Cloud
the while, does threat, t'o'er-whelme them many ways.
By pouring down the Hail of hard Conceits
against God and Goodness, that do batter both:
Or else, by saddest Showers of dark Deceits,
borne as the fickle Wind of Fancy blowth:
By Lightning; that does still more hurt than good;
while Errors Thunderclaps make sour the sweet
(Yea, sweetest) drink of Nature (our best Blood)
that does with Melancholy-madnesse meet.
By all that may (at least) give some offense
to complete Virtue, Wisdom, Wit, and Art:
For, Ignorance, has often more Insolence,
than puffing Knowledge to take Errors part.
Disease of Times, of Minds, Men, Arts, and Fame,
vain Self-conceit, how do thou ply the Press
Of People and the Printer, with thy shame,
clad in the Coat of Fustian-foolishnesse?
For, all that but pretend to have Art or Wit,
so travel with Conceit, amiss conceived,
That, till the Press deliver them of It,
their Throws are such as make them Wit-bereau'd!
Yet, if the Issue of their crazed Brains
do chance (though monstrously) to com^ to light;
Lord! how they hug it, like the Ape that strains
her young so hard, in love, as kills it quite.
What Piles of Pamphlets, and more wordy Books,
now farse the World! wherein, if Wisdom look^,
She shall see nothing worthy of her Looks,
unless the idle Likeness of a Book!
But WIT's most wronged by privilege of School:
for, Learnings Drunkards now so ply the Pot
(Of Incke I mean) Posterity to fool,
as shames Wits Name, although they touch him not.
Some that but look into Divinity
with their left Eye, with their left Hand do write
What they observe, to wrong Posterity,
that by this Ignis fatuis roame by Night.
Some search the Corpse of all Philosophy,
and every Nerve and Vein so scrible on,
That where it should be Truths Anatomy,
they make it Errors rightest Scheleton.
Some others on some other Faculties,
still (fondly) labor, but to be in Print:
(O poor Ambition!) so, their Folly flies
abroad the World, like Slips, that shame their Mint.
But Poesy (dismal Poesy) thou art
most subject to this sovereign Sottishness;
So, there is good Cause thou should be out of heart,
since all, almost, now put thee under Press.
And Wit lies shrouded so in Paper-sheetes,
bound Hand and Foot with Cords of Vanities:
That (first) with all Obscurity it meets;
so, it is impossible it ere should rise.
But you^ Three Graces, (whom our Muse would grace,
had she that glory, that our Philip had,
That was the Beauty of Arts Soul and Face)
you press the Press with little you have made.
No; you well know the Press so much is wronged,
by abject Rimers that great Hearts do scorn
To have their Measures with such Nombers thronged.
as are so basely got, conceived, and borne.
And, did my Fortunes not expose me to
contempt of Greatness, since so mean I am,
I should, with Greatness, greatly scorn it too,
since Fame for Versing, now, is held but Shame.
But, in that Vein lies not that Malady;
no, It is sound, and holds Arts purest Blood,
Which therein flows to each Extremity
of Arts whole Body, for the publike-good.
Here-hence it came, that divine Oracles
( Apollos Dialect, great God of Art)
Were still expressed in measured Sillables,
since squarest Thoughts most roundly they impart.
In which respect it is meet'st to make Records
of memorable Accidents of Time:
Of Princes Lives, and Actions of great Lords:
which Poets, first, did Chronicle in Rhyme.
Nay, they were first that Natures Works observed,
and Bookt it out for young Philosophers:
Yea, they were first, by whom, is still conseru'd
the knowledge of Heavens motions, and the Stars.
Who sought to find each Substance separate,
and, in their curious Search, sound what they were;
And, to the Life, did them delineate
on Arts fair Front, that there, more fair, appear!
Then, Poets were the first Philosophers;
first State-obseruers, and Historians:
First Metaphickes, and Astronomers,
yea, first Great-clarks, and Astrologians.
And, therefore, were they, in the Worlds first Age,
pow'rfull'st Perswaders; whose sweet Eloquence:
(That ever, staidly, ran from holy Rage)
was the first Rethoricke sprung from Sapience.
For, should we give this Empress but her due,
(Empress of speech that Monarchizeth Ears)
We must confess, she can all Souls subdue,
to Passions causing Joy, or forcing Tears.
It is a Speech of most Maiesticke state,
that makes Reas'ns Forces not to be with-stood:
The Tethys, that does still predominate
th'outragious Ocean of our boiling blood!
For, it does flow more fluent from the Tongue.
and, in the flowing, carries all with it,
Which but attempts the Torrent to impugn
and Rocks of Art remoues, to Seas of Wit!
Succinct it is, and easier to retain
(which with our Wits and Wills does ^est agree)
Than Prose, less subject to just Measures Reign:
for, Prose from Measures Rule is (loosely) free.
And, for it is ofter used, it cloyes the ^a^e;
nor so composed of Measures Musical;
And not allowed that Beauty Verse mu^^ bear,
nor yet the Cadence so Harmonicall.
It Is not adornd with choice of such rich Words,
which heavenly Poesi^ gracefully does hear;
Nor licensed that fine phra^e* Art Verse affords,
then, to diuinest Spirits it should be dear:
For, it is the Honey of all Rethoricks Flow'ers,
the Quintessence of Art, and Soul of Wit;
Right spirit of Words, true phrase of Heavenly Powers;
and, in a Word; for Heaven, all-onely, fit.
But Time these Times, it seems, in Malice chose,
to mischief Poets; for, it never brought ^orth
To this wild World (mad-merry still in Prose)
such worthy Poets, yet so litt^e worth.
And, how should they be otherwise? for, they
can twist no Lines, that hold eternal Rhyme,
On Rocks of Art; but much Time turns away;
so, get but Fame and Famine in that Time.
For, Time they spend in that which none regards,
but such as would, but can no Larges give:
While other Arts, more poor, get rich Rewards:
so, Phoebus Sons, by Luster, only, live!
The Painter, that is Master of his Skill,
and but with Earthly Coulors paints (alone)
Mere Forms of Beasts, has often Reward at Will;
but, Poets Paintings, though divine, have none.
But Painters, since to Poets they draw nigh,
(save that they draw inn Gold (unlike them) still)
And, paint so lively in dumb Poesy,
I wish their gain as great as is their Skill.
For, Pictures speak, although they still be dumb;
and what they cannot speak, they recompense
With Demonstration; so, can Souls o'er-come,
as soon by silence, as by Eloquence.
But Trades (that do but Case the Corpse aright
with our own Cost, (which often they tei^h, at least)
But ask and have: when they that clothe the Sp'rit
in Virtues Robes, are paid but with a Jest.
Which Iesters Memories I wish may be
among Trencher-Bustons, Fools, and Naturals,
Preserved by Poets for Posterity
to weep or laugh at, as the Humor falls.
For, Poets best preserve the fame or shame
of good or bad* since with their powerful Pen,
They give the Virtuous an immortal Names;
but, make the vicious live, still loathed of Men.
No earthly Matter (howsoever wrought,
though it (withal) be raised above the Clouds)
Can Fame uphold, but it will fall to naught:
for, Earth, in Time, her brauest Buildings shrouds.
Those Threatners of the Sky, proud Ilium,
Byrza of Carthage, Tower of Babylon,
Where are they now, with all their state, become?
are they not all, to all Confusion gone?
Where Is Neroes golden-Palace, that drew dry
(had it been^ liquid) freest Founts of Gold?
Asinius Pollioes Court of Liberty,
so rare for state, are now turned both to Molde.
Nay, that proud Pyramed is come to naught,
that, pight near Memphis, seemed to proppe the Sky,
Whereon, three-hundred-threescore-thousand wrought
full twenty Years, before it rought so hie!
Some Authors say, the Groundwork of the same
took up an hundred Furlongs in the Round:
Which higher rose, aspiring, like a Flame,
yet now, of this, no Spark is to be found.
Much less does any Author testify
what King (of Fame desirous) raised the same:
A most just plague to check their vanity,
that so-in Lime and Stone, entombe their Name.
What rests of Scaurus Amphitheater,
than which, I wot not whether all the Cost
Caligula and Nero did confer
on all their Buildings, most admired, were most!
The Scene whereof, three Stages did contain,
whereon three hundred Collumnes and threescore
Of rarest Marble (decked with many a Vein
of orient Coulors) held up either Floor.
Which Pillars, eight and thirty Foot in height,
were each but of one Piece, in each Degree;
Wherein an hundred thousand people might
be placed, secure, the Spectacles to see.
And (in the midst) the Stage was all of Glass,
made thick, to bear the Actors weight thereon;
Three Thousand Copper-statues all did grace;
besides some Gold, and some refulgent Stone!
And only for a Month (no longer then
the Plays were playing) was it to endure:
Yet, being but a Roman Citizen
that made it such, his Fame still suits his power.
The rather since he, to adorn the Muse,
this Cost bestowed; or else (perhaps) his Fame
Had been, with Neroes, much, but monstrous;
the Muse alone then, well renownes a Name.
Yet, now her Agents are so poor become
in Mind and state, that, for an abject Fee,
They Will honor (to their shame) but HONORS skum;
yea, Deifie a Diu'll, if he be free.
But, strong Necessity constraines the same,
(as Isreal's Singer did the Shewbread eat
By like constraint) yet, they are Lords of FAME;
and, where they charge with it, there is no retreit.
For, though Time-present see it mis-bestow'd,
yet if Wits Engine it do rarely raise,
Of Times to come, It shall be so allowed,
that both the praised and praiser, they will praise!
Yet, speak a Language ^ew do apprehend;
so few affect it: for, we naught affect,
But what our Understandings comprehend;
no marvel then the most this Art neglect.
Nay, were it but so, yet Poesy still should find
some grace with some, whom Art makes great, of vile:
But now such think, it but distracts the Mind;
for, broken-Braines such great-Ones Poets style.
Unfit for serious use, unfit for all
that tends to perfect Man's Felicity;
Light, idle, vain, and what we worse may call,
yea, though it were the Skumme of Vanity.
And would these Truths were all true Falacies,
(though Poets used to none of these incline:
For, personal faults are not the Faculties,
that is not only faultlesse, but divine.)
But it is too true in many that profess
the Art; though Leaden Lumpes: for, none can swim
In Helicon without that Happiness,
which, from his Mothers Womb, he brought with him.
And, it is as true if Grace and Government,
do not contain the Mind, in Raptures high,
But it, of Wit, may make so large extent,
as it may crack the strongest Ingenie.
So may it do in other Mysteries,
and that which we most praise, may most impeach:
Divinity it self may soon'st do this,
if Grace withold not from too high a Reach.
Then, let this Art (which is the Angels speech)
(for, to the Highest they speak in naught but Hymns,
Which, in the Womb, they do true Poets teach)
be freed from speech, that but her glory dimmes.
Then let the ignorant-great-highly base
revile her never so much, they but bewray
Their own Defects thereby, and give but grace
to Folly, darkened with Arts glorious Raie.
But no great Spirit* (whose temper is divine,
and dwells in real- GREATNESS) but adores
The Heavenly MUSE, that in Arts Heaven does shine
like Phoebus, lending Light to other Lores.
To you therefore (that Art predominate
great in your Virtue, Skill, and Fortunes too)
My Muse held meet'st these Flights to consecrate,
since you most grace the Muse in most you do.
And as the Sun does glorify each Thing
(how ever base) on which he deignes to smile:
So, your clear Eyes do give resplendishing
to all their Objects be they never so vile:
Then, look on These and Me, with such a Glance,
That both may shine through your bright Countenance.
The unfeigned lover, honorer, and admirer of your rare Perfections, John Dauies
of Hereford.

The Sacrifices of God are a contrite Spirit: A contrite and a broken Heart, O
God, thou will not despise. Psalm. 51.17.
A Broken Heart (dear LORD) thy Grace respects,
as Loves best Sacrifice; the, break my Heart
To make it sound thereby, in his Affects;
and Sin (that wounds It still) from It to part.
How is it (Lord) that who so seeks thy Face
must with the whole-heart se^ke the same to see?
Yet Broken-hearts as soon do get thy Grace;
so, whole or broken, are all One to Thee.
Then, break my heart, to make it whole; that so
(being broken quite, and made whole afterward)
It, in thy Kingdom, still may current go,
made flat to take thy Print, with Pressures hard:
That, though the Fiend abuse thy Form in me,
It, through thy Test, may current pass to Thee.
Another of the same.
AS in the Sacrifices of the Law,
there was an Alter, Priest Host, Fire and Wood:
So This to That, in likeness near does draw;
and wants but holy- Fire to make it good.
The Alter, is my Hope; the Host, my Heart;
the Priest, my Faith; my Love, the Fu^ll is:
All these (o Lord) are ready; but the Art
to fire the Fuel, wants; then, do thou This.
I am but Passive in this holy Act,
Thou the sole Agent: yet, o make me fi^
To work with thee together in this Fact,
with all the Forces of my Will and Wit:
And since (dear ^o^d) all things so ready be,
Give Fire, to sacrifice my Heart to Thee!
Another.
DEscend sweet hallowed Fire from that high FLAME
that ever burns in LOVES eternal Breast;
consume this Sacrifice, and let the rest
Lick up my Tears for Sin about the same:
That Mis-beleeuers thereby still may know
There is no GOD but he that fired it so.
I am no Prophet, Seer, Saint, nor ought,
that may expect such Favor; but a Wretch
made meek by Sin; yet Hands of Faith do stretch
To thee, whom men profane, do set at naught:
If Faith then, with thee work this wondrous Feate,
They will confess my God as good, as great!
To ouer-curious Critiques.
Ye ouer-curious Eyes (that naught can please
produced by Art or Nature) o auert
Your All-deprauing-banefull looks from these
pure Flames, that sacrifice our dying Heart.
Here are no Nouels (which ye most d^s^re)
nor ought unusual; but, here shall you see
What has been said of old, in new Attire,
with our Thoughts interlac'd; so, ours they be.
The Spider-webbe, which in her Womb is bred,
we prize no more for that: nor, estimate
Pure Honey less, for being gathered
from many Sweets; nay, more (much more) for that:
What we have gathered, is from others Flowers;
And, what is added, is from sweet'st of ours.
To the Indifferent Readers.
TO pray, in measured Sillables, is strange,
Familiars with perverseness may conceive:
But PIETY her self, allows this Change;
and, for our Learning, does Example leave.
Her Friend (sweet Psalmist) sung his Soules-Conceits
in sacred Numbers; and, the Heavens did Charm
With powerful Verse: so, those commanding- Heights,
he wanne but with his Muses mighty Arm!
All Christian- Churches (howsoe'er a^ ods)
with Psalms and Hymns beat Heavens resounding Wall:
Nay, more, the Pagans sing unto their Gods;
and, which is most, the Angels most o^ all:
Then, since both Heaven and Earth This still do use,
He shames himself that for this blames our Muse.
The Author, of, and to his Muse.
MY Muse is tirde with tyring but on Leaves
that fruitless are; yet, leave ill fruits behind:
She only works for Air, that but deceiues:
so, works for nothing, but deceitful Wind.
And what she seiseth, as her Subject, is
but vain, if it be light; and lightly what
She preyes upon, is such: then, now on This,
she needs to pray, for preying so on That.
O Muse, did thou but know thy native kind,
(being all divine) thou never would wave thy wings
In that which does but only mar the Mind;
but, endlesly, about Celestial Things.
Th'wilt be deplum'd for pluming so on Trash,
and (like a Flesh-^lye) lighting but on Sores;
Then, in Arts fairest Founts, thy Feathers wash,
to fly to him that Heaven and Earth adores!
Thy Raptures else, are but such Rauishments,
as are reproachful, penal, lewd, and light:
But Raptures far above the Elements,
do show thy Virtue in the fairest ^light.
O then, thou great vnlimitable Muse,
(that rests, in motion, in th'ETERNALS Breast)
Inspire my Muse, with grace her power to use
in naught, but what to thee shall be addressed:
So shall that Spirit that made thy David sing,
Make Dauies too, (a Beggar) like a King.
THE MUSES SACRIFICE.
A Confession of sins, with petition for grace.
O Trinall Union, God creating Gods,
O sole resistles all-effecting Power,
When wilt atone twixt me and thee the ods?
Till when, eternal I account each how'r.
I am (O Lord) thy Creature re-created;
Made, marred, re-made, by Love, by Sin, by Grace:
Shall Love, and Grace, by Sin be so defeated,
That Love should lose her labor; Grace, her place?
Thou art the Salve, and I the mortal Sore;
Yet with one touch, thy virtue can revive me:
To heal this Sore, a Spear thy heart did gore,
(Kind Pelican) that thy Blood might relieve me,
Thy Hands that formed, reformed, and me conformed
Were to a Cross transfixed for my sake,
To help my hateful hands that sin inorm'd;
Then can those helping Hands their Cure forsake?
Thy Head was crowned with Thorny Diadem,
To cure mine, crowned with Sins sweet-pricking Roses:
Thy body (ah) did blood and water stream,
To wash away Sins soil which mine encloses.
Thy Feet was crosse-wise nailed to a Cross,
To heal mine, swollen with running into vice:
On thy saire Skin whips did my Crimes engrosse,
So freedst thou me from them with bloody price.
Then can such Love now leave the thing it loved?
Is Sin so sour to turn sweet Love to Hate?
To dye for Sin, it thee alone behou'd,
And yet shall Sin thy Deaths desert abate?
O God forbid; since Sin, and Death, and Hell,
Thou on the Cross did conquer through thy death,
And by the power thereof their power did quell
To lowest deepes, and it restrained beneath.
Besides, thou say (but Truth what can thou say?)
A Gulf is set the two Extremes between;
Twixt Heaven and Hell no entercourses may
(By means thereof at any time) be seen.
I am in Heaven; for, in thy glorious Wounds
By Faith I hide me, from Sin, Death and Hell:
If Satan (for my plague) would break his bounds,
Those Gulphes of grace to stay will him compel.
Then keep me in thy Wounds (my souls sole heaven)
From whence if outcast, I to Hell must fall;
Where out-cast-like of Hope shall be bereau'n:
If reft of Hope, then reft of Help withal.
But help me (Lord) else hopeless shall I be;
Thy help the hopeful never faild at need:
Then, since my hope of help alone's in thee,
Let speedy help my ready hope succeed.
Upon thine Hand thine hand has writ my Name;
Then read thy Hand, and save me by the same.
A Sinners acknowledgement of his Vileness and Mutabilitie.
SPare me (dear Lord) my days as nothing be,
Consumed in Sin, then which is nothing worse:
Yet Sin is nothing: yet can well agree
With nothing but thy vengeance and thy curse.
Yet is it that, without which none can live,
Sprung from our Proto-parents (roots of strife)
Linckt to that Curse; that Life a cross does give,
not cross of Life, but cross in Book of Life.
Then happy that, that Life yet never had;
( Life that still subject is to such a cross)
And hapless I that live in life so bad,
Where life is found with life's eternal loss.
Ah what am I, but slime, dirt, dounge and dust,
Graue-monste^s food, Worms pittance (most impure)
Sprung from the earth and unto earth that must?
How, where, or when, I (sure) am most unsure.
Abortiue Brat of damned Concupiscence,
Hels heir, Heavens hate, eternal food for Fire,
A Gulf of grief, and Sincke of foul offense,
Scum of vain Pride, and froth of damned Desire:
Copesmate of Beasts, and to a Beast transformed,
A Dungeon dark, a loathsome Lump of Earth,
Fardle of filth, prodigious, foul, deformed,
Dishonours vassaile, cursed child of Wrath:
Pattern of Vice, and Mould of Vanity,
Made of the Molde that marres what ere it makes;
Errors misse-maze, where lost is Verity,
Or blinded so, that still wrong course it takes:
A Bramble Bryer, an vse-lesse barren Plant,
A Dog, a Hog, a Viper most unkind,
A Rock of wrack, dry Well of every Want,
A Weather-cocke, more wau'ring then the wind:
A thing of naught, a naughty thing, that marres
What Goodness makes; a damned incarnate Devil;
Contentions Source, Loves hate, still causing iarres,
A banefull weed, and Root of every evil:
What shall I say? A Map of misery,
Confusions Chaos, Frailties Spectacle,
The Worlds disease, Times vgli^^^ Prodigy;
The Abuse of Men, and Sh(...) ^btectacle.
Mortal, and to a Bubble suitable,
Whose slesh as Flowers, whose life as Hours consumes,
Of matter made, more then most mutable,
Yet (sure of certain death) of life presumes:
Frail life, which more it lasts, the sooner worn,
The longer drawn, the shorter is the date,
Hedged in with cares, as with an Hedge of Thorn;
Whose piercing pricks the mind do vulnerate.
If merry now, anon with woe I weep;
If lusty now, forthwith am water-weake;
If now alive, anon am buried deep;
That hour that glads the heart, the heart does break:
One while I laugh, another while I lowre;
Now joy in Grief, and then in Joy I grieve;
Now wake in Care, then sleep I straight secure,
Now I despair, then Hope does me relieve;
Now sigh for sin, then sin, so sigh in vain;
Now mind I Heaven, then Earth excogitate;
Now fast and pray, then feast and prate again;
Now labors end, then labors renouate;
Now am I loose, then lose I liberty;
Now sound, then sick; now up, then down I fall;
Now am I safe, and then in ieopardie;
Now ouerco(...) then, put to the wall;
Now I discourse, then (mute again) I muse;
Now seek the World, then search I for thy Ways;
Now am abused, and then I do abuse;
Now hate, then love; now praise, then straight dispraise;
Now This I long for, by and by for That;
This now delights me; then with that am cloid;
Now would have this, and then I wot not what:
And thus with This, and That, am still annoid.
To count the count-lesse vain varieties
Wherewith this mortal life surrounded is,
Or to recite our veins in vanities,
I may (as of the Stars) the reckoning miss.
All that this earthy Boowle on breast does bear
Is subject most to most unconstant state:
One moment makes as if they never were,
And every minute draws them to their date.
The heat, the cold* the hunger, thirst, and all
The miseries that life (frail life) annoy,
(Which swarming hide this Globe terrestrial)
No Tongue can tell, though all their powers employ.
Death seconds these, (if not the second Death)
Who with his fatal Fan sweepes all away,
At All (says he) whose nostrils bound their breath;
Thus carelessly (at All) with All does play.
One dies with Sickness, Thought another kills;
With Hunger this, with Thirst that man does pine:
Some Water choakes, an Halter others spils:
Some Fire consumes, some Beasts devour in fine.
This man he murders with the ruthlesse Sword;
That man with Poison he does suffocate:
With Bullet this; that with a bitter Word
He ends; and others end with worser Fate.
No Flesh (though framed in height of Natures skill,
With composition more then half divine)
But it is subject made to death, until
The Immortal do that mortal flesh refine.
Thus all he ends; yet none their ends foreknow,
A secret it is, to Death himself unknown:
Whom he must strike thy finger (Lord) must show,
Nor dares he shoot til thou the Mark have shown.
To some he is thy mercies Minister;
To other some the Engine of thy wrath:
This sadness to my Soul does minister,
For, bleeding Conscience many faintings has:
But wash the same with thy sweet mercies dew,
And it anoint with unction spiritual,
Then health, and rest, and peace shall straight ensue,
Which to my Conscience will be cordial:
I have discourst to thine all-hearing Ears
My dismal plight, in doleful Elegy,
With Tragic accents, accents causing tears,
(Sad tears) attending matchless misery:
Thy pitties Ear therefore, bow down, O Lord,
To these most pensive, and most just complaints:
Let mercies Eyes, with pitties Ears accord,
To cheer the conscience that with bleeding faints:
In hope were of my soul shall rest in peace,
Till thou vouchsafe to send her full release.
A Confession of a Sinner, acknowledging the misery of humane frailty.
Celestial Lord, Creator of this ALL,
Embracer, Prop, and Ruler of the same,
Whose unseen Eye beholds the general,
And singly see at once this double Frame,
O veil that Christall-cleere all-seeing eye,
On vtter-darknesse, that, Lord, that am I.
Mine Intellect is dark, dark my souls sight;
My body dark (dark dungeon of my soul)
Is opposite (for darkness) to thy light,
What can be darker, or more ugly foul?
Thus darkness striving much more dark to be,
(Hell being too light) infused it self in me.
O Justice Sun with Taper pointed beams,
Dart through this Darkness, open loopes for light,
By which the influence of thy lights leames
Through my dark soul may be dispersed quight:
For what is that which extreme darkness cleares
But extreme light of lights, when it appears?
Where extreme darkness harbors, there is Hell,
In me (dear Lord of Heaven) that hell is placed,
My heart (hard heart) wherein all horrors dwell,
With vexing thoughts (like Fiends) away does wast:
My Conscience quite confounded with my miss,
Is lowest Hell, where highest Anguish is.
Descend sweet Christ, and harrow with thy Cross
This hell of Conscience, flee my soul from thence;
It is thine own (dear Lord) it is thy loss,
If it do perish through my sins offense:
Why, sin is nothing; then for thing of naught
Lose not my soul (poor purchase) dearly boght.
In Deaths dark shade (o'er-shadowed with my sin
Upon the black pit brinck of deep Despair)
I lie, (dear Lord) half out, but more half in;
Help, help, o help, Lord hear, Lord hear my prayer
Now, now, o now, if ever, help me now,
I sincke, I sincke, help ere I sincke too low.
Remember Lord, Lord call to mind again
The drops (strange drops) of Water mixed with Blood
Which from thy paine-prest Body ran amain,
What time on ground it lay in pensive mood:
If then thou praid'st that Cup might pass from thee,
I well may pray let this Cup pass from me.
A Cup of cares, confected by sour sin,
Baning my Soul with bitter operation:
Let this Cup pass before I do begin;
Least it effect my crazed souls damnation.
O thou that felt'st frail man's infirmity,
Respect frail Me, else in despair I die.
Whose Faith (too like a feather in the wind)
Is tossed with the least temptations blast:
With doubtings daunted; when the faithful find
A calm in conscience till such storms are past:
But I (vile wretch) am tossed to and fro,
With every Storm that rise, or Blast that blow.
See Lord (ah see) see, see, how all my Veins
Do pant with pain, through sense of my misdeedes:
Behold my Heart, wherein all sorrow reigns,
(Griefe-wounded heart) behold it how it bleedes:
O pour therein thy precious Balmes of grace,
That from thy wounded Heart do run apace.
Where Is Much forgiu'n, Love must there be much;
Forgive me Much, much more shall be my loue^
I have Much to forgive, no sinner such;
My Sin surmounting, Love shall be above:
Forgive me then, and I in Love will strive
To match that more then Much thou do forgive.
Be thou for me unto the Old of days,
My Daysman so, to stay his angers heat;
That for thy sake he would vouchsafe to raise
His vengeance siege, which my Souls wrack does threat.
O tell him to his Grace, I (weakling) yield,
And give him praise and glory of the Field.
O pray him bend his pu'sance on the proud,
Whose brazen Necks will rather break then bow:
I, creeping on my knees, do seek for shrowde,
Till Tempests of his fury ouer-blow:
And like a Spaniell at his Masters threat,
In humble wise fall prostrate at his feet.
With eyes vp-lifted slowly by degrees,
And lifted so, are thrown down straight again,
With face confounded on his humbled knees,
Inuoking mercy, yet does mute remain:
O so, even so, do I (poor wretched I)
At foot but of his Foote-stoole crowching lie.
If this may move, and moving may provoke
Thy sans-beginning Sire in Love to stay
Of his just vengeance the resistlesse stroke
(A touch whereof does Rocks to po^der bray)
I will ascribe the praise (o Christ) to thee
Since for thy sake alone, he spares me.
My strength's not stony, nor my flesh yet brass;
O no, then weakness much more weak it is;
Apt still to fall, more brittle far then glass;
Composed of that, that is more then most amiss:
O how unable then am I to bear
His heavy vengeance stroke, that rocks does tear?
With hands of Mercy stay my sincking Soul,
Which were, in mercy, mercilesly wounded,
For me (vile wretch) and for my trespass foul,
That Grace might over abound where Sin abounded.
They are not shortened since they racked were
For Sin, that Sin might sinnelesse so appear.
With those same hands (dear Lord) my Soul sustain
Oppressed with Po^se that made thy manhood groan:
My load's as great, though far less be my pain,
Whose sinne's as great as all the worlds, alone:
Then Worlds of Sin when on my back I bear,
What meruell is it I faint, if not despair?
Froth of Infirmity, and Weakness skumme,
I am no other; how then should I bear
The heavy sentence of true Justice doom
If to this Load of Sin it added were?
None but a God and Man can bear that weight,
Since God and Man bowed underneath that freight.
I am far spent, o be not far from me,
I panting labor near the latest gasp.
My Soul dismayed, not knowing where to flee,
With hands of Hope (wan Hope) at thee does graspe.
Fasten their fingers, give them strength to hold,
As Ancors sure, in roughest Tempests would.
Kind Lord, sole comfort, hope of each poor wretch,
With Eyes converting Peter, look on me:
Those glittring Suns their beams of comfort, stretch
To cursed'st sinners if they contrite be:
Then, let those sacred Sunbeams gild with grace
My black dispairing Soul, and rue her case.
The longing of the Soul to be with God.
SOule-searching Lord, and sole selfe-searching God;
Let my poor Soul thy unknown sweetness know.
Thy staying Staff, and sin-correcting Rod
On me, on me (sweet Love) in love bestow.
Strength of my weakness, my great weakness strength,
guide thou my Goings, stay my stumbling feet:
My stumbling feet establish ( Lord) at length,
in paths that are as pure, as sure and sweet.
Eye of mine Eye, let my dim Eye behold thee;
(Dimmed with the hellish mist of damned desires)
Joy of my heart, o let my heart i^fold thee,
and take my Spirit, that still to thee aspires.
O Beauties Beauty, wound my heart with Love:
Life of my life, let my life live in thee;
In thee I have my being, live and move,
Of me but thou, then who should mouer be?
Celestial Bridegroom, kiss thy Spouse, my Soul,
With kisses sweet of vnconceiued peace;
On thy transpierced palm her name enrowle,
With thy sinne-purging blood my sins release.
Mellefluous Sweetness (sweetning sweetest sweets)
Sweeten my Sour (sour Leaven of offense)
Season my fleshes Lump with matter meet
For Sacrifice sweet smelling to thy sense.
O Goodness, let me ( Badnesse) thee embrace
With hold-fast arms of euer-lasting love:
O Well of Life, in this dry barren place,
Quench thou my thirst for thee which here I prove.
Be thou to me a plague preventing Tower,
When plagues ^ngirt my Soul with fierce assault:
My forcelesse force, then strengthen with thy power,
that if o'er-borne, yet not through my Wills fault.
Do open the entries of my deafned Ears,
Deaf with the dinne of words, breathed by despair:
O thundering Voice, that Hell from Heaven hears,
Break through the bars that let thy words repair.
O let the deepes, in dreadful harmony,
Their Billows tune unto that awful voice;
Let Heaven and Earth (in joint conspiracy)
with it accord, to drowned Sins hellish noise.
Turn thou mine Eyes, with fearful Lightnings flash,
From Eye-bewitching Objects of offense:
Deaden my flesh, my bones to ponder dash,
That dead to Sin, may quick in thee, have sense.
Increase thy Streams, lay open the water-springs,
That Earths foundations (proplesse) may appear;
My earthly thoughts, all soild with earthly things,
Thy troubles streams (through mercy straind) will clear.
O light unseen (enlightening all that see)
Lighten mine eyes that they may see thy light,
That light that with no darkness can agree,
O light of lights present that to my sight.
Savor of life, give new life to my smell;
That on the sent of thy divine perfumes,
I may run after thee through Heaven and Hell,
Through comfort, or through care that life consumes.
O touch my sensual ill-affected Taste
With finger of thy sweet life-giuing Love,
That it may prove the sweetness which thou have,
Which may thy sweetness to my soul approve.
Give me a Mind to mind thee, Heart to love thee;
Soul to adore thee, Spirit to discern thee:
A Reas'n that may in reason most approve thee,
And Reason most, for that does most concern thee.
O lively Sweet! o sweet Life-giuing Life;
O let my Love in thy Loves life be bounded,
The life of love portcullized from strife,
which lively life, with lovely love's surrounded!
O life! my life, life without which I die,
( O laborinth of life, o maze of love!)
Where shall I find thee? sweet love, when shall I
my love to love, and life to life remove?
O where art thou, thou great all-mouing mouer?
Can clouds encompasse thy vncompast Greatness?
(Thou endless life, vnlimitable lover)
No, no sweet love, then show to me thy sweetness!
Be near me in my heart, my mind, my mouth,
Near in my hearing, and each other sense:
Near in mine age, and near me in my youth,
near in mine end, to end without offense.
Through ardent love, I pine away for thee;
For want of thee (dear sweet) my Soul is sad;
Then longd-for lovely love, appear to me,
And with thy glorious presence make me glad.
Thy sense-refreshing sent my Spirit reuiues;
To mind thee's Nectar to my thirsty Soul,
Thy Inspiration, Consolation gives,
Such consolations as all cares control.
But yet, o yet, even as the chased Heart
For water thirsts, so thirsts my Soul for thee;
For thee (sweet love) for my souls soul thou art,
Without which soul can my soul living be?
O when shall I (dear Lord) unworthy I
Appear in thy pure Palace Christiline?
My mounting Spirit (winged with Desire) does fly
Above it self, to see that Court of thine!
Joy of my soul, when, when (aye me) o when
Shall I with eyes immortal, see thy glory?
Alas I live a dying life till then,
Till when my longing soul can be but sorry.
O why turnst thou (my Joy, my hearts desire!)
Thy Sunne-ecclipsing glorious face from me?
Where art thou hid? Earth, Water, Air or Fire
Cannot contain the smallest glimpse of thee!
Then where art hid? (o changelesse fairest Fair)
For whom my ravished soul, in love does languish,
The smell of whom life's ruins does repair,
Though life assailed be with mortal anguish.
But ah (aye me) I see, I see thee not,
And that I cannot, kills my loving heart;
Yet when I hear thy voice I have forgot
What me annoid, and joy suppresseth smart.
But why (ah why) from me hid'st thou thy face?
Perhaps thou ^aist, Man cannot, living, see it:
Be It so (sweet Lord) I feign would death embrace,
To see the same; so be it, o so be it.
Here let me dye, that I may see thee There,
There, where my Soul so much desires to see it:
That life as death I hold that holds me here,
Then let me dye, so be it, o so be it.
Feign would my Soul this fardle of my Flesh
Lay down at ghastly Deaths vnfleshy feet,
That, being consumed, I may resume afresh
^mmortall flesh, for thy pure presence meet.
O Christ, my Jesus, take my spirit to thee,
(My spirit aspiring clogged with fleshes weight)
It Is jaild too long, it longs let loose to be,
And every moment for release does wait.
My Joy draw thou my heart, that joys in naught
but in thy joy sole joy of blissful hearts:
To thy true joy, whose griess such bliss has bought,
which bliss my griefs (with joy) to bliss conuerts.
Enter into me, Sweetness, make me sweet,
Sweet Joy possess me, make me (sad) rejoice:
Eternal light shine on me, make me meet
To see and know, and love thee as my Choice.
The cause I love not, is, I know thee not:
I know thee not in not perceiving thee;
I not perceive; for, darkness light does blot,
Light shines in darkness, yet It cannot see.
Who sees thee, knows; who knows thee still does love thee,
Who sees, and knows, and loves thee, loves his soul:
To see, to know, to love thee, grace must move me;
For flesh does fancy by-pathes, filthy foul!
Who knows thee, shall of force himself forget,
Who loves thee as his life, his life will loath;
Yea, lose his life, that he his life may get,
Immortal making Soul and Body both.
But I alas (accursed that I am)
For externe joy, from interne bliss does range;
My fairest sollace, is my foulest shame,
My sense betrayed, the best for worst does change.
Here-hence it is, I like not that thou love:
I (wretch) love outward, but thou inward Joy:
I fleshly pleasures, spiritual thou approu'st;
I abject things, which things thee most annoy.
Thou art in Heaven, and I in Earth do dwell,
Nay, Heaven of Heavens is thine abiding place;
But I in Earth, as low as lowest Hell
Remain, and joy in pain, in senseless case.
Thou light, I dark; thou good, I passing bad,
Thou Joy, I grief; thou love, I lump of hate;
Thou wise, I fond; thou meek, with pride, I mad;
Thou rich, (most rich) and I in staruing state.
Then how (dear Lord) should so great difference
Be reconciled, and linckt in unity?
Ah here is my fear, here is all my diffid^nce!
Then help, o help, help holy Trinity.
In that all-doing powerful power of thine,
Mend mine amiss, and me to Thee combine.
The Complaint of a Sinner.
IN the vexation of an humbled Spirit,
Devoured in the depth of wretched State:
With fear and trembling I approach thy fight,
As one, dear Lord, as poor, as desolate!
Near to thy mercies floods, my self I set,
Upon the Banckes of thy rich Graces streams;
That my dry Soul may so therewith be wet,
Before the Sun of Justice scorching Beams.
Lo, I a mass of rude unformed Clay,
Present my self to thine All-making skill;
To do all my deformities away,
And to inform my Wit, reform my Will.
Great is my boldness so to tempt thy Grace
With such presumption; but (dear Lord) let me
Make bold thy love (still tendered) to embrace,
Lest strange to It, I might be strange to Thee.
Yet, when I weigh mine own unworthiness,
Together with thy Loves high dignity;
I am too bold with It, I do confess,
To entertain It to such misery.
I am too vile to love, or to be loved
Of thee (dear Lord) the life of dearest Love;
Yet by thy Love, to love I still am moved,
Though I thy love, to hate, do ever move.
Thou do command (give, what command thou do,
Then what thou will command, It shall be done,)
That I should love beyond mine uttermost,
As thou do love beyond comparison.
In Love thou made me, only but to love;
And me re-mad'st in love, to love alone:
Thou threatnest me, if I unloving prove;
And would that we, though two, should be as One.
Yea, for my love thou (ceaselesse) so do woo me,
That seeing me (in love) quite dull and dead,
Thou give me Thee; that I should give me to thee,
In form of Flesh, as thou in form of Bread.
Lord, what am I, that thou should woo me so.
And seek t'inflame my love with thy Loves heat?
What am I to Thee, but a world of woe?
A little World, of Sin, past measure great!
A Cross of Crosses; for, so cross I am,
That every thing I do, is quite a thwart;
And, that which is most cross, I bless the same
As that which most agrees with my curst-heart.
And what art thou to me but peace and rest,
Salvation, Joy, and whatsoever is good?
By whom I (most accursed) most am blessed,
Who made me blameless in thy blessed blood.
Then of such pledges of thy Love possessed,
And that but love alone thy love does crave:
O give me that which thy love does request,
And I will give thee what thy Love would have.
Ill, I can give Thee; that, is only mine;
But Good, I have from Thee, thy gift it is:
If thou will none of mine, then give me thine;
Take that from me, dear Lord, and give me this.
Thou art not pleased but with what is only thine;
Yet, I am thine; and yet not pleased thou art:
If thou have naught with me, but what is mine,
Although I gave to thee me dearest heart.
For, as it is my Heart, it is most unclean;
And all uncleanness thou do most detest:
Then, thou art both the cause, effect, and mean,
That thou do love it, as thine interest.
Yet, as mine own I have (what have I not
with it, that is not absolutely good?)
My Christ; but, ah, alas I have forgot
Thou gave him first, and bought'st me with his blood.
But yet that is all I have, (that is all in all)
To give thee, as goods common us betwixt;
To me He came from Thee; to thee He shall
For me, in passion, with my passions mixed.
If mine be such, as make his much the more,
They his are much more meritorious:
And yet if Mine be covered with his gore,
Then will deserve thy love and fasten us!
Then take him Lord, I have none other shift
To show my Love, but with thine only Gift.
The thirst of the Soul after God, the Fountain of Life.
MIne heavenly Head, give me, thy Member, grace
Thee to desire; desiring, thee to secke:
Seeking, to find; finding, to love thy face:
And, loving, loathe what is thee most unlike.
To my Heart, Faith; to mine Eyes floods of tears;
To my Soul, grief; to that grief, joy of Spirit:
To my Faith, Hope; to my Hope, Love and Fear;
And, unto all, give all direction right.
O Love essential! increated Love!
Love infinite! the Fount of Love and Grace:
With power overflowing all the powers above;
Or whatsoever is in blessed case!
How can I choose but love thee? how can I
But with such flaming Love be fired quite?
That fires the whole Worlds University,
Yea, well-nigh burns, and melts the same outright!
O God! thou art the most abstracted GOOD;
Which, yet abstracted, art much more abstract!
Which is Loves Object, and Life's liuelihood:
Which does my Love to Love, in Love, coact!
How can I choose but flame, so set on fire
With love, which burns what ere, in love was made?
What, but that Love, can quench my Loves desire?
Or me, to Love, so pow'rfully persuade?
And if I cannot love Thee for thy Love,
Nor for thy goodness being more then GOOD,
Yet, me thereto should Profit more then move;
For, of all Good thou art the boundless flood.
Youth loves the Eld, from whom it Being draws;
The Members love the Head, by whom they live:
And all Effects, by nature, love their Cause;
Since It to those Effects does Essence give.
Then since thou art my Cause, my Head, my Sir^,
Look what Those owe to These, by whom they be,
(Nay, more; for, thou art all in all entire)
That LORD, and more then That, I owe to Thee!
Thou gave me Being, ere my Sire it gave:
For, with Thee was I, ere I was of Thee!
And now preseru'st the Being which I have,
Better then by the Head the Members be.
Thou do effect what in me wanting is;
(And from my second Cause my wants proceed)
Then what can cause so good effect as this,
But thou whose Will is still in act and dead?
Look what I am at best, I am by Thee;
And when at worst, in thee my hope still is:
For, as no one, but Tho^, could fashion me;
So none, but Thou, can mend my least amiss.
Then what I am in deed or else in hope,
(When I am best in both) of thee I am:
Thou art my Soul and bodies utmost scope;
Thou made them both, then oughst to have the same.
If then Thou be my Beauties beauty; yea,
The beauty of my Souls diuinest Part,
(For Thou of beauty art the bancklesse Sea)
Who then but thou should wholly have my Heart?
O Love, that burn'st in Heavens eternal Breast!
O Dart that woundest the whole Tr^nitie!
O more, much more, then Crosse-wound me at least;
And let that Fire still burn me till I die.
O let my Soul melt Lord in thine applause,
Through holy-raging Flames of quenchlesse Love;
O cause of causes, this vouchsafe to cause;
And let these Flames their force upon me prove.
O holy, holy, holy Trinity!
Most holy Father! and most gracious Son!
Most loving Holy Ghost, in Unity
A Trinity, and but one God alone!
When, when, o when will you three dwell in me?
And make me one with you, as one you are?
Of three make, four; and one of one and three:
Your Essence keep, let me your goodness share.
When will it be? o when? o were it now!
Shall I never see it? o how long delay!
O tedious tarrying! how, o LORD, o how
Shall I straight rest in thee, mine only stay?
Haste thee, my Jesus* haste (dear Love) make haste,
I cannot stay; then come (my Joy) o come;
My haste is great, and I but Time do waste,
Till I thy Love, and Time do overcome.
O my Souls Center! my Wills sweet repose!
Light of my Minds Eye! my Thoughts Paradise!
Heaven of my Heart! Companion of my Woes!
Salve of my Sores! Cure of my Maladies!
Joy of mine Exile! and my Guide therein;
Breath of my Nostrils! End of my Desires!
Judge of my Life, Forgiuer of my Sin!
O all in all, whereto mine All aspires!
If thou be these, and all in all to me,
Can I forget thee during but a Thought?
If so I should, let me remembered be
With pinching plagues to mind thee as I ought.
If I so much forget my self and thee,
Let my right Hand forget her cunning quight:
Nay, let me not remember what I see;
That Memory so wronged, may mind thy right.
No sleep mine Eyes, no rest mine Head shall have,
Till thou my Head, within my Heart do rest:
Then enter Love, to enter o vouchsafe,
It is but what thou offer'st I request:
Then let this offer of my Will and Love,
Move me to that, to which thou me do move.
An acknowledgement of Gods gifts, with desire of union with the Giver.
IF we for fading Gifts are ever bound
To love our Friends (for Gifts still love do breed)
And if the Fire do more, or less abound,
According as the Fuel It does feed:
Then o! how great a Flame of endless love
Should (o dear Lord) still feed upon mine All:
Since past all measure I thy bounties prove;
And feed'st this Fire with Vnction-spirituall!
If the whole frame of Nature; nay, sweet Lord,
If Heaven and Earth, and all they do contain,
Be but mere Gifts, which thou do me afford,
Then how should Love but in me more then reign?
And that so much the more, because there be
In thee, besides, all Causes causing love;
Which, in their highest perfection, are in thee!
Then, can such Motives but much more then move?
If Goodness I respect, in thee it is
As far from Limit as Similitude:
For thou art (LORD) the boundless Sea of Bliss,
Because thou art the highest Beatitude.
If Beauty I regard, then thou art He
That art the Fount from whence all Beauty flows:
Whose Face the Angels still desire to see,
Whose Influence their Faces ouer-flowes!
If Bounty; then, who is so liberal
As thou (self- bounty) that do, gratis, give
All, and much more (in deed) then all, to All:
By which they more then liberally do live.
If Riches; who so rich as he that owes
What not? If Being; or what can be beside:
If Friendship; who so kind? who, for his Foes,
Did Death, with torment, willingly abide.
If Likeness be a cause that love effects;
Then who like that, by which I am, but thou?
For thou made it like thee ^n all respects,
Save that, like thee* it knows not where, nor how!
And, if the END; for which we all things do,
(The Final END) be infinitely loved;
Then who mine ALPHA, and OMEGA too,
But thou, to whom, by Nature, I am moved?
From thee to Thee, by only Natures skill,
I come, and go; but go not as I came:
For, I came from thee just, as thou art still,
But do return oppressed with sin and shame.
If then to be thine Image, with the rest,
Be seu'rall motives (strong) of Love entire,
Then what ought that to be bred of the best
Nay bred of all, but Loves eternal fire?
For, as the Sea is greater then each Flood,
Which from, and to her Bosom ever moves:
So, is thy Goodness greater then each Good;
And thy love more then other lasting loves.
Ah Lord! what made thee make me, but that love?
What to redeem me but that tender mood?
Of naught thou made me (which can nothing move
Being Naught) and me redeemest, to make me good.
O let me stretch the arms of mine Affects,
To hold thee to the Breast of my d^sires:
O cause of sweetness, cause these sweet effects;
And make my Breast the Furnace for these Fires.
The Iuy still does clip her neighboring Tree,
Because thereby it is ^d^anced often:
Then will I cling to that on Caluaree;
Because, thereby, I shall be raised aloft.
The Iuy spreads her branches not so far,
Nor by a Cedar so advanced can be,
As my Souls powers increast in virtue are,
And made to mount by virtue of this Tree.
Then o that all my bodies Limbs were Arms,
That I, on every side, might it embrace!
Thy Cross (o Christ) does bless al thine from harms;
And with joy comforts them in woeful case!
O Christ that did the Crosses Tree ascend,
That so thou might draw all things unto thee;
O draw me then, let my life with thine end;
That so my life, with thine, may endless be!
Thou that did Deity to Manhood knit
(Two Natures so in Nature different!)
Making one person of them, infinite,
To make me one with the Omnipotent,
Grant that the virtue of that UNION
May ever make us more entire then ONE.
A thankful remembrance of our preservation notwithstanding our manifold
sins.
WIth wounded Spirit I salute thy Wound^,
O all-bewounding Sacrifice for Sin!
For, my Souls health from thy Hearts hurt redounds,
Because thou dyedst to live my Heart within.
With what love shall I quite such wondrous Love,
That comes from such unheard of Clemency?
Who art thou, and who am I, that can move
Heavens God t'immure himself in misery?
That thou whose Glory, Glory it self admires,
Sholdst deigne to dwell in dirt, more vile then dung:
Since Holiness, sweet Lord, thy House requires,
Which hardly rests where many vices throng.
Heaven is thy Seat, the Earth thy Footstool is;
(For Heaven and Earth thy Majesty does fill!
Then why, great God, art thou well pleased with this
That thou art made but Mud for mire so ill?
For, if the Heaven, nay, Heaven of Heavens be
But too too small thy greatness to contain;
Then how can my heart, less then naught, hold thee?
How in a Bit of Wormes-meate can thou reign?
O Wonder! that all Maruels far surmounts,
He that upon the Cherubins does ride,
And viewes all Deeps from thence, himself dismounts
That he may in my Heart, (deep Hell) abide!
It not suffiz'd thy glowing Charity
To give me Angels for my Guards and Guides,
Nay, wast not only pleased for me to dye,
But dwelst in me to give me life besides!
There do thou visit, in the kindest kind,
The Sick (sore sick!) to give him health thereby?
Sore sick in Body, but more sick in Mind:
And raise the Dead, that willingly did dye.
My Soul exulteth (with joy ravished)
When as I mind that Miracle; how once
A Prophets dead Bones raised to life the Dead,
Only by touching those life-giuing Bones!
If those dead Bones had such reviving power,
Then, what shall not Gods living Body do?
The living Body of Life's Governor,
Must needs give endless Life and Glory too.
And if dead Bones, conceived in Sin, have might
To give life to a sinful Body, dead;
What shall that do conceived by thy Spirit?
That, must needs life-inspire even senseless Bread.
My Soul though dead in Sin, yet touching Thee
By Faith; and in thy blood being sanctifide,
Can it but more then live in Thee and me,
When Thou therein do more then still abide?
And since that Corpse was raised that craved not life,
By touching those dead Bones; then, Lord let me
(That, as my Husband, clip thee, as thy Wise)
Be raised to life, that beg the same of thee.
I cannot think (because I think of thee
as more then Grace it self!) that thou have borne
My sins, and in my sins, do bear with me;
that of thy Grace I shall be quite forlorn.
O! can my Soul but melt to think how often
thou might have slain me, yet did use thy knife
To prune, and make me grow in Grace aloft,
and ^lu'st my Foes therewith that sought my life?
How many thousand Souls now burn in Hell,
that have (perhaps) sinned less then sinful I?
Who held thy Hands when I did so rebel,
that I should live when Souls less sinful dye?
My sins cry to thee, and thou stop'st thine Ears
lest thou sholdst hear them; and the more they cry,
The more thy deafness to them still appears,
as if thou did their clamorous suite deny.
I do but sin, and thou do me but save;
if I fly fast from thee, thou followest faster:
Though I be tired with Sin, thy Mercies have
no mean to tire; but means my Sins to master.
The more I sin, the more thou humblest me;
so, make me know my self, by knowing Sin:
Nay more, it pulls me from my self to thee;
so, though I lose my self, yet thee I win.
O strange disposing of the worst of Ill!
mere Concord of maine Contradiction:
That which pulls from, does draw together still,
where love draws Discords to make Union.
So then, my Faults, as if they Virtues were,
wrought for my good, by thee that have the skill
To bear with men, to make them sin forbear;
and so, through Grace, to pull good out of Ill!
Yet did thou whisper in my Souls right Ear,
that I should do no ill for such good end;
But made me (sinning) Sin to hate and fear;
(in love) for that it did thee (LOVE) offend.
With Thorns thou do hedge-in my narrow Way,
that if I ere so little step awry,
They straight do prick me, and so make me pray
for help to thee, in whom all help does lie.
And as the Hunter stops up each Gap,
wher-through the wild Bore may escape vncaught:
So, do thou stop my way with each misse-hap,
when I would run away from thee to naught.
Am I escapt from out thy mercies Hands?
thy Hand of Justice pulls me in again:
So, Mercy holds me, by which Justice stands
to help to hold me safe by ease and pain.
Have I a Will, by Death, to damn my Soul,
(by desprate Death to damn, not mine, but thine?)
Thou do that Will with thy good Will control,
And make my Will thy Will in spite of mine.
Am I resolved to sin presumptuously,
and, that of purpose to despite thee too?
Thou make the Will without the Deed to dye;
and, make me damn the Deed ere it I do.
Would I, for any indiuine respect,
sell Heaven for Earth, and God (so) for the Devil?
Thou God do make that Would work good effect;
for, when it proves the Ill, it shuns the evil.
Is my Hand stretched out, my faith to plight
to black Perdition? twixt my hand and It
Thou putst thy hand of Justice, which does smite
away my hand, before that knot be knit.
The Weapons me thou gave my self to save,
I (monster) did against thy Goodness bend;
And with thy glorious gifts I thee did brave;
so, did I shame my self, and Thee offend.
The Tongue thou mouedst that blasphemed Thee;
thou rul'dst the limbs that did thy Members rend:
Thou gave Wit power with Thee to disagree;
and gave Will force the giver to offend.
So, that not only I ingrate have bin
for thy good gifts, but have the same imployd
As weapons of unrighteousness, in Sin,
and so with thine own Grace have thee annoid.
Thou made all Creatures for mine only use,
t'allure me to thy gainful Love thereby;
But, I abused thee, by their abuse;
so, with thy Good deeds did thee damnifie.
So, that through whom the seeing of thy Face
was to be taken, through them I could not see:
For I, as Gods, did them (in Love) embrace
which thou had given, to guide me unto Thee.
That I might serve thee, me did all things serve;
I did command, that me thou might entreat:
They did me Good, when I did ill deserve;
and when I made thee small, they made me great.
Thou gave me Faith, and Hell the Fruits has had;
thou gave me Grace, and Sin has used the same;
Thou gave me Wit* which Will abused, as mad;
thou gave me Sense, wherewith my self I shame.
Thou gave me Health, which, sickely, I have used,
in riot, surfeit, and in all excess:
Thou gave me Strength, which I have still abused
in waging war with thine own Mightiness.
Thou, for my profit, plaguedst other men;
that so, from Sin, I might be kept, with ease:
But I (vnplagued) plagued my Brethren,
so far off was I from remorse by These.
These Gifts I (most ungrateful) gratis had;
which (though abused) I used when I would:
And, being Gifts too good, made me too bad;
For, they made me too proud, and too too bold.
The rage of Lions, Tigers, and the like,
Is lenified with gifts, and turned to love;
But, with thy gifts, to grieve thee I did seek;
Yet still thou made me their increase to prove.
Thou Man becam'st to make a God of me;
(at least a God, that Heaven and Earth do serve:)
And I became a Devil, in Deed, to Thee;
that wronged thee more, the more thou did deserve.
Highest Justice, shining through thy Passions Cloud,
could not enforce me it to love, or dread:
Thou had no hole, wherein thy head to shroud;
but, all this All Is too little for my head.
Though thou art God, Foes Fists thy face enorme;
if any touch my Coat, I touch them home
By word, and deed; that yet am but a Worm;
thou striu'st for lowest, I for highest Room.
Thou would be slain, to slaughter Sin in me;
but, by thy death, I life-inspir'd the same:
So, thy great Mercy made me martyr Thee;
and, with the Jews, I made thy griefs my game.
The Medicine, so, thou gave to cure my Wounds,
I venomed to make my hurt the more,
Which both with Sin and shame my Soul confounds,
since Sin, by Grace, I made more sinful sore.
If from the Law, to take a cause to sin,
is much more damned then sin without the Law;
What is it then, when Grace so used has bin:
and force to fight with Grace, from grace to draw?
The wilde-fire of my Passions burned me;
my Thoughts Distractions did me quite divide;
The Worm of Conscience raged, where thou would be
yet these I did (as one in thee) abide!
For, mine Affections cried naught but Peace,
when those Affections most did Peace impunge;
And when I was in Hell, they seemed in ease,
so much the old misled Affections young.
And, Fury-like, toward hell I always made;
but, thou more ways then all ways broughtst me back.
The Trade of Virtue, I held Vices Trade;
since, more then Vice, she seemed to live in lack.
How often have I been at the gates of Hell
and could not enter, though I went about:
Thou did the Devil from his charge compel;
so, Porter wast thy self to keep me out.
Nay, when I have been even in his Jaws,
and that his Fangs were entering in my Soul,
Till thou did pull me thence, thou made him pause;
so, came I, as from Heaven, as Meek, as Whole.
O! how can I such powerful Grace requite;
that forces Justice with Her force to join
From wrack to save me in mine own despite,
and made restore, who did my self purloyne?
Had I the lives of Angels and of Men,
and, offered all to thee in sacrifice,
And, if those lives were thrice resumed again,
and, offered up as often, it would not suffice.
It Would not suffice to recomp^^ce thy love;
it were too cheap to quite t^y dear deser^;
O then can I (wretch) so ungrateful prove,
as not to give thee one poor wretched Heart?
Can I, o can I be so much besides
Grace, Faith, Sense, Mother-wit, my self and all,
That having yet these gifts to be my guides,
do yet but stand by these, by these to fall?
If I be lost, it must not be in Hell,
(though never so dark) for there thou foundst me out:
It must be somewhere, which no where can tell;
for where that is, both Time and Place do doubt.
It cannot be in Hell; for, thou art there;
then Heavens thy Seat (ah! would I there were lost)
Nay, not in Place; for, thou art every where!
Then not in Time, which, ere It was, thou know!
If then in Heaven, nor Hell, in Time, nor Place,
where then? in my self lost, I cannot be:
Yet, lost I am, if I do lose thy grace;
which found me when I stole my self from thee!
But yet, if needs I will be lost, at last,
(for grace, at last, saues none against their will)
No Lost-child ever was locked half so fast
from losing; and, deserves half so ill.
The worst of Ill, m^^e worse with Ill made Whole,
is too too good for one made worse then That:
Too little he does lose, to lose his Soul,
that, maugre grace, still does he cares not what.
Therefore (dear Lord) let me not enter in
this strict reuisall of my Sin and grace
The less to make excusable my Sin,
but, thereby more, much more, thy Love embrace.
For these Confessions written by my Hand
against my self, against my self will go
To thy Tribunal; and against me stand,
if now I do not ever Sin forgo.
Then let thy W^unds be once more opened
(dear Christ) to wash me in thy reeking blood:
Revive me, by thy death, that being dead
(still dead) to Ill, I may still live to good.
O! iuycie Bunch of Soule-refreshing grapes,
(hard pressed in the Wine Press of the Cross!)
Make druncke my thirsty Soul, that (gasping) gapes
for thy pure blood, to purge mine, being too gross.
Mine Ire, Pride Lust, Presumption* Hate and Scorn,
yea, all my Sins* which I can never recite)
I cast into thy wounds which wide are torn;
O keep them There then, from thy Fathers sight.
As much as those confound* these comfort me;
nay, more, much more, since more thou can forgive
Then I can sin, although I quartred Thee,
if when the deed is done, through grace I grieve.
Mellefluous Sea of Comforts most divine,
Meridian Light, whence springs true glories Day,
With both over whelme me, till through both I shine
in perfect glory by thy glories Ray.
Let not my Deeds, or inofficious Sloth
do or omit, what should not, or be done:
For, both are cursed by thy blessed mouth,
since Ill to do, and good omit, is one:
But, let this league be constant to the end;
For they but mend to mar, that mar to mend.
And Wisdom, at our wisdom* does but scoff,
When we do ill, that good may come thereof.
The sighs of a Pensive Soul, groaning under the burden of sin.
WHo art thou Lord? thou Lord whose magnitude
admits no Name! and what, or who am I
That dare but think of such an Altitude,
far past the reach of highest Angels Eye?
What am I but a Sack of sickenesses;
Immodestie it self; Dust, Clay, Dirt, Dung:
Slyme, Food for Worms, less slymie Carcasses;
with filth, much more uncleanly, mixed among!
Mere gall of bitterness, true Heir of Hell,
begot twixt Sin and Satan, life of Death:
Rebellion in the abstract; Vices Shell:
the breath of Sin, that baneth but with breath.
Gods grief, Men's plague, and Angels sole annoy,
since sad I make them by incessant sin:
Let to the sorrow which does cause their Joy
since mine example hinders some therein.
In Counsel, blind; in Actions, most unwise;
In thought, unstaid; unconstant in desire:
Then Nothing, less; yet great in mine own Eyes:
for, past my self my self would feign aspire!
In sum; I am the total sum of Ill;
ill in my flesh, and evil in my sp'rit,
Worse in my Wit, and worser in my Will:
this, Lord, is he thou would to thee unite!
But what? and who art thou? thou namelesse GREAT!
since thou art great, beyond all quantity!
How good art thou? thou goodness most complete,
for, thou art great beyond all quality!
Beyond all measure, thou art (only) wise,
thou art (alone) eternal without Time:
In power almighty, with all-seeing Eyes;
in Judgment, deep; in Counsels, most sublime.
But what! go I about to bring thee here,
within the compass of description:
Thou art as far past Compass, as past Peer,
being immense and infinite alone.
If Men or Angels could, nay more, could thou
by deed or word, thine Essence once define,
Thou art no more thy self, in deed, or show;
for, thou all Bounds do in thy self confine.
Of Thee, therefore, no search can notice give,
further then that thou art most infinite;
And that to know, is only to believe
that so thou art in wisdom, grace, and might.
The Sun, Moon, Stars, with bright beams glorifide,
in presence of thy glory, lose their Light:
The Cherubins (like Bastard Eaglets) hide
their Eyes, that cannot brook thy glories sight.
The sturdy Pillars of th'Etheriall Frame
do trembling stand, when thou but knitst thy brow;
Yea, all the Powers therein s^rincke at the same,
and (with those Props) with fear and reu'rence bow.
Whose Voice does make the Mountains melt like
whose Check confounds the order of this All^ wax,
Whose Breath consumes thy foes, as fire does flax;
in few; thou art what thou thy self can call.
Then how dare I (vile Clod of base Contempt)
approach the presence of such Majesty:
That is from all impuritie exempt,
and, I a Sincke of all sordiditie?
To touch the Arc was death; and, one did dye
for touching It, being at the point to fall:
Then woe is me, how dare I (wretch) come nigh
they sacred self, that stand staying All?
The Bethshamites received a mortal check,
for prying on that Arc too curiously:
And many thousands, for it, went to wrecke;
then dare I (Worm) cling to thy Deity?
How can thy grace so vile a Vermin brook?
much less embosome such a lothed Thing;
That leaves offense behind but with a look;
and, like a Viper, with a touch does sting?
What Concord can there be twixt Contraries?
can good and evil be incorporate?
Then how should thou self- goodness me comprise,
that am self- evil, which thou most do hate?
For I have been, o Lord, I shame to say,
what, in times past, I did not shame to do;
Who (worse then Treas'n it self) did (ah) betray
God unto Man, and Man to Satan too.
There was a Time, I was that Frantic Fool,
that said (at least in Heart) there is no God:
But since thy grace my Heart did better school
I think not so, by reason of thy Rod.
Thy Rod recou'red that my straying thought,
reducing It into the way of Truth:
I to my self, and thee, by force was brought;
and made repent that madness of my youth.
Thanks kindest Rod, I kiss thee, for thy grace,
which, like a Potion, did with Nature strive,
To conquer that which Nature did disgrace;
and made me (dead in Sin) in grace to live.
But Lord, how blessed, and better had I bin,
if thy smooth Staff had staid me in the Way;
For, thy rough Rod does Love, by terror, win;
and, Love is lame, that does by terror stay.
But yet let terror (as loves Harbinger)
make way to lodge thy Love within my Heart;
Which of thy Love would feign be Harbourer,
because thou make it feign by force of smart.
But let thy love be of my Heart embraced
merely for Love; and kept with loving fear:
Let not my Love with terror be disgraced,
but let It, free from terrors Let appear.
O let me love thee, as thou love me,
thou love me for my self and thy Loves sake:
Then for thy self (alone) let me love thee,
without respect of what Love lame does make.
I now desire (with more then hot desire)
to be new molt, and cast into the Molde
Of all perfection, by Afflictions fire;
since, for thy Temple, That, refines the Gold.
Lord, if thou will, thou can; then make me clean:
Draw me with Cords of Love, made fast by Fear:
Though my Sins measure pass, thou have no mean
in mercy; then, let mercy make me clear.
If thou requir'st contrition for my faults,
with Sin and Sorrow, lo, I labor ^ore;
A iarring Twin, each other that assaults
(within the womb that breeds them) more and more.
If Satisfaction thou of me require,
Lo, here I offer up my Flesh to thee,
To be consumed in Afflictions fire,
so thou vouchsafe to save the Soul of me.
Pour out thy Vengeance Vials all there-on;
make, it like Vapor, to euaporate
The Humors ill, wherewith it is ouer-gone,
that Flesh from flesh, may so be separate.
O thou whose Love enflames all good desires,
quench thou the thirst of my desire, that flames
To be consumed in those thrice sacred fires,
which mend the forms of mind, and Spirits frames.
Give me thy Love, and throw me into Hell;
for, there thy Love will pleasure me in pain;
Yea, pains, to bring me pleasures, will compel;
and make me Heaven by Hell so to obtain.
This only Boon I crave, by Grace to be
armed with Patience, most invincible,
In all thy fiery Trials made of me;
that Sense make brook them as insensible.
Which Patience still consociates constant Love;
which can endure more then Pain can inflict:
O then let me that Love, in mercy, prove;
then prove me with all proofs though never so strict.
Thy will be mine, and mine be ever thine;
give me no power to will or not to will
But as thou will: and let no will be mine,
but that which, maugre Flesh, may thine fulfill.
Thou know what is best for me; then, is that best,
which thou (what ere it be) for me shall do:
Then, let me lock my cares within thy Chest,
when they, too strong, would my weak Chest undo.
Be thou the Center of my Souls desires;
and, let them rest in Thee in all unrest:
Be thou the Unction, still to feed those fires,
till of eternal Light they be possessed.
To which, as to the utmost of their hope,
Bring thou them (Lord) that art their utmost scope.
Of Life's brevity, the Fleshes frailty, the Worlds vanity, and the Devils
tyranny.
THou Eld of Days, teach me my days to count,
(dear Lord) mine End, learn me mine end to know;
That of the same I may yield rust account*
These secrets (Lord) to me, in secret, show.
To think of long life, is, in death, to live;
To think of Death',s long life, which Death does give.
My Time is in thy hands; then It display,
That I may know It, so to use It well:
A thousand years, with thee, is scarce a day;
But they are more with me then Time can tell:
In twice five Ages, Time can tell no more,
Then, no Man's time thrice trebl'd, tells such store.
Are not my Days few? and mine end at hand,
Whose life is like the shadow of a Dream?
What Substance is it, by which such shadows stand?
Is It ought but Nothing, in the great'st extreme?
If less then Nothing then, be all my Days,
Can I love Life, which Truth does so dispraise?
A Ship, a Shaft, a Shuttle were too slow
(Or whatsoever else does swiftly glide)
The flight of Time in this short life to show;
But, It, as less then Nothing, must abide:
Then ah! shall less then Nothing make me lose,
Thee, Thing of Things, that do each Thing enclose?
And, what a less then Nothing is this Life?
It Is worse then Naught, that is lesser then That L^sse:
So fraught with Mischief, Sorrow, Sin and Strife,
That It (like Hell) is Hold of Heaviness:
For who so has most ease and rest therein,
Are most diseased (most often) with restless Sin.
No foot of Ground, Earths dismal face contains,
That is not ouer-laid with treble Snares;
A Flies foot rests not on It without Pains;
Besides Deaths danger, and a World of cares:
I speak, but speak with grief, what I have found
On Earth; then, Earth of grief is but the Ground.
For scarce is one Temptation ouer-past,
But in the Neck thereof another comes;
Like Circles, that Stones cause in Waters cast,
Which chase each other, till the last o'er-comes:
So and none otherwise Temptations strive,
Which, by the spoil of others, best shall thrive.
Nay, so each other to succeed were ease,
(More then temptation uses to admit)
But, while the first endures (like swelling Seas,)
Another rises worse, much worse, then It:
Then in temptations Seas, with Waves thus driu'n,
How hard is it t'attaine the Hau'n of Heaven!
The treble Snares (forementioned,) three fell Foes
Do lay for me, to catch me if they can;
The Flesh, the Devil, and the World are Those,
Which three still watch to catch me careless Man:
The least of which has skill exceeding great;
Then how should I (poor Wren) their drifts defeat?
On this side fights my Flesh; the World on that;
The Devil at my Back; and, all as One
Do me assail; nay, do they care not what,
So I (thereby) may quite be ouer-throwne:
And thus, like cunning Foes, they compass me,
That I may have no way, away to flee.
I cannot from my Body flee; because
It is my Clogge, and I am tide thereto:
Nor must I It undo, for any cause,
For, so undone, I do my soul undo:
If I do feed the same, my foe I fat,
That will assault me much the more for that.
Then must I needs my Body bear about,
Though feign I would forsake It, knew I how;
And yet the same is always running out;
Yet draws me with it, as Colts do draw the Plow:
It tires my Spirit, that toiles to keep it in,
From being tir'de in running out to sin.
Besides, th'iniurious World beleaguers me
This, that, and every way, with maine and might;
And through the Loope-holes of my Senses, He
With my weak Soul, continually does fight:
Which still, though faintly, fights to keep out death,
And often (poor Soul) quite she is out of breath.
If at those Loopes the World repulse does take,
He sets his Slaves to watch me, in my way;
That they may, through my slippings, me o'er-take;
And so to wound my Fame, with sharp Dispraise:
Or, draw me else before Authority,
Where I may know what it is to slip awry.
But, that is a favor done, against his will:
Herein his malice mends me; makes me watch
My sinful self from running into ill;
Lest that these Fiends should me in evil catch:
For (Lord) thou know, they watch not for my good;
But how, by mischief, they may suck my blood.
If thee I serve, they call me Hypocrite;
If I do not, then Atheist am I named:
If I give Alms, it is that beg praise I might;
So, do I good or evil, I am blamed:
Then this thrice wayward World, by his good will,
Will have me to be Nothing; good, nor ill.
The Devil is a Spirit which is unseen,
Then how should I avoid his mortal Blows?
Whose weapons are as long, as strong and keen;
And sends flaming Shafts from fiery Bows:
The least of which to death my Soul will wound,
If thou confound them not ere they confound.
So then these three strong armed Enemies,
Me ceaselessely assail to make me fall:
The Flesh suggesteth to me Luxuries;
The World obiecteth Sweetes; the Devil, Gall:
And all, as most entire, conspire in this,
To make me ill to live, to die amiss.
The Flesh importunes me with dainty food;
With Sleep, Sloth, Lust, and carnal Liberty:
The World does move me to ambitious mood:
The Devil to Malice, Ire, and Treachery:
Thus all in seu'rall sort, in one agree
To pare my Crown, if not to conquer me.
Behold (o Lord) with whom I live, perforce;
I dwell with Scorpions, Vipers, and the like:
Which kill, by Nature, without all remorce;
And with their stings, they good and bad do strike:
O Lord how long, how long (dear Lord) shall I
Endure this Death, the Life of misery?
Atheists and Infidels do neighbor me,
Beside these foes; and with them still do join,
To work my wrack; for, they still boring be
Betwixt thy Spirit and mine, them to vnioyne:
Among the Tents of Kedars, thus, I dwell,
Whose In-mates are as Serpent-wise, as fell.
Example, more then Precept, makes us good;
And, is there none that does good? no, not one,
Then ah! what can live with this Vipers Brood
That is not brought to naught, no not a Stone?
Then I being Flesh, how can I hurt avoid
By them, by whom, even Stones are often annoid?
In these sore Conflicts if I should retire
Into my self, I find me fraught within,
With fleshly,-worldly,-diuellish-damn'd desire,
The threefold Bastard of these Foes, and Sin.
Who will with them conspire to conquer me,
Then in my self, I least secure shall be.
My Heart's more moveable then Motion is;
Unconstant, fugitive, vain, light, lewd, blind;
Wandering each way, and yet the way does misle;
Yet still holds on that Course, by course of kind:
Agent and Patient it is, in Sin and Shame,
That both effects and suffers for the same.
And, as a Mill does grind what it receives,
Else grindes it self, if naught be thrown thereon;
So, does the Heart grind what the same conceiues;
Else grindes it self, till it to naught be gone:
But it (by Nature) still conceiueth Sin,
Then Sin (by Nature) still is ground therein.
But, if thy Grace (Lord) thou therein infuse,
It grindes the same, like flower of finest Wheat,
To make sweet- Bread, unleavened, to use
When as the Soul does grind thee as her meat:
And as the heart does grind, the Soul to feed
