With good, or bad; so, our lives haps succeed.
Sometimes it grindes but griefs, infused by Sin;
And often but Dusty thoughts, and Earthy cares:
Thou, when such Griefs it grindes, pour'st Joy therein:
And me, for thee by griefs* that Joy prepares:
Then may I say, when so the Mill does run:
I had been, if I had not been undone.
But, for the most part; it is ever cloid
(Like an hard Mi^l-stone) with the softest things:
As fleshly lusts, and vain Joys ouer-ioyde;
And with that harts-case which most torment brings
So, that my Heart, to them, my Heart betraies,
And all, to spoil it, seek by all assaies.
It is the Shop where base Affections frame
The Emb^ion of Sin; which, growing great,
Breaks out to Action, to the Actors shame;
Unless thy Deed (o Lord) the Deed defeat:
Then in the heart, the Seat of Peace and Life,
I find the certain'st Death, the surest strife*
Lord help, Lord help me to subdue my Heart,
Before these Foes my Heart do quite o'er-throw:
O let it labor with a World of smart,
It self to conquer, and it self to know:
They that so fight, great Hearts, and Glory have;
Then let me fight, my Fame and Heart to save.
To save my Heart, which, though it little be;
Yet naught but thine own Greatness can suffice:
For it is a Kingdom only made for thee;
Though Traitors to thee, do it often surprise;
But chase from thence the traitors to thy Crown,
That thou may still, in peace, possess thine own.
O take away these Scandals of thy reign,
Thieves of thy Glory (most vainglorious Thieves)
For, Tyrant PRIDE would be my Sovereign;
Which (for rejecting her) me ever grieves:
For, Pride (dear Lord) is of that spightfull vain,
That where she most seeks love, she most does pain.
Then Lust, Ire, Envy, Malice, Scorn, and Hate,
Strive, in me, for me; but, as much as I
Am holp by thee, do strive to keep my State
From usurpation of their Tyranny:
Which freely I surrender up to thee,
That freely, twice, did render me, to me.
For, I no King recognize but my God
Worthy to sit as Sovereign in my Heart:
Before all Scepters I adore thy Rod;
Which drives to endless pleasure, though it smart.
O then away from me, ye cursed Crew,
Ye have no part in me, His only due.
And come (dear Lord) destroy the^ in their strength,
Confound their Councels, all their Drifts defeat;
That I, through thee, may win my self at length
From out their Hands, that make me as their Meat:
And let me (so won) lose my self in thee:
Where, to be lost, is still most safe to be.
Give me (o Lord) that empire over my Heart
That It thy Beck and mine may still obey:
For, that, and more is due to thy desert;
Since that due is much more then I can pay:
For, I can pay no more then what is mine,
And I have naught but sin, but what is thine!
Then as I am oblieg'd thee to obey;
So, Equity and Profit do persuade
That I should walk no Way, but in thy Way;
For, that is the Way by which good Men are made:
Then till I go away for good and all,
Let me run in this Way, and never fall.
For that is to run that so we may obtain,
Else get we pain eternal for our pain.
If many run, and labor lose,
How easy is it to be of those?
The Soul desires to know God.
FRom out the Soul of my most happy Soul,
I praise thee, migthy Maker of this All,
For that when I was nothing (fair nor foul)
thou made me of thy Creatures Capital!
For, to thine Image did thou fashion me,
giving my Soul Intelligence, and Will;
That so, at least, she might b'in love with thee,
since all things love their like, by Nature, still.
Thou might have made me some detested Worm;
some Toade or Viper, or some Croc^dile:
Or else some Monster, both in mood and form;
or ought what is most harmefull and most vile.
And, that thou did not, it was of thy grace;
for, what could I deserve when I was not?
No, not a Being in the basest place,
much less Earths Lordship, which is now my Lot!
And, lest a Creature, so resembling thee,
should instantly to nothing fall again,
Thou me endu'dst with immortality;
that I might, in all Worlds, still live and reign.
Yet seemed that nothing to thy boundless Love,
unless, of nothing, thou had made my Soul
But little less, if not some way abo^ue
the Angels; for, they serve, and I control.
Oxen and Sheep with Grass are satisfide;
Fish, Foul, and Worms with Food of baser kind:
But my Souls Meat is more then Deifide;
for nothing but her God contents her Mind!
For, She is made of that Capacity
(because like thee She is directly made)
That Heaven and Earth her cannot satisfy,
since She shall flowrish most, when these shall fade.
For, though she once began, yet now she is
eternal made, and truly infinite;
Then naught but thou that have these properties,
can satiate her insatiate appetite.
Wretch that I am, this World, why do I love?
or seek the fading glory of the same?
Why do I riches s^eke and pleasures prove,
that do the Soul vnioynt, and Mind vnframe?
These Husks suffice not; and, these painted Fires
warm but the bare imagination:
While the So^le starues through cold, with vain desires
bred by that powers misinformation.
O no, her Food's much more substantial,
( Supersubstantiall I should rather say)
Because it is so passing spiritual,
as none but purest Spirits it relish may.
Then know my Soul, know what (by kind) thou art
thy Makers Type, and viue Similitude;
Whole in the Whole, and whole in every Part;
another God, of boundless magnitude!
How can thy Palate then, taste any thing
(without distaste) that is not most divine?
Why drink'st of this Worlds Dike, and leau'st the Spring,
that ever ouer-flowes with Angels Wine?
All under Heaven is too unsweet for thee;
for, it is but Elementall; still, in strife:
Nay, naught in Heaven, but the sweet Trinity,
can feed thee fat, or keep thee but in life.
That food, whose sweetness rauisheth the sense
of sweetest souls diuinest Faculties,
Must feed thy Will, and thine Intelligence,
else can they not to grace or glory rise.
That Lord, whose Beauty Sun and Moon admires,
whose Majesty the Hoasts of Heaven adore:
Whose Grace is praised by the Angels Quires,
He that was, is, and shall be evermore:
God, infinite in power and Majesty,
has made thee but to fill thee with his Love;
Which being infini^e in quantity,
thine All, and Parts (all whole in each) can move.
He, only He, can thy desires fulfill,
albe't they did exceed Immensitie:
And, being Three in One can fitly fill
thine Understanding, Will and Memory!
Then, o my Soul run out, this Guest to meet;
and him into thee gladly introduce:
Who is as sweet as great, and good as sweet;
that used augments, and fades for want of use.
Then, lock him in the Closet of thine Heart,
where thou, in secret, may unfold thy Love:
There clip him fast, let him not thence depart,
till He with him, from hence, do thee remove.
Who will be soon entreated There to stay,
because it is the rest of his desire:
And needs he must take thee with him away,
if Nuptial Love do make you two entire.
Which dignity, of my Celestial Soul,
when well I weigh (dear Lord) I marvel not
Though in my Mud, thy Son himself did roll,
to seek, in my true shape, to knit this knot.
But muse I may at mine ingratitude,
my madness, dullness, and gross impudence;
That do neglect they Loves beatitude,
and prostitute my Soul to foul Offense.
That I should, carelessly, his Love neglect,
that is the beaming beauty of thy State;
And woo the ugly Devil, in effect,
thy sacred Image to adulterate.
This does exceed all wonderments excess;
this Prodigy, is more then monsterous;
That any Soul should love mere ugliness,
before mere beauty, more then glorious!
How can I think upon thy boundless Love;
and not pursue my self with endless Hate?
That, for my sake, did hels of torments prove,
to pull me out of Hell, and damned state.
And, when I view my Bodies Edifice,
I find so many of thy bounties there,
As might the Heart of Hate to Love entice;
for, in each haire-breadth of it they appear.
The Arteries, Sinews, Nerves, Veins, Ligaments,
Heart, Lungs, Lights; and, in few, the All, in All,
Are thy Loue-tokens, and kind Complements,
that make thy self, through Lordly love, my thrall.
Wherein if I should still Philosophize,
I should find matter still to praise thy name;
For this Minds Organ yields such Harmonies
as still in silence celebrate thy Fame.
This Wonder is the Worlds Epitomie,
a little World, true abstract of the Great,
Yet greater then the Great in dignity,
though that in quantity be more complete.
O! how should I to grace thy Grace be glad,
for that thou made me not in deed, or sight,
Blind, lame, deaf, epilepticke, mute, or mad;
but sound in Soul and Mind; in Body, right.
Yet (Lord) o yet I want, (for nothing is
brought from Not-being to a Being blessed
Immediately) since yet I am amiss;
but all things, by degrees, attain their best.
For, in the Work of Nature, Sense perceiues
that first of all the Matter she prepares;
Then fits it to the Form which it receives;
but forms it not perhaps in many years.
Yet she does not, as lacking Power, or Art,
leave ought imperfect which she takes in hand;
Yet, out of hand, she perfecteth no Part;
but, that she does in time, in Sea, and Land.
Then thou that art her Soueragine, can thou lack
of her perfection in thy Works begun?
Can thou, Almighty, see Them go to wrack?
or, through neglect, to leave them half undone?
Effects unto their Causes only look,
that they from them Perfection may receive;
Then, of their Causes, if they be forsook,
they make a show but only to deceive.
Thou art my sole beginning, and mine end;
then end that well which thou have well begun:
Thou art my Cause; then me, the Effect, amend;
that I from grace, to grace may ever run.
Thine Eyes, all-seeing, see great Wants in me;
supply those wants (dear Lord) and let me want
Nothing but wants that wanting are in thee,
since what thou want, to thee is discrepant.
Let no Block be more dull to apprehend
that thou would have escape, untried, then I;
Let my Wit for thy foolishness contend;
and, let that Folly be my Wisdoms Eye.
Then, in the Egyptian darkness of this life,
I shall behold the glory of thy Son:
And shape my course, by him, in Storms of strife:
for all thy fools do strive to him to run.
Then, with that Protomartire, shall I see
(the Canopie of Heaven being opened wide)
The beaming beauty of the Trinity;
that by none, but such fools, can be espide.
Let me be wise in deed, and not in show,
since never shades have substances begot;
And they know nothing, as they ought to know,
that know not they are fools that know thee not.
The Fool has said, in heart, No God there is:
so says he, since he knows not otherwise:
Then, Truth and Wisdom calls him Fool for this;
because true Wisdom in this Knowledge lies.
The Pagan-wisedome, though it knew, what not?
that was beneath the Circuit of the Sun;
Yet was that wisdom fondly ouer-shot,
since all was vain It knew, when all was done:
For, under Heaven (as says thy sacred Truth,)
remains naught that is not more then vain:
What wisdom then, from knowing it ensu'th;
but such as Fools, by knowing Bables, gain?
Then let the World still make a Fool of me,
So I may only know my self and Thee.
A Thanksgiving for our Being.
LEst Thankelesnesse should close thy Bounties hand,
(which it alone (kind Lord) has power to do)
And since thou give what thou do command,
if we but stretch our Good-wils hand thereto:
Kind lib'rall Lord, give me an able will
to thank thee for thy gifts; that by one gift
I may be grateful for another still;
which is of Willing-want the only shift.
I thank thee then, not only for my Being,
(being as I am the lively form of thee)
But for that thy high Providence all-seeing
does strive to make me ever better Be!
For, should thy hand be but a moment closed,
I should to naught resolve, as once I was;
For thou my time of moments have composed,
the last of which I cannot ouer-passe.
Then look how many moments I exist,
so many blessings do thou give to me;
Preventing me with others ere I wist,
that so my Being might right blessed be.
From my Conception, to Nativity
thou keptst me saf^ (though strait kept) in the womb,
My Mothers Bowels might have strangled me,
but that thy Mercies hand still made me room!
Wherein I felt (ere I could feel, or see)
the blessings of thy tender Providence:
And, lest I should (perhaps) abortiue be,
thou gave me there, full nine Months residence.
Where, how thou fedd'st me, by the Navle-string,
I may admire, but never the same express!
And how thou did my Parts together bring
(confused in slime) it is no wonder less!
The longings of my Mothers appetite,
her food, fears, griefs, falls, and such accidents,
Might have enforced her, ere my Frame was pight,
eft to diffuse me in the Elements.
For, when I was an Embrio, but a thought
might have redrown'd me in Not-beings Pit;
But then thou thoughtst on me, and so have wrought
that Danger, from her Mouth, me, safe, did spit.
How happily-vnhappy had I bin
to be made Man in possibility,
And marred, even as my making did begin;
so, straight to find, and lose Humanity.
That which we never had, we never lost:
therefore for loss of that we cannot grieve:
But, rare things had, to lose, does grieve us most;
for better still dead, then but now to live.
Then to be borne within no Pagan Clyme,
adds no small weight to this great Benefit:
But, come of Christians, in good place and time,
and, am a Christian, much more makes it.
And am a Christian! o that so I were
as I am named; and still desire to b^;
That I might say I am; and so appear:
since but to seem good, is too bad with thee.
For thou great GOOD, that call'st thy self I AM,
do love I am; not was, nor yet will be:
Then, let me say I am (in deed, and name)
thy Servant, that but lives to honor thee.
For, since I have such Being, let me be
such as I AM, not as I am; that is,
Such as Thou art, most perfect Piety:
for, thou art, wast, and ever wilt-be this.
Besides, thou have and do preserve me still
from all misse-fortunes, and from so daine Death:
Which, in this World (that dangers ouer-fill)
is more then Fortune can to Man bequeath.
How many have I seen the Wars to wear!
and might have seen ha^g'd, drowned, starved, burnt, and torn!
How many poisoned! spill themselves, with fear,
with Pox, Plagues, Pestilence how many worn!
The thousands blind, deaf, dumb, lame, leperous;
besides the Millions otherwise distressed
In Mind and Body, with griefs dolorous,
make me to see how much my State is blessed.
For, that which fell to any one of these
might me befall, be'ing evil as they be;
And, that I have more soundness, joy, and ease,
it is (to win my love) thy love to me.
If any mortal King should for one crime,
many condemn; and save but one or two:
And, I, of those condemnd, should be the prime,
yet first of those two saved, should be too:
How would my Heart be ravished with his Love?
and how would all my Powers strive him to serve?
Then, no less Grace thy grace does make me prove;
nay, more, much more, thou do my love deserve.
For, double thou deseru'st, in treble kind;
thou sau'dst my Soul and body, doomed to Death;
And from all frantic passions keep'st my Mind:
therefore I owe thee Mind, Soul, Body, Breath.
For, it is thy Grace, we be not all consumed;
but, most of all my self, that most does sin:
Since on that Grace I have, to sin, presumed;
yet still, by grace, seek'st me, from sin, to win.
A Body thou have given me, that does lack,
all that thou give me to continue life:
And, lest, through want thereof, It should to wrack,
with me those gifts are no less rich, then rife.
All things thou made for me; and me, for Thee;
for me Ground, Grain; Trees, Fruit; Mines, Metal bear:
Air, Foul; Seas, Fish; and Fish and Foul, for me,
produce most glorious Pearl, and Plumes to wear!
For me, Seas, Ships; Ships, Sails; Sails, Winds endure,
to bring me Benefis from foreign Lands:
For me, Floods, flow; Wels, spring; Springs, Water pure
do yield; that I should yield to thy commands.
Sheep, Oxen, Kine, Goats, Buckes, and other Beasts
yield Flesh, Fleece, Fels, Milk, Oil, and Horns for me:
For me, the Hound does cry, the Spaniell quests,
to teach me how to cry, with hope, to Thee.
The Horns of Unicorns (that precious be)
are mine, though they do wear them for my sake:
Plants Virtue have, not for themselves, but me:
so, things of every suite me Prime do make!
What would I more? there is naught has being got
on, or in Earth, in Water, or in Air,
That either feeds, or heales, or sports me not:
so that this World does naught but me repair.
If I the Elementall World transcend,
to view the Heavenly Orbs; what Wonders There
Sun, Moon, and Stars, I see, who all attend
but for my good, for which they framed were.
For me, alone, they influence impart
to these inferior Bodies, serving mine;
For me, does Time himself in pieces part,
that I, beyond Time, might be wholly thine.
Nay, let me pass the nine-fold Orbs of Heaven,
and to thy sacred Mansion let me flee;
For whom had all thine Angels essence given,
But for thy service, and to wait on me?
To back me, and defend me from my Foes;
to hold me up, when ere I did decline:
To comfort me in Soule-afflicting Woes;
and, to thy presence bring my Soul in fine.
Now if the Ends, for which Things formed were,
be better then the Things (for, so they be)
Then, better than the Angels Men appear;
since they (it seems) for men were made by Thee.
And, Men, and Angels fell through only Pride;
but, for dear Man's Redemption thou didd'st die:
Yet, for no one of the Angels have thou di'd;
which much augments man's hope, and dignity!
O then what Heart can once but thought-conceiue
in what strict Terms I stand obleig'd to thee;
Since me thou made most Glory to receive
through me; as, through the Eye, Men glory see.
Wake, wake thy self, my Soul; why sleep'st thou still?
see who it is that has thus done: for whom?
Not for the Angels, which obey his Will;
but, for thee, sinful Soul, his choicest Home!
Cast, if thou can, a Number numberless;
and, count his gifts with Stars, or with Sea-sand:
The bottom gage, of his Grace bottomless;
Or, if thou can not, wonder-mazed stand!
Yet, stand thou with, and for Him, while thou art;
that is, as long as he himself exists:
That is, while GOD has but an humane Heart;
which is, but while Eternity consists.
As God is GOD, he has no Heart at all;
but, as true Man he is, he has Man's Heart:
Then, GOD, and MAN can never asunder fall;
though Men from GOD themselves too often part.
But GOD, that have Man's Heart (and, so, have mine;
since I am Man, although a sinful one:)
Still let thy Heart be mine, and mine be thine:
that I may have no Heart to grieve our own.
I greatly do desire, with great desire*
to praise and love thee GOD (Man's hearts repose!)
But Praise and Love, in Mouth, and Heart of mire
(through foulenes of that filth) their grace do lose.
But, since all Creaetures thou have made for me,
(for, whatsoever is made, I owe the same!)
I Will call on them, with me, to call on THEE,
to give me grace to love and praise thy Name.
Then, o ye all his Works, your voices rear
(with man his masterpiece) that He would grant
To me his Grace, to sound his praises clear:
and to supply, in Love, my louings want.
To make my Mouth pure, fit to hold his praise;
and make my Heart clean, meet to lodge his love:
That Heart and mouth may so his glory raise,
while I his Grace, in grace or glory prove:
That I in Grace, and Glory may be known,
To live but for that praise and love alone.
A Meditation gratulatory for our redemption.
WHen I excogitate the great Good-turnes
thou have done for me, o extremest Good!
With heat of Zeal, my seathing Marrow burns;
and, flames of seruent Love do boil my blood!
Especially, for that when thou had formed
my Soul and body, I deforming each,
Thou, with thine own dierewrack, have me reformed
and, with thy precious blood becam'st my Leach.
Thou might, for ever, have banished me thy sight,
with the proud Angel, and his cursed Crew:
For, my fault was like his; but, more unright:
then, to the same a greater Plague was due!
And, that thou have not only spared my Pain;
but, therewithal, bought endless bliss for me:
(So that my Fall does fall out to my gain!)
I am in straightest bonds oblieg'd to Thee.
And, for thou made me, me to thee I owe;
since thou redeemed me, much more owe I thee:
And, would, o would, I could my self bestovv
to pay that More, that is less then due from me.
And, so much more thou ought to be beloved,
by how much greater were thy griefs, and state;
And how much less then ought to be reprou'd,
whose life was more then most immaculate!
Who, What, and Wherefore, do thou suffer, Lord?
and, who art Thou, that suffer'st for man's sake?
O tell me; for, I will diuulge thy Word
that all things made, men marred to re-marke.
First, for thy self, with what rich terms of Art
shall I express Thee, inexpressable!
I Will say, as thou said, Thou art, what Thou art;
because, Thou know, Thou were ineffable!
Thou art a BEING more then infinite;
and, being of thy self, proceed'st of none:
Without thee, can no being chance to light;
for, Chance, and being light by Thee alone.
Thy matchless power, of nothing, all things made;
thy Goodness saues all without other aid:
And, if thou would, to nothing They should fade;
for, in, for, and by thee, they all are staid.
Thou only art that art! and, nothing is
besides thee, in comparison of thee!
The Lamps of Heaven their light before thee miss,
whose brightness bright'st Eyes are blind to see!
All beautie's Foulness; Power, infirmity;
Wisdom, Gross Folly; Goodness, worse then naught;
Weighed with thy more then All sufficiency:
more fair, strong, wise, and good, then can be thought.
More then most fair, since self- Formositie;
and, more then powerful, since Omnipotent!
Much more then Wisdom, since her Souls right eye;
exceeding Goodness, since her Continent!
Yea, good thou art, both to the good and bad;
for good and bad suck sweetness still from thee:
With good gifts, good Souls, thou do ouer-lade;
and good'st the bad, to make them better be.
Without distraction, thou do all in All;
Thou All contain'st, yet art in every place:
And yet, art all alike in great and small,
yet here then there much greater by thy Grace.
Thou ever work'st; yet, ever art at rest,
resting in endless doing thy good Will:
Thou all vphold'st; and yet, art not suppressed:
thou art Good alone; and yet, thou suffer'st ill.
Thou Cause of Causes art; yet caused art
to punish sin; yet, did for sinners die:
Thou art impassible; yet sufferd'st smart:
lower then Hell; yet, more then Heaven hie.
What shall I say of thy dread Majesty?
Thou Earth behold'st, and It does trembling stand!
Touch but the Mountains, and they smoke thereby,
then Seas and Winds do rest at thy command.
The Sun (with gloomy Clouds enueloped)
does hide his head, when thou (his head) do frown:
The Moon and Stars, with Cloud-cloakes covered,
in their confusion (shamed) do, then, lie down.
Thou spread'st the Heavens, and marchest on the deep,
whilst her deep Base yields dreadful harmony:
Thou make the Spheres both Time and Tune to keep,
maugre their Discords, and variety.
Thou call'st the Stars by name, who come at call,
and like true Sentinols keep well their watch*
Hiperion, that guides the Capital,
(to thee subordinate) does key their Catch.
Thou anglest for the huge Leuiathan;
and through his Nostrils, make thy Hook appear:
Which being hanged thou playest with him than,
as with a Fish, that hangs but by an Hair.
Hell quakes when thou do volly forth thy voice,
which Bandies Earth as twere a Racket- Batt:
The Heavens shall melt and pass away with noise,
when thou thy Creatures to account shall call.
Upon the Necks of Monarchs thou do tread;
and pau'st the Pauement with their Diadems.
The dreadful Powers of thy Power stand in dread;
and Glory it self, is blinded by thy Beams.
The Seraphins (though glitt'ring-glorious Spirits)
in thy bright presence seem but Butterflies:
Thou rid'st upon the Cherubins, whose sights
thy Beauty blinds, with rates that thence arise.
To thee the Gates of Death lie open wide,
which, on their Hinges, play as thou do will:
Nay, Death himself does quake, when thou do chide
as if it would his Soul, immortal, kill.
The Heavens declare thy glory; Fire, thy brightness;
the Air, thy subtiletie; the Sea thy Dread:
The flowers of the Field, thy Beauties brightness:
thus, all in All, thy praise abroad do spread.
Such, and so great! such, and so great (quoth I)
nay, Lord much more then such, or so, Thou art:
For Words defective are; so, needs must lie;
but, thou (Lord) art deficient in no part.
And now, let me recount the wretched wrongs
which so great Majesty has borne for me;
And, whiles I count, let Men and Angels Tongues
sound endless Peales of Praises unto Thee!
Who, being so sublime, in dignity,
did from the height of Majesty descend
Into this vale of deepest misery;
and, clothed thee with my flesh, the same to mend.
Wherein thou suffer'dst, for my sinful sake,
Hunger and Thirst, in famishing excess:
With Plagues and Persecutions; which did make
to seem accursed thy true blessedness.
The Passions of the Air, thou did abide,
as Prologus to thy PASSIONS Tragedy;
For, Heat, and cold thy Body damnifide,
(as needs they must) that had no where to lie.
Whose Poverty was such, that Birds and Beasts
were much more rich, that Neasts, and Holes enjoyed
But thou (dear Lord) had neither Holes nor neasts,
nor ought besides, wherein thy Head to hide.
Borne in a Stable; Cradel'd in a Cratch;
begging the breath of beasts to keep thee warm;
Wrapped in Rags, that coursest Clouts did patch;
which did thy tender flesh less warm then harm.
O sight of force to wonder-rap all Eyes!
Ye Angels all admire this Novelty*
For lo, your Lord, in base Rags wrapped lies,
to show the riches of Humility!
And, eight days after, took'st a Sinners Mark,
that came, indeed, to abrogate the same:
Soon after, wast constrained to use the Dark
to hide thy Flight, that fledd'st to hide thy Fame.
Therefore thou sought'st the silence of the Night
to be the Triton of thy Lowliness:
Yet, now the World began thy Fame to spite,
and in the rise did seek It to suppress.
Herod, thy Hunter, like a Bloud-hound fell,
did hunt for Thee, that He on Thee might pray:
For, what thou were, he, by and by, did smell;
and, hunted after Thee a likely way.
But, to a Nation, most Idolatrous,
thou wast constraind, from his p^rsuite, to fly:
So, Innocence Life preserved thus:
for which, dear Innocents were forced to dye.
Then, Innocence, Innocence slew:
how then could It therein be innocent?
For, both are innocent; yet both is true:
the first in deed; the other, in event.
They lost their blood for Him; He, his for Them:
so, both did bleed; and for each other bled:
And, both, as Innocents, their bloods did stream;
He, as their Head; They, Members of that Head.
O! had I been so blessed, ere Sin I knew,
to have di'd for thee, among those Innocents:
Or, that I could my sins, to death, pursue;
or, make them live like banished malcontents.
Then would I dye for thee, an Innocent,
if cursed Herodian hands would bless me so:
O let me try this dear Experiment,
(although it cost my Heart-bloud) er^ I go.
For when, before my Minds Eye, thou do come
in all thy Passions, my desire does melt
My very Marrow, to taste Martyrdom;
and Sense feels pain till it such pains has felt!
It may be, that I do but, now, desire
to do that; then, I may desire to fly;
For, he that was thy bodies hardiest Squire
so thought, and said; but did It, then, deny!
Flesh, is a Traitor, worse then he that sold thee;
it will, for Meede, or Dread, the Soul betray:
Nor, in fire, is it willing to behold thee;
in fiery trials then, it shrinckes away.
Therefore, when it a Champion, of such might,
betrayed to fear, I dare not say, I will;
(No, that is Presumption) but I wish I might:
for, willing well, without thee, we do ill.
Then, be with me, strong Power, and I will say
I will; and will perform, that will, in Deed:
For, where thou art, by Power, it is but a play
in greatest torments (then) to burn, or bleed.
Now, as thy Body grew, so grew thy griefs;
for, who (dear Lord) can possibly express
Thy Persecutions, void of all relief,
save Praying, Fasting, Watching, Weariness!
They spoke against thee, who sat in the Gate;
and common Drunkards ballads made of thee:
That thou might say (in worse then David's state)
being poor, I labor from mine Infancy.
These were the griefs (dear Love) thy life did brook,
but, in thy Death what Sense ere understood
What pains thou felt'st; when (like a rising brook)
thy body, more and more, o'erflow'd with blood
Freedom, made Captive; Mercy, Misery;
Grace, quite disgraced; beauty, vilifide;
Innocence, strooken; Justice, doomed to dye;
Glory, quite shamed; and, Life, crucifide!
O Heavens! what can amaze, with Wonderment,
the Sense of Man more then this? what shall I
Call this so strange unheard of Loves extent,
that ouer-fils all Names Capacity!
In few: now Grace, alone, seems Sin, alone;
Life, dies; State's, whipped; and, Power bound to a Poast
The Glory of the Father spet upon!
and, in a word, God, seems to be lost!
In this Deep, further, may my Soul not wade,
my strength is spent; for, my heart bleedes in me:
O glorious Grace! O Majesty unmade!
is this for me, O boundless Charity!
If I, for my Redemption, am so tide
to love, and honor thee; What shall I be
For that thou did so many Deaths abide,
(when one would serve) to make me more than free?
With what love shall I quite this, more then, Love?
with what life shall I imitate thy life?
With what tears shall I my repose reprove?
and, with what Peace shall I conclude my strife?
I owe thee more for my redeeming (Lord)
(since in the same thou Death of deaths did prove)
Then for my Making; (which was with a Word!)
for, more, much more, thy Passion showde thy love!
For, if for Cherubins, or Seraphins
thou had thus di'd, t'had been less meruellous:
But, thou have di'd for me (a Sincke of sins!)
which, of all Wonders, is most wondrous!
What are we, Lord? or what our Fathers House,
(we Sons of wretched Men) that Gods dear Senne
Does in such love and mercy visit us,
as, through Death, to re-make us quite undone,
If in the balance of thy Sanctuary
thou weigh our body, it will be found more light
Than Vanity; more grave then Misery*
as if It did consist in Natures spite!
And, if our Conversation thou respect,
what is it but a Chaos of Offense!
The Goodness of whose All, is all Defect!
whose very Sou^'s but Hell of Conscience!
Do thou, o God, then for such Devils die,
(the Sons of Satan most opposed to thee!)
For the Subuerters of all Honesty!
for breakers of good Laws that blessed be?
For thy Contemners, for thy Gloryes Clouds!
for thy Deprauers, for the worst of Ils!
For mere cu^st Thwarts, of all Beatitudes!
for thy Tormentors that thy Soul would kill!
Whose Hearts, no gifts, can once allure to love,
much less, with Menaces, are terrifide!
Nor moved with heavy Plagues, that Rocks would move;
nor yet, with sweet'st Indulgence, mollifide!
For Fiends, who not sufficed with their own vice,
the Earth do compass; so, to compass more!
And, not contented others to entice
diue to the Devils to augment their store!
Where, robbing those Egyptians of their wealth,
to weet, Pride, Envy, Malice, blasphemy,)
Away they steal (so, all they do by stealth)
to make them Idols for their Fantasy.
Who, when they have rak'^ Hell for every Evil,
and, got as much as Hell can hold, or yield,
They then devise themselves (worse then the Devil)
new kinds of sins, that Hell yet never held:
Adding thereto obduracie of Heart;
and, do, their Conscience, more then cauterize:
Pleasing themselves (like Fiends) in others smart;
and, for that end, do many means devise:
Are these (dear Lord) the things for which thou
the things (I say) for, (no Name is so ill
As they deserve;) What, only must the Highest die?
dye for vile Vipers, that their Maker kill?
My Heart does fail, my Spirit is extinct,
when thus I weigh thy Mercies with my Sin:
And were not for thy graces mere instinct,
I should despair (dear Lord) and dye therein.
Yet, since I have begun to speak to thee,
O be not angry if I yet do speak:
Let Dust, and Ashes once so saucie be
to ask their God what He, hereby, does seek?
Seek'st thou the love of such mere Lumps of Hate?
or else the service of such Vermin vile?
Alas (great Lord) it stands not with thy State,
since where they come, by nature, they defile.
If thy desire of Marriage did so burn,
that Thou thy Creatures would needs espouse,
Why then did Seraphins not serve thy turn,
that are more Noble, and thee better use?
Why of a prepuce Nation took'st a Wife,
which afterward did Thee betray and kill?
So, marriedst, as it were, the very Knife,
that cut thy throat; so, seem'dst thy self to spill.
What answer'st (Lord) to these too high Demands?
I would have this, because I would have this:
This is thine Answer; and, the reason stands
upon thy Will, which cannot will amiss.
Then be it (Lord) according to thy Will;
for, so it mu^^ be, be it how so ere:
By life, or death then, let me It fulfill;
that do by both, thee, so, to me endeere.
For, since Man's fall, none pass to Paradise
but by the dreadful burning Cherubins;
To Canaan none, but by where Marah lies;
since there the inheritance of joy begins.
And none unto the happy City goes,
that goes not by the Babel-Riuers side:
And, none Jerusalem or sees, or knows,
that through the vale of Tears nor go* ^orride.
The way to Heaven, is by the Gates of Hell;
and Wormwood-wine, though bitter, wholesome is:
Thy Cross (o Christ) does Heavens strong (...) compel
to open wide; for, it is the Key of bliss!
And, since for me, so well thou loud'st that Cross,
Let me, for thee, count all things else but loss.
A Thanksgiving for our Vocation.
WIth all the power and virtues of my Soul
I do adore thee holy Lord of All!
That when I had no name in thy check-rowle,
thou wrat'st it on thy Palm, and me did call.
I dwelt, sometimes, in black Obliuions Land,
where, in the shade of Death, I sadly sat;
But, thou (kind Lord) did reach me, then, thy hand,
which, from thence, drew me to a glorious state.
When as I wandered, in the crooked ways,
that, too directly, led to endless pain,
Thou did thy forces, then, against me raise,
to put me in thy way, perforce again.
When thou had plunged me in the Font of Grace,
so clens'd the filth I was conceived in,
Though there I vowed to keep me in that case,
I brake my vow and me re-suncke in sin.
So that sweet Temple which thou sanctifi'dst
in me, for thee, I, cursedly, did bless:
Raising therein, that which thou least abid'st,
namely, the Idol of Voluptuousness.
Then, lived I as an Outlaw; when, it seemed
by Law, or Fiend, or Foe might me surprise:
But, I, of thee yet, then, was so esteemed,
that thou, by Law, did quit me, in this wise:
The Law required Death, or Obedience;
then, thou, for me, did more then Law required:
Which di'dst for sin, yet liu'dst in innocence:
so thou, thereby, did more then It desired!
Yet, ere I once did think upon thy Grace
I lived as loose, as if I had been bound
To nothing but to Persons, Time and Place
that sought my Soul and body to confound.
So, past my Days that rather looked like Night;
nay, rather like the Dark that may be felt;
Wherein my self never came within my sight,
although I might mine unsweet life have smelt.
Then, like blind Baiard, being bold as blind,
I ran, as Fancy led me, every where,
To do the Deeds of darkness, in their kind,
and, with me, others blinded so, did bear.
Then, what was it the Devil could devise
to clog a Soul with Sin, exceeding Sin,
But I to do it was as quick, as wise?
the rather, since my Soul did joy therein.
Then, carnal beauty was the only Sun
that warmed me at the heart; and lent me light:
A Light, and Heat by which were quite undone
mine Eyes, and Heart; nay, Body, Soul, and Spright.
For, all confounded were, as they had bin
no more themselves, but beauties shadows vain;
Attending her in whatsoever Sin,
as Toys, that had bin stitched to her Train.
Then, were my Feet as swift as swiftest Roes
Man's blood to shed; and, so thy Form deface:
My friends to wrong, and treble wrong my foes;
to shun the good, and bad men to embrace.
Then, those things only, pleased best my taste,
that were distastiue to thy sacred Sense:
And, that time (only) I esteemed waste,
that to thy Service had most reference.
Thy Name, to my uncircumcised Ear,
was harsh, and filled the same with all offense:
Which I did deadly hate, through servile fear;
but, served thy Foes with treble diligence.
The World, the Flesh, and thy Competitor
(that for my Soul with Thee do aye contend)
Made me their Slave, and seruil'dst Seruitor;
so, gave my Mind, thy Kindome, to the Fiend.
Thy Word to me seeem'd most ridiculous,
as full of Crackes, as Contradiction:
And, no less witless, then most barberous;
so, made I it a Ground to play upon.
The fairest Church (then) seemed the fowlest Iaile;
a Preacher, like an Headsman, killed me quite:
Words, least divine, with me did most prevail;
and Peace of Conscience still in me did fight.
In brief, I was, for which my self I hate,
such, as on whom VICE showed what she could do
When she did light but on a low estate:
for, what Deeds she devised, my Hand was to.
In this time of my young, yet doting, Age,
thou did expect me (Lord) and lent'st me breath:
Yea, did attend me, like that Princes Page,
that always put his Lord in mind of Death.
O altitude of Grace surmounting Grace!
o magnitude of Mercy most extreme!
How many settings-out, in such a Race,
have been o'er-taken with thy Furies Stream?
Yet, I, most blessed-cursed-blessed I
have (by the Mercy, more then most divine)
Been suffered to be tired with vanity,
and, yet preserved, till brought to Grace in fine.
Had Justice hands, which, then, still urged were,
drawn me before her High Tribunal Throne,
And, by a Quest of Angels, tried me there,
I had been cast, and more then overthrown.
But, blessed be thine unconquered Patience,
that me forbore, till I to sin forbear:
And, blessed be thy Mercies prepotence,
by which, I warded was, and bid beware.
Forcing into my Soul the fear of Hell,
the sight of Sin, Life's vain and short expense,
With thy Laws strictness; all which still impell
my Heart, though Steel, to melt in penitence!
Yea, when my feet were fast in Follies Stocks,
thou did by Grace (past Grace) extort from me
Whole Floods of Tears, from two most flintie Rocks,
(my Heart, and Eyes) for, so, offending thee.
And, when I fled from thee, as if it had
been matter of small moment Thee to flee,
Thou follow'dst me (I being worse then mad)
to keep me from the Furies following me.
Thus, long we straue, and, striving long, at length
thou did prevail, and tamed my Coltish Will;
Yet it was by holy Fraud, and mighty Strength,
which clawed me while they did restrain me still.
For, no less was thy Mercies skill herein,
then thy Powers force: for, sinful Souls to cure:
Shows skillful Grace: and, Men that most do sin
to justify, bewraies almighty Power.
And, o how many Graces give thou me
with this mere guilt of my Vocation!
Firm Faith, sure Hope, and perfect Charity,
with all the Virtues that attend thereon.
And though I cannot be assured Lord,
to serve thee to the end, and meek withal:
Yet, do my Faith, and Hope rest on thy Word;
which sure does stand, though often (unsure) I fall.
Thy Sp'rit likewise, does witness to my Sp'rit,
that thou do love me more than tenderly:
Since in thy Love, thou make my Love delight;
which love erst lothed thy Love mortally.
Blessed be thou, therefore, great Lord of Grace,
for giving me thy dear adopting Spirit
To nurse, and teach, and rule me in my Race,
and, thee and me, vnioynde, to re-vnite.
And, blessed be that euer-blessed DAY,
wherein that Ghest did make my Soul his Inn:
And be that Hour, and Moment blessed aye,
wherein my Will gave way to let him in.
That Day was the true Sabbath of my rest;
that Day I left the Egyptian servitude:
That was my second Birthday, truly blessed,
who, then, was borne to all Beatitude.
It was mine Easter Day, wherein I rose
from Death of Sin, unto the Life of Grace!
It was the Day my Heavenly Husband chose
to marry me; and, Coort me face, to face.
Let Job and Jeremy ban their Birthday,
this will I bless with Heart Mind, Mouth, and Pen;
Since, then, the Angels, in their best array,
saluted me, as their Co-cittizen.
Wherein God called me Son, and Christ dear Spouse;
the Holy Ghost his Temple; and when all
The Holy TRINITY did trim the House
of my poor Soul, that teady was to fall.
Dear Lord! with what dear Words, or dearer Deeds
no, dearest Words and Deeds are all too weak
To match thy Mercies; but my Soul must needs
quite break, if not into thy Praises break.
I Will sing to thee as David once did sing,
O Lord, how glorious are thy Works of Grace!
And as the Angels Peales of Praises ring,
so, will I praise thee though my voice be base.
The work of my Creation showed great Love;
and that of my Redemption, more expressed:
Yet that of my Vocation most did move;
but, that, that Iustifide me past the rest!
The gift of Glory (still to Saints assigned)
is great, so great, that none may greater be;
Yet to be justified, is, in his kind,
as great a gift, and no less laudeth thee.
To make Men just that are in sinful case,
is more then to make just Men glorious:
Since greater ods there is twixt Sin and Grace,
then is twixt Grace, and Glory; God and Us.
My Making and Redemption had but made
m'excuse the less, and my Damnation more;
Except my Soul thou had made justly glad,
in justifying me that sinned sore!
Whiles, therefore, on these things I meditate,
my Soul entranced lies; as if she were
No more my Senses; or my bodies Mate,
but, were transformed to Admiration here.
What shall I render Lord? o how shall I
remunerate, (o that can never be done:)
Or how shall I but praise thee worthily?
but, such praise does my power no less out-runne.
O that the Organs of my Soul were such,
as, with thy praise, they Heaven and Earth might fill;
I would therein rejoice much more then much,
but, Lord, accept the freedom of my Will.
For, could it make thee more then what thou art,
(though more cannot be wished, much less conceived)
I would perform a right Well-willers part,
and make thee what it could, for Grace received:
Then, let my Will be aided by thy Might,
That Will, in Deed, may praise thy Name aright.
Of Gods unutterable Being, with desire of the Soul to be swallowed up with
the love of his Majesty.
O Past-beginning, and immortal Sp'rit;
eternal, and incomprehensible:
Incircumscrib'd in Majesty and Might;
seen all in All, yet most insensible:
Immutable, impassible, most just;
inscrutable; in mercy, most complete:
From whom they came, and unto whom they must
that do believe thou art as good, as great:
Who by thy ne'er-too-much applauded Word
have framed whatsoever created is;
One blessed TRINITY, in true accord
of perfect Unity, and boundless bliss!
If that great Patriarcke, Father of the Just,
(who albeit thou deign'dst to call thy Friend)
Yet in respect He Ashes was, and Dust,
did fear to speak to Thee, that Ear did lend:
Nay, if the highest Orders of those Sprights,
that, in thy presence, burn, through love of thee,
Dare not, upon thine Essence, fix their sights,
lest they, through glory, should confounded be:
If these so pure, so dear, so holy Ones,
so fearful are to speak, or look on thee,
Who albeit they sit themselves on Thrones,
yet, in thy sight, through love, so awful be:
How shall a Shorelesse Sea of Misery,
a Sincke of Sin, a Sack of filthiest dung,
(All which, ah woe therefore, dear Lord, am I!)
once dare, to thee, to stir or Eye, or Tongue?
But, since (sweet Lord) I can no way obtain
that awful reu'rence, which is due to thee,
Unless mine Eyes still fixed on thee remain,
and made amazed with thy Majesty:
Vouchsafe me leave (dread God) vouchsafe me leave
to lift mine Eyes unto thy Throne of Grace;
O let thy brightness, mine Eyes, splendour give;
and blind them not that long to see thy Face.
I see (dread Lord) thou, only, thou art He
that do transcend our understandings reach;
And yet, by understanding, well I see
they see thee best, to whom thy beams do stretch.
Then, o most bright, fair, wise, kind, liberal;
most stable, simple, subtle, gracious;
Secret, yet known; vnscene, yet seeing All:
unmoved, yet moving; in rest, making us:
Whom Latitudes dilate, nor Bounds restrain;
Variety does change, nor Passions move:
Rest makes not idle, nor Work puts to pain,
who art not hurt by Hate, nor holp by Love.
From whom, Oblivion, nothing can detract;
to whom, Remembrance, can as little add:
Who art Dilated most, yet most Compact:
not grieved in Sorrow, nor in Solace glad:
To whom there is nothing past, much less to come;
since Time and Place still present be with thee:
Of all this All thou art the total Sum;
beyond which nothing is, much less can be!
For, thou art in all things, yet art not included;
but yet, in all things, art thou, by sufficing:
Thou art without all, yet art not excluded;
but, without all things, thou art, by comprising.
Thou Art under All; yet subject unto none;
but under All, that All might rest on thee:
And far above All, yet not proud thereon;
but, All, above; that All might governed be.
Perfect in All, in none deficient;
Great without bounds, and Good without compare:
Present in each Place, yet in no Place pent;
yet, whole in All, and parts, in All that are.
In Power, and Wisdom, most-most infinite!
in Counsel, wonderful; in Judgment, just;
Secret, in thoughts; in word, and Promise, right:
glorious in Deeds, which glorify our Dust!
Past all extent, thy Love does far extend;
whose Mercie's more, then most indefinite:
Thy Patience more, than Power can comprehend:
because it is no less then is thy Might!
What shall I say, great-good, good-great-great Lord!
I fear, in these my Words, I do offend:
To seem to circumscribe thee in a Word;
that art without all measure, mean, or end!
Thou art, (o sacred Spirits Angelical,
(that have fruition of Him face to face)
Lend me a Name by which I Him may call;
and may express some measure of his Grace!
Thou art too great, for GREATNESS, never so GREAT!
and far too good, for GOODNESS, ever so GOOD!
Who (were it possible) art more complete
in GOODNESS, then thine own Trin-vnionhood*
Yet thou (thou nameles Good! who, though thus great)
do bid us seek thee, for, who seeks finds:
Who, though not to be seen upon thy Seat,
yet sitt'st thou, seen, in Eyes of humble Minds.
Thou, thou art He, whom, to forsake, is death;
and, for whom life to leave, is life alone:
In whom, to breathe, is to breathe blessed Breath;
and, for whom to contend, is Union.
No man fosakes thee, but the forlorn Fool:
and, no one seeks thee, but whom thou do seek:
Nor none can find thee, but whom thou do school;
and, thou school'st none, but whom thy Lessons like.
What should I say of thee? or how shall I
thy Goodness praise? how shall I celebrate
The glory of thy, back- parts, Majesty,
though never so much thou it extenuate?
I Will say as those, whom thou taugh'st what to say,
thou measurest the Waters with thine Hand;
Upon thy Palm thou do the Heavens weigh:
and, on thy Finger all the Earth does stand!
Thou art that Ancient, ancienter then Days,
whose Throne is like a bright ay-burning Flame;
The Wheels whereof, like Fire that Sparks does raise;
upon whose Thigh is writ a glorious Name:
Thy Body, like a beaming Chrisolite;
thy Face, like Lightning; thine Eyes, Lamps of Fire!
Thine Arms, and Feet, like Brass, all burnisht bright;
thy Voice like Thunder, but It sounds high'r.
A fiery Stream, still flows from thy Throne;
a thousand thousand minister to thee:
Ten thousand thousand wait on thee alone;
and, Millions, by the Thousands, ready be!
Who, with a Beck, nay less, but with a Thought,
rul'st Heaven, and Earth, according to thy Will;
Which, tho most glorious both, thou made of noght
and, if thou would, a thought again would spill!
Help, Lord, for I am in a groundless DEEP,
or endless Maze, that has no coming out!
My Wits from drowning, and distraction keep;
and, let me go no more, this Gulf, about.
For; he that goes about to tell, with Words,
what one thou art, does even as if he would
Quite drown the Sea, within the shallow'st Foords;
nay, more, since thou art much more manifold.
Who ere assisted thee? or thee advised?
who brought thee up in Lore? or gave thee Wit?
And who thy Power, at any time surprised?
or, what Foe was not dashed to Dust by It?
With whom the Sea seems scarce a Water-drop;
the Islands, and maine Lands, a little Dust:
The highest Heaven is but thy Foot-stooles Top;
and, but into a Prick, thou Earth do thrust!
The Woods of Libanus cannot suffice
to make Religious Altars fume to Thee:
Nor all the Beasts can serve for Sacrifice
that on a thousand thousand Mountains be!
All Nations, nay, all Creatures whatsoever
(be they Celestial, or Terrestrial)
Stand in thy sight, as if they Nothing were;
and, in respect of thee, are not at all.
For if, in the bright presence of the Sun,
the Stars seem not to be, although they are;
Then, in thy sight, must All to nothing run;
since, in the same, the Sun cannot appear.
Therefore, with all created Essences,
o holy, blessed, and glorious TRINITY,
I do adore, with all obseruances,
the Scepter of thy dread Divinity!
Thy Being's universal; most exact!
then, being such, what should my homage be?
And, being Grace, and Goodness, most abstract,
how can I, wanting both, serenize thee?
Agnizing, then, the Wonders of thy Worth,
(prostrate, before thy sacred Mercies seat,
With whatsoever Loves fear can utter forth)
I more then celebrate thy glory great.
With those thrice blessed Spirits, who laying down
their Crowns of Glory, at thy sacred Feet,
Prostrate adore thee; lo, I veil the Crown
of all my Glory, to thee, blessed SWEET!
My Glory, Lord? alas! what do I give?
if I have any, it is more than vain:
Then may thou not that Gift of me receive;
since it must needs thy sacred Glory stain.
Return It then (dear Lord) my gift put back;
and, I will give thee what thou gave to me:
That Is Love, and Fear; thou do no Glory lack;
yet, if thou give it me, I Will give it thee.
But not to us (dear Lord) o not to us,
but to thy gracious NAME all glory give;
Which was, and is, and shall be glorious,
as long as God is God, or LIFE does live.
But, unto us, Confusion onely's due;
for, Flesh and Blood has naught to glory in,
But (that which may decayed shame renew,)
a bleeding Conscience, and a world of Sin!
But why does Sol give Light, the Fire give Heat?
why's Water cold? Earth, thick? or Air so thin?
The reason's clear; by kind, they do that feate:
so, thou, by kind, (kind Lord) forgiuest Sin.
Where Sin abounds, there Grace does oe'er-abound;
for, it is thy glory (God) Sins to forgive:
For should thou Sinners, with the Sin, confound,
then, none should live; so, none should glory give.
For, in the nether Hell they praise thee not,
since it is the Hold of hedious Blasphemy:
There is the Land where all things are forgot
save sad Despair, and Death which cannot dye.
But, in thine euer-blessed Hierarchy,
the holy, holy, holy Lord of Hoasts
(In Soule-inchanting Heavenly Harmony)
is ever heard through those Celestial Coasts.
Then, make me such that, in the Life of grace,
I temp'ra^^ly may glorify thy Name:
And, in the Life of glory, face to face,
I may, for ever, much more do the same.
Consume the cloudy Fancies of my Mind
with sacred flames of thine eternal Love;
That, being by that purging fire refinde,
thou may if, on thy Trials Touch, approve.
Then, let thy Glories zeal quite eat me up,
that all my Being may consist therein:
So, I Will carowse thy bitter Passions Cup;
since to my Health (kind Lord) thou did begin.
O let my life (poor life) naught else appear
but a sweet smelling Sacrifice to thee:
Or rather let it be an Offering here,
that, with thy Loves fire, may consumed be.
Then, metamorphose me into thy Love,
let me be quite transmuted to the same:
That I may ever upwards, flaming, move,
as does, by Nature, a material flame.
O! bottomless Abyss of Charity,
engulph me in thy Bowels, let thy Womb
Receive Sins seed, that longs for sanctity;
Then, let it still lie, buried, in this Tomb:
That so, I may, quite dead to Sin, and Shame,
Still live in LOVE, to love and praise thy NAME.
A Repetition of Gods many Benefits, and Preseruations, with desire of the
continuance thereof.
Upon the bended knees of my poor Sp'rit,
(made poor by thy rich Mercies showered thereon)
Thy Virtue I adore, with all my might,
o divine VERTVE, Isreal's Holy ONE!
Since thou, of thy vnlimitable grace,
have deigned to make me know thy grace and power.
Nay, showed to me the splendour of thy face,
which does my love, much more then much allure.
For giving me my Being, being naught
before; and since, and since, for mending me:
Though yet (dear Lord) I am not as I ought;
yet, as I am, I am most bound to thee.
For that thou do conserve me in such case;
that, as a lively Member, I do feel
Thy lively Members dolor, or disgrace;
and sinck in Soul, when they (near falling) reele.
For that thou do my Natures essence keep
from running to the wrack of grace and nature:
And, of a Wolf, for making me a Sheep;
and, of a Castaway, a costly Creature.
A costly Creature I right well may say;
since it thy Heart-bloud cost to make me such;
Even, then, when I was worse then cast away:
for, I was damned before I knew so much.
By thee I am, in sinful state, preserved
from sinful state; which stands where Sin does reign.
By thee I am from prides contrect conseru'd;
that feign would take out of thy hand thy reign.
If I do fall, it is when Thou stai'st me not;
if I do rise, it is by thy helping Hand:
But, I never fall but when I have forgot
that by thy Rod, and Staff, I rise, and stand.
As many good thoughts as my heart ere held,
he held, in Capite, of Thee his Head:
If well my little-World I ere did weild,
I did it with thy Power, but in thy stead.
If none can say Lord Jesus but by Thee,
much less, without thee, thee can any serve;
No more then Boughs yield Fruit, cut from the tree:
then what we work for (Lord) thou do deserve.
Therefore, if I have fasted, watched, or praid,
if I have Crosses borne, with Back unbroke,
If I have shed Contritions Tears, unstaid,
or crossed my Will, or up thy Cross have took:
If in my prayers I have thought on Thee,
or that they have prevailed, or I relieved;
Or if my Will to Virtue has been free;
all these, as gifts, from Thee I have received.
How many virtuous Friends, and Ghostly Guides,
how many good Examples, Lights of life,
What like Books have thou sent me, these besides,
as Loue-signes whilst thou woo'dst me for thy Wife?
All which, to thee, I freely do refer,
that they, from thee, more freely may reflow:
For, since thou did all these on me confer
ere I was thine; now all Thine I do owe.
But, to recount the Perils I have past
(thou being present, pointing out the way)
Is more then well Arithmetic can cast,
which do thy mercies, numberless, bewray!
And, into what, more than prodigious, Crimes
I might have fallen, had thou not me vpheld,
(Yea, had thou not prevented me be times)
appears by those that have in sin excelled:
For, never did the Devil sin so much,
but that a frail Man may commit as great
If, with thy grace, his Heart thou do not touch,
and, with thy Power, the power of Sin defeat.
As many Means, then, as I had to sin,
and, fit Occasions as I had to fall;
So many might my Sins, and Falls have bin,
if I by thee had had no help at all.
Occasions, are so violent, in Power
that they could Giant-foyling David fall:
For, they once took him up into his Tower,
from whence he fell, in part; and after, all.
Occasion is a Ianus, which does look
to Vice and Virtue most indifferently;
But, by Sins watching him, he often is took,
and made the mean of much Impiety.
He Is feigned to stand upon an upright Bowl,
to show his course is most indifferent:
Except he meets with rubs, which make him roll
out of the way, which way those Rubs are bent.
But, o! how often have thou, with strong hand,
^ane him out of my way, lest I should fall,
By reason he, in my way, right, did stand:
for often I stumble at Occasion small.
How often have Thou the FOES hand manacled,
because he should not fight, or if he should
I should be Victor, and not vanquished;
and so get Grace, since (tried) I held my Hold?
How often have I marched upon my Foes,
(the Adder, Viper, and the Cocatrice)
And never was once stung, or hurt by those,
(thou had so charmed the malice of their vice!
How often have thou walked hand, in hand, with me,
through Fire and Water, all untouched of either;
Nay, so their Powers were tempered by thee,
that they wrought for my comfort altogether!
And, how often have they quite consumed bin
who (for not worshipping their senseless Sawes)
Have, in Afflictions Furnace, kept me in,
the whiles I sang, for joy, in Sorrows Jaws!
That with the Psalmist, I may justly say
I was sore thrust at, that I (so) might fall;
But, thou o'er-threw'st my thrusters, with my stay;
as Bals quite break, thrown hard against a wall.
How often have my Sins sold me to thy wrath?
how often have I fallen, yet never fell!
For, in thy Mercy, which no measure has,
(though I did passing ill) I have done well!
Though I gave, that, to Nature, due to Grace,
and, to my self, that, which belonged to Thee;
(Who might have graced Thee (then) with my disgrace
yet gave thou grace, not shame, even then to me!
And, though the Angels fell for less offense,
and, that proud Babel-Monarch, for the same,
Yet I, as one of more pre-eminence,
escaped vnskarr'd, in Body, Goods and Name!
I Perils loved, yet perished not therein;
by many by-ways I found out the right:
With Grace familiar was I made by Sin:
so, foild I Sin by Sin, and not by fight.
Was ever Love so won? was ever Man
so wooed by GOD! or can my Soul conceive
How much thy Mercies may, that so much can!
or can I thee of Love (dear Love) deceive!
O rather of my Being quite bereave me,
and, turn me to my pristrin^ Nihil-hood,
Then I should, so, of but mere Love deceive thee;
that make mine Evils do me so much good.
Burn me (kind LOVE) to Nothing, that of naught
thou mai'st, in Love, make something, good for thee:
Of naught, thou made me good; mine Ill, I wrought:
then, now, of naught, good make me ever be.
Perfect, great Master Builder of mine All,
thy Work begun; and let my Nothing be
Apt to be-come, in Form, material;
and, be conformed to matter loved of Thee.
Breathe on this CHAOS (Lord) and let thy Spirit
walk on the Waters of my Humors, vain:
My Darkness, palpable, covert to Light;
so, my Confusion shall, in Order, reign.
Over Sin, and Death, and darke-darke Ignorance,
in datelesse Terms of all Eternities;
The value of thy Virtue to advance,
which, of thy self, above all Heights does rise!
This is my Hope, which is, because thou ART
Just in thy Word, and Deed; in All, and Part!
For Power and Grace to withstand the Flesh, the World, and the Devil.
WHen I look up and see the Heavenly Spheres
roll on my Time, and my Life's Line therein,
Thus say I to my Soul; Vain Soul thy years
are nothing, since thou nothing do but sin?
Yet art thou made eternal (like thy Sire)
and all conceiu'st (like him) beneath the Sun;
Thou Art in my Whole and Parts (like him) entire;
and have (like him) right Reason Ill to shun!
And yet, o yet, thou do, but Ill withal;
so all that good thou turn'st to Evil still:
Who, through Infirmity, to Sin do fall,
when thou stand'st highest in grace of Powers goodwill.
Then Lord of Power and Grace, with both so arm
my falling Soul, that she thereby may stand
Against the Assaults of all that would her harm:
for, she can foil but with thy Forces Band.
Great Lord of Hosts, Jehovah, God of Power,
then leave me not alone among my Foes;
But strengthen me from thy Strengths trusty Tower,
that I, by Thee, may ever vanquish those.
The Flesh the World, the Devil, with their Bands
of strong as fraudfull Foes, me still assail;
While all my Power lies only in thy Hands,
which when I hold, I foil; withheld, I fail!
If then thou would I still should Victor be,
give me those helps that in thy hand still lie:
Flesh fights not with it self; but thou in me
can foil it quite, and make it Mercy cry.
The World does love it self (and, so, her Own)
too well t'endamage either; and does charge
Too strongly on me to be overthrown
by my small power, if thou it not enlarge.
And Satan, in himself, is not divided,
though, in himself, still turbulent he be:
He is too crafty, strong, and well-prouided,
for me t'encounter, if thou help not me.
Then o estsoones (again, and still again)
I thine assistance humbly do inuoke;
That so I may still vanquish, so, still reign
over these our Foes that ever us provoke.
So will I yield the glory unto Thee,
That foilst such strength by Weakness; that is by me!
The Sinner, confessing his sin, strives with God (by importunity of
Prayer) for Grace.
GReat God! from who^ no thought can be concea'ld
since it thou know ere thought; and searched have
All Hearts ere they, in Nature, are revealed,
forgive my thoughts, that give thee but distaste.
To my Confusion needs I must confess,
my thoughts and Sin are One; and, so, most base:
And though so base they be, yet n'erthelesse
often Grace they mind; so, Sin presumes on Grace.
Lord! how am I deprau'd by Sin, that can
scarce think a thought, but I do sin therein?
Then blessed Lord, how can thou chose but banne
so vile a Slave, so subject unto Sin?
I must not leave Thee thus; no, though my Heart
be well-neere Flint, I must not leave thee so:
With thee, for Grace, I Will wrestle ere we part,
then let me find it in mine Ouer-throw.
And if such Sin thou do forgive by Grace,
and that where much is pardoned, Love is much,
My Love shall ouer-fill all Time and Place;
such is my Sin, my Love shall then be such!
Deny me not (dear Lord) for I will take
no nay of thee; no; thou do me inuit,
Being heavy laden, to thee;, o then make
me free therefrom, lest it do quell me quite.
And learn me (Lord) to woo thee for thy Grace;
and win it, by my wooing, to relieve me;
Thou can soon lighten this my heavy case;
then, thy Will's good, with good will then, forgive me.
Make my Heart feel, although the while it ache,
some Sign of Grace, that thereby I may know
Thou love such wooers as no nay will take;
and Wrastlers such, as will not let thee go!
Though speak I cannot as I would, my Spright
still woos thy grace with sighs, then words more deep:
Thou know her speech, and do therein delight;
then o let thy kind Answer make me weep.
Thy Louing-kindnesse has the power to strike
her dumb with joy; and after make her shrill
In thine applause: for, whom thou (Lord) do like,
thou still make drunk with joy through thy goodwill!
Then if I have found Favor in thy sight,
or else wilt give me any hope of Grace,
Make druncke my Soul with thy sweet loues-delight,
and let her so (ioy-rauisht) thee embrace.
I sue to thee, for that I needs must have;
I cannot be without It, since within
It Is all mine All: then, It I still will crave,
until by ceasely begging it I win:
Then, grant me grace from Sin me still to free,
Else, by thy grace, I Will cry for it still to thee.
The Sinner confessing he can neither will, think, nor do any good thing
without Gods preventing and assisting Grace, importunes the same.
O Thou that from the Bottom of Not-being
did raise me to BE thus, a MAN, like Thee;
And, ere I WAS, through thy divine Foreseeing,
did more then see what would become of me;
Give, give me leave (thou God of endless Grace)
to enterplead with Thee without thine ire:
Why AM I, if thou turn'st from me thy Face,
since so a Brand I am but for Hellfire?
I could not choose but Be when thou would have me;
for how could nothing cross Almightinesse?
And now I Am am lost, unless thou save me;
but, none thou sau'st that still thy Will transgress.
Nor, can I do thy Will, without thou will;
and if thou will, thy Will no Power can cross,
Much less my Weakness; then, if I be spilt,
it seems thy Will, although my blame and loss.
And yet thou sai'st, thou will no Sinners Death;
thy Word is Truth it self; then, if thou would
That I should live, o let me spend my Breath
as those whom thou, by Grace, from sin with-hold'st.
So in thy Will (which no Power ever impugnes)
consists mine everlasting Weal or Woe:
Then, not to me, so much as thee belongs
to save me from eternal overthrow.
I can but Will; but, well I cannot Will,
if thou first will it not; nor ever shall:
Then, will it first; for, I can will but Ill
without thy Grace; so, Grace does all in All.
I cannot think, then much less can I do
ought pleasing thee, without thy Grace first got:
And yet to do it, still thou me do woo;
which, yet, I cannot, if thou do it not:
For, look what good I do, it is not I
but thou that do it in and by me still:
Then still I can do all things in and by
thee ( Lord of Power) agreeing with thy Will.
O then incline my Will thy Will to do;
and give me Power, with Will, else Will will fail:
Will thou but this, then me thou need'st not woo,
because thy Will with mine must needs pr^uaile!
So, shall there be but one Will twixt us two:
Grant this (dear Lord) for this I thee do woo.
The Sinner because of the darkness of his understanding, confesses his
inabilitie to come to the knowledge of Gods will by his Word; and desires to
be holp and enlightened by Gods spirit therein.
THou have commanded ( Lord) in every Want,
that Man, thy Creature, still should call on thee;
And thou his just desires have said to grant,
then now (o now) thy Promise keep with me:
For now, even from the Bottom of the Deep,
I cry to thee that art all Heights above;
I, crying, call; or rather, calling, weep
for what I want; that is, thy Grace, and Love.
Then, as thou art still soothfast, grant them me
that by them still I may thy Heasts perform;
Then if thou would I should obedient be,
let Love and Grace my will to thine conform.
Lo, I entend, and by thy holy Grace
will still contend, thy holy Will to do;
Then through the luster of thy brightest Face,
show it, that I may know, and do it too.
Give that which thou have given me Power to crave,
and Promise to obtain; thy guiding Spirit:
Thou still do tender that which I would have;
yet cannot take it, if I lack thy light.
A Chaos (Lord) of Darkness still I am,
without th'inlightning Spirit still move thereon;
Then let thy Spirit with light so clear the same,
that it may be an Heaven for thee alone.
Vnseele mine Eyes, that long thy Light to see;
for, they are blinded with black Ignorance:
Then, Light of Lights, to Heaven direct thou me
the rightest way with thy bright Countenance.
Men are of various minds about this Way;
some this, some say that way the way does lie:
And to it Scripture ( Truths right Rule) do lay;
but Truth never lay in such diversity.
For, Truth is one, but these are manifold;
then lead me in this way, else stray I shall:
Incline my Will this rightest way to hold
(how ever strait) and in it never fall.
O trade me in thy Paths, I beg of thee
with all the forces of my mind and mouth;
And when I step awry, straight show it me
by inspiration of thy Spirit of Truth.
If in thy Word I look for help herein
from all Presumption keep my private Spright:
For, many Doctors so deceived have been;
then make my Soul still see, and take the right.
Thy Word's a Lantern to direct their steps,
that are as humble, as intelligent:
Yet often the Wise thy meaning ouer-leapes,
while it is revealed to the innocent.
Thou spak'st therein to all Capacities,
and lispst to Babes, to make them know thy mind:
Yet if thou guide them not, and open their eyes,
the Wonders of thy Law they cannot find.
Thy Will then ( shown and hidden in thy Word)
is hid, though shown, from those not prompt by thee:
Though Camels there may swim, and Gnats may ford,
yet both may drown, if (there) too bold they be.
In shallow'st places, there, great Clerks have suncke
into the depth of Heresy, and drew
Whole Nations after them; yea, made Kings drunk
therewith, while they Beleeuers-right pursue.
So then, as none could ever see the Sun
but by the Sun; so, none can rightly see
Thee in thy Word, but by reflection
of that pure Light of Lights that comes from thee!
If so, then light me in that Light (thy Word)
since thou art Light of lights; else may mine Eyes
Be daz'led, and (so) drown me in each Ford
of those pure Rivers of thy Paradise.
Thy Word is Truth; but those it does misguide
that know not well thy Language, nor will know;
Since they will learn but of them- selves and Pride;
so, not thy Word, but they are erring so.
None can be saved without they do thy Will,
which none can do, unless the same they know:
And none can know it, much less it fulfill,
if it, by special grace, thou do not show.
Then, if thou will that I shall saved be,
(for thou will no man's Death that seeks thy face)
Let me be taught to know thy Will by thee;
and made to do it by thy Power and Grace:
So shall I find what I am seeking still,
To know Thee well, and well to do thy Will.
An Invocation against use of offending, or bad Custom.
Dear Lord! while I bethink me of the Ils
that me surround; and weigh the Woes I feel
Through mine own fault (which me with Sorrow (fills)
from Life to Death I ready am to reele.
The Sun of my Care-clouded life has past
his full Meridian; and, does now decline
To Seas of griefs, where Age does sincke at last;
and, at each breath, Death seeks it to define.
Use of offending, in my passed Days,
does pass my strength to change, though feign I would
Custom (to Nature turned) my Nature swayes;
and of my self, the while, I have no hold.
Yet, if, I dye ere so bad use I leave,
my life must leave me hopeless at my death;
For, what I give to GOD I shall receive;
and, as I spend, so shall I yield my Breath.
I mind to mend; but still procrastinate;
for, my Familiar, Sin, is loath to part;
And does my halfe-dead body animate
to use her still; so, wounds, and heales my Heart.
But since I am not sure to breath once more,
and that my life and death are well-neere met,
And Death t'eternall Weal or woe's the Door,
why sin I now, my life's Sun near is set?
What is in Sin, that it should so be witch?
A bitter-sweete (if Sweet it be) and makes
The Body glad, but still the Soul to grutch;
and even from life the vitall-vertue takes.
The wisest yet, that ever breathed this Air
of Humane Race, well tried it to be so,
Whose equal Wealth and Wisdom did repair
to all in Nature, but this Sweet to know.
And yet he found the Sow'r excelled the Sweet:
the Sweet but short, the Sow'r surmounting Time:
We want his Means, his high Delights to meet;
yet hazard we our souls to them to climb!
Lord, make me wise by his experience,
who, in great wealth and Wisdom, played the Fool:
And for mere Folly was at huge expense;
then, let his follies me still wisely school.
Yea, let me learn of Him that all does teach;
of whom the wisest learn Sins snares to shun;
He was a King, and Preacher; and did preach
that All is vanity beneath the Sun.
If all be vain beneath, and true he says,
let me above the Sun seek true delight;
Which I shall find by walking in thy Ways*
so thou (dear Lord) consort me with thy Spright.
O then consort me so, and with his power
enable me all lets to overrun;
Let me not stay one Minute of an How'r
to joy in any thing beneath the Sun.
But in thy Sun of Justice let me joy,
which fills the Heavens and Earth with purest light:
Then, let all other joys my soul annoy,
that so in him I may alone delight:
Thou can do this; then doubt I not thy Will,
Which still is good, then my good will fulfill.
The Sinner refers his Will to Gods will in all things* desiring help for
perseverance therein.
Dear Lord, and God, true Lover of my Soul,
in my desires, I wholly do resign
unto thy blessed Will this Will of mine
To form, reform, direct, and still control.
And as my Soul my body moves alone
without whose motion it would still be still:
so let thy Sp'rit still move my soul and will.
Else, let them have no motion of their own.
Let me forsake my self for thy dear sake;
yea, truly hate my self for love of thee,
and let no pleasures please or profit me,
If thou (dear Lord) at them displeasure take.
I offer unto thee mine All, and more
(had I much more than All) to mortify
my senses and affections; that thereby
I may (so mortifide) live evermore.
My self I (likewise) offer to the lack
of sensible devotion, grace, and love,
so it may humble me, and make me prove
Thy might the more in my sins utter wrack.
I offer (too) my self, with prompt desire,
t'indure all loss, in name, fame, goods, and friends,
all pleasure, pain, and what else flesh offends,
That by their weight, my sp'rit may mount the higher.
In sum; I offer up my self above
my self, to all mischance that can befall
save sin alone; yet, if thy goodness shall
Put me in Hell, I Will brook it for thy Love.
And though it be impossible for Flesh
to suffer it; yet, should my Will be pressed,
If thou would have it so, in Hell to rest;
For Love in quenchlesse flames can sense refresh.
Then love me (Lord) and still my love inflame;
then put me where thou will, I Will there abide
without repining, ire, or ghostly pride,
With Martyrs, that, in torments, laud thy Name.
But since by reason of my Flesh (too frail)
I cannot be so prompt these pains to brook;
then, help me (Lord) but with a loving look,
And over Death and Hell I shall prevail.
Look kindly on me then (dear Lord) and so
Our Wills shall still be one in weal and woe.
The Sinner desires fruition of the Deity; and that his Soul should be ever
the habitation thereof.
Eternal LORD, who art more prompt to hear
then Faith to pray; of that great grace of thine
Regard the Boon I ask in Love and Fear;
and to mine humble suite thine ears incline.
Grant me fruition of thy DEITY
that all my Soul may so be satisfied;
For less then that can her not satisfy,
though all else (boundless) were still amplifide!
Those gifts and graces that thy Grace may move
t'inhabit my poor Soul, vouchsafe thou me:
That with thy gifts thy grace may be in Love;
and love my Soul for harbring them, and thee.
But, in those gifts O let her be reposed
none outherwise then as they fashion her
To harbor Thee; (that is, make her well disposed)
else let her rest be restless ever there.
My Son (says thou, dear Lord) give me thy heart,
o small request! my Heart, Lord! what is it
But one poor bit of wormes-meate? can no Part
of me delight thee, but so vile a bit?
Why, thou did wholly give thy self to me:
shall I return thee then but that alone?
O it is (sweet Savior) most unworthy Thee;
for which (thou know) it is, meekly, wo-begon*
Yet gladly would I give it; but, it is
so small, unclean, unquiet, and accursed,
That I do fear to give it so amiss,
since, of all gifts, it is worser than the worst.
Yet take it ( Lord of Love) it is thine own,
how ever I have abused it; make it such
As thou would have it; let it still be known
fit for thy Stamp, upon thy Trials Touch.
O glorious King, what grace is it to our Hearts
to be accepted, and desired of thee?
Then take my Heart, yea, all mine other parts;
for they are safe in thee, but lost in me.
And is this all thy gain (o kindest Lord?)
and is this all our gift, one wretched Heart?
And for the same do thou thy self afford?
then take it to thee (Lord) through joy, or smart.
For, nothing can I give thee, but the same
augments my gain and glory endlesly:
Then take it wholly, set me all on flame
to melt me into thee by Charity!
For, were my Heart as great as is the Heaven
that all includes; and, that past price it were,
It should to thee (desiring it) be given,
since, I have thee for it, who have no Peer.
Then, World be silent, call it not again;
Flesh be as still, permit it still to go;
And Devil strive not; for, it is in vain;
my God will have it, then it shall be so.
Vade, vade; for, all you cannot fill my Heart,
my God alone can do it; and He must
Have it to fill: then from me all depart,
that seek to fill it but with wind or dust.
And sole Sufficer, chain it still to Thee
with Adamantine Linckes of endless Love;
That through those Straites which thou have past for me,
it may be drawn to thee, if slow it move.
Let it attend thee to the Iudgement-Hall,
where thou wast doomed to death; and to the Hill,
Whereon thou suffer'dst; let it taste thy Gall;
and; on thy Cross let it be fixed still:
That be'ing with thee thus plagued, disgraced and slain,
It may with thee be raised, and crowned, and reign.
A sovereign Salve against Sin and Despair out of S. Augustine.
Dear Lord, when sinful thoughts do me assail
to thy dear Wounds then let me hye with speed;
When burning lust against my thoughts prevail,
quench it, by minding me how long they bleed!
In all Extremes I find no Mean so good
as thy wide Wounds to keep my Soul still whole*
They cannot dye that drowned are in thy blood;
for, that is Aqua vitae to the Soul!
Thy Death is my desert; then do I not
lack merits; since thy Death destroys my Sin:
Thy Mercy is my merit; and, my Lot
is glories Crown, through my firm hope therein:
For, if thy grace be great; then is it clear
my glory shall be great: and, the more power
Thou have to save, the less I ruin fear:
for, Grace abounding, makes Loves hope secure.
Yet I acknowledge mine iniquities;
and, Conscience, with her thousand Witnesses,
Accuse me of extreme impieties;
yet will I hope of mercy ne'erthelesse:
For, where Sin has abounded, there has grace
abounded more; so, love enflaming in
The grieved delinquent: who does enterlace
sweet tears of Joy, with bitter Tears for Sin.
For, who dispaires, God, utterly denies;
deny his Attributes, himself deny:
His Justice we provoke; his mercies rise
but from him- self, who is self- Clemency!
Then, let my thoughts still murmr while they will,
and ask, why such a Sinner grace should seek?
Yet in firm hope I will continue still,
since he has promised that cannot break.
Who can do what he will; and he will do
what he has sworn: which is; he will make whole
The broken Heart for sin, and grace it too;
yea, help contrition in the willing Soul.
My Sins (though great) then, me no whit dismay,
when his dear Death I mind: for, all my Crimes
Can never o'er-match his Mercies, if I pray
for grace, to hope in his sure help betimes.
His Thorny Crown, and Nayl^s, that him transpierc'd
assures my hope that He and I are One:
Which have his Iudgemeuts against my sins reuerst,
if I but grieve for what I have misdone.
Longius has cleared the sad coast to his Heart
with his fell Spear; that (kind to me) made way:
There rest I now in Joy and joyful smart,
of safety sure, while there, in hope, I stay.
Upon the Cross he does his Arms extend,
to embrace the Contrite: then, between those arms,
Devoutly will I throw me till mine end;
so, safe I shall be there, from foes, and harms.
He bowed his Head, before Death brake his Heart,
to kiss his Lovers with the kiss of Peace;
Then, still I Will kiss him: so, shall I depart
in peace to him that is my Sins release.
Sweet Christ embrace me then, and kiss me till
I dye to live, to clip and kiss thee still.
The crazed Soul being almost in despair, desires Grace to hope in Gods
mercy.
LOrd, in thy Love, let me be none of them
that love but in a Calm; a time believe;
But when a Storm arises, do blaspheme;
and with infernal S'prits, thy Sp'rit do grieve.
Thus what I need, I crave; but what I fear
thou know (dear Lord:) I fear I am too bold
To seek thy love, because I do appear
no correspondence with thy love to hold:
For, he that merits hate (Lord) how can he
straight look for love? and who has shame deserved
Seek for immortal glory? or, to be
from shame and pain, which he deserves, preserved?
He moves but his Judge to iustest wrath
that, being faulty, looks he him should clear,
Without meet satisfaction for the scath
which he has done; all these my hopes do fear.
For, he that is to shame and death condemned
small reason has to look for highest respect;
If but his death by grace might be redeemed,
in sense, it should be all he could expect.
But why, o why, do I now call to mind
what I have done, to make my fears more rife?
