thus cruelly persecute him by Gods just judgment. He desires to be restored
to grace. To be governed by his holy Spirit, that he may spend the remnant of
his life in the true fear and service of God.

LOrd hear my prayer with thine all-hearing ears;
and, for thy truths sake, note mine humble suite:
O hear me in thy Righteousness, which hears
All those that mourn, although they still be mute.
And into Judgment enter not (O Lord)
with me, frail man: for I, nor none beside,
(Because of sin which we have all incurred)
in thy clear sight shall then be iustifide.
For, the enemy (the Fiend, our common Foe)
has long pursued my Soul (that flesh misse-led)
My Life in Earth, his Fury has brought low;
and hid the same in darkness, with the dead.
My Spirit (therefore) is vexed, my Mind, and Heart
are greatly troubled; yet, I minded still
Thy days of old (thy Works and thy Desert)
which did my Muse with Joy and Wonder fill.
My hands to thee have still outstretched bin,
my Soul (that thirsts (as earth that water wants)
For drops of grace, to quench her flames of sin)
I lift to thee, the while, for grace she pants.
Then (kindest Lord) with speed attend my cries;
because my fainting spirit has failed me:
Auert not from me thy converting Eyes,
lest I be like to those that burying be.
And in the dawning of the longed-for Day,
(the Day when Justice Son shall Comfort give)
Let me the voice of mercy hear, I pray;
since still I hope that thou will me relieve.
And since so many Heads, so many Ways
are said to lead to thee, by Heads of Sects;
Show me the Way that straight to thee conueyes,
since my poor Soul both thee and it affects.
And from my Foes preserve me (weakling) still:
to thee alone I fly in all distress:
Then teach me to perform thy blessed Will:
for thou art only all my blessedness.
Thy Spirit that cannot err, nor yet deceive,
shall bring me to the Land that Justice bears:
And, for thy Names sake thou shall me receive,
according to thy grace (that never wears.)
From Trouble thou shall bring my Soul to rest;
and, through thy Mercy shall destroy my Foes:
Yea, all annoy that do my Soul molest,
since as thy Servant, I on thee repose.
To God the Father, Son, and Holy Ghost,
three Persons, and one God; all glory be;
As it was, is, and shallbe in each Coast,
thoughout all worlds in all eternity.
FINIS.
RIGHTS OF THE LIVING, AND THE DEAD: Being, A proper Appendix to the precedent
Meditations.

OBIT-RIGHTS.
A Funeral Elegy, on the death of the most virtuous, and no less lovely,
Mirs. Elizabeth Dutton; eldest Daughter of the Worthy, and generally beloved
Sir Thomas Egerton Knight, eldest Son to the right Honorable, Thomas, Lord
Elesmere, Lord Chancellor of England: which Elizabeth was, at the age of eleven
years, married to John Dutton, of the age of fifteen years, Son and Heir
of Thomas Dutton, of Dutton, in the County of Chester Esquier: which John,
deceased about the age of seauenteene years, and left the said Elizabeth a
Virgin-Widow: who so lived till she died the first of October, at the age of
six teene years and a half, in Anno 1611.

A Virgin, Wife, and Widow, three that One
Held rarely perfect in like Union,
Incites my Muse; nay, more, does her co^strain
To empt my Pen of Praise, of Wit my Brain
In her deserved honor: she whose all
Was naught but good; yet so, as we may call
That good but naught (and justly) if the same
Give not her goodness glory more than fame!
A Maid, in whom Virginity gave place
(Though most exact) to Modesty and Grace.
A Wife (who like old Joseph's blessed Bride)
Though wedded, but vnbedded till she did,
Yet from her came, on her by Grace begot,
Faith, Fear and Duty, in a True-loue knot,
Till his decease, to whom these three she bare:
And after, for him, nursed them still with care.
She lived a Widow; but it was hard to know
Whether she lived or did when she was so:
Since when she lost her Pheare, she lost her Breath;
For, Turtle-like, she mourned and droupt to death.
But while it was losing she such Patience wanne
(By his Death mortifide) as she began
(Before her end) her Heaven on Earth thereby,
In hope to live with Him when Life should die.
So, in her Patience, she her Soul possessed
Her God; in whom her Soul, with his, did rest;
Yet rested so, that still (unseen) she moved
to both devoutly, whom so much she loved!
Poets can shape of things that grace forsakes
Far rarer things than grace or nature makes.
But let all Poets all their Art unite
To fable praise, the moral is her right.
Nature profusely had on her bestowed
(Borrowing of grace) more grace then ever she owed!
And grace (as envying Natures Gifts, so rare)
Unlocked the Heavens where all her Treasures are,
And showered them down so on this dearest Maid,
As she for worth, an Angel should have waide.
Wit for her worth can never hiperbolize;
Much less a Poet in it Poetize;
Since what or Wit or Poetry can praise
(With their best Art) was found in her: then raise
Her up my Muse, ere she be raised, at last;
And her enthrone in glory high as fast:
That when the Virgin, whom all Virgins bless.,
Shall, for her graces, see her gloriousness
In Heaven and Earth, she may (as worthy her)
Enbozome her, or fix her in a Star,
Whose Name and Fame while mortal Virgins live,
To them, with hers, may Light and Virtue give!
For this, her Soul still labored to be gone,
T'returne her Errand of Creation:
As fiery Matter working in a Cloud
Breaks through, for want of Matter it to shrowde:
So Souls, with stirring much, are said to fire
The best Complexions; and (so) home retire.
But, Sickness (ah too sweet-lipt) sucked her Blood,
That she had none to fire in likelihood:
And so her vitall-flame, vnnourished;
Her Soul through coldness, left her body dead.
A short life made her Virgin, Widow, Wife,
But, well she l^u'd, which is the Well of life.
This old World was unworthy such a Iemme,
Therefore she shines in new Jerusalem!
I best can witness how her time she spent,
Who taught her hand to show her hearts intent:
Then may I best renown (for known desert)
The Pupil of my Hand that had my Heart.
Thou had my Heart (dear Pupil) since in thee
Was all that might entirely master me.
And did my Power but equal half my Will,
Laura should be thy Foil: for, I (by skill)
Would set thee so above her, that thy light
(With poynant Beams) should thrust through Earth and Night:
For, when Formositie and Virtue strive
In one sole Subject for Prerogative,
That Subjects praise must reign (all Terms above)
In height of Glory, Memory, and Love!
The Grand-sire of thy Flesh in Earth's renowned;
And thy spirits Grand-sire King of Heaven is crowned:
Thou living, then, as coming from such Sires,
Our Songs must answer the Celestial Quires,
That chant the praise of Virtue in their King,
In whom thou art, then we on earth must sing
Thy praise in his, since his all praise contains:
So thine in his. eternal glory gains!
To thee then sing I, as I sing of Thee,
Who art sole Base of this high Harmony:
For, knowing Tombs have ends as well as wastes,
And that strong Rhyme their ruin far out-lasts,
My Muse shall labor on this ground of Fame,
To raise a Pile of Rhyme, whereon thy Name
Shall ever shine, through Wits Celestial Plashes,
Until another Phoenix of the Ashes
Produced be; that when it eft shall burn
In those eternal flames, it eft may turn
To pristine plight; and by such alteration,
Live Phoenix-like (still bright) in admiration!3
We wail their want whose Lives our wants supplide,
Not weighing how they lived, but when they did:
For, the best livers here, do living dye;
But after death they live immortally.
Children and Fools are angry still with those
That, to distill, disleaue the fairest Rose;
Not pond'ring how the sweetness in the Juice
Is so increast, and longer lasts for use:
So, we that see this Rose (whose hue and breath
Celestial were) divided so by Death,
Though it for heavenly purposes be done,
Yet still our thoughts but on the spoil do run.
But o be it far from us, to think thee spoiled
In living blessed, and dying so vnsoil'd:
No: we thy Memory will celebrate;
Whose weal we wail not, but rejoice thereat.
If in this Paper Monument there be
One Ornament of Art that is worthy thee,
Or any Work of Wit that may retain
Thy Memory; my Labor for my Pain
Is too great Meede; since by the same I show
Times future, what will better them to know.
So, shall I in thy Praise include mine own;
And making thee so known still, still be known:
For, if this Shrine chance to be visited
By any, that regard the worthy dead,
It may be they will think me worthy Love,
That on this Pile did all my cunning prove.
The Egyptians with their Pirameds did strive
(Against the Heavens) to keep such, dead, alive:
And Artemisia with a matchless Tomb
Makes her Mausolus live until the doom:
Though It be now demolished and gone;
Yet is he known by It, as It was known.
And Wit, but with mere Words, has often raised
A Monument of Praise, far longer praised.
Then may this Work, which but weak words erect,
(Upon so sure a Ground) work like effect.
The Name of Egerton she does renown;
And Dutton.
that by which she last of all was known:
Nay, had she had, by Fortune, all the Names
That Wit for Natures vilest Creatures frames,
Sh'had so much Grace consorting still her Blood
As to have made them all as great as good!
The Days of old did lay their Macchabes
Under Worlds-wonders, huge Piramides!
Semiramis, in her bright Polymite:
And Cyrus, in his Obelisk as bright.
In his Columna they Augustus shut:
And in his Mole-magno, Hadrian put.
Alaricus, the Gothe that ruinde Rome,
In his rich Rubico they did entombe.
Those, dead, yet lived by these: and these again,
Live yet by those, though naught of them remain!
But, were I able, I my Saint would shrine
Within the mouths of Angels most divine;
Since they out-last all Worlds, that Time does end,
And have (of creatures) best mouths to commend!
But live (sweet Saint) in mine immortal Rhyme
(Made by thy virtue such) past Tombs and Time:
For, if eternal Virtue cannot dye,
Then thou must live, till She does ruined lie.
Farewell, dear Maid (whose body (like a soul)
Had power t'inflame the Love it did control)
Farewell while we, by thy dear loss fare ill;
That is; while griefs do grow the Heart to fill:
For she that held all Hearts (by her deserts)
To her entire, her Death must break all Hearts.
Ye Ladies (that (alive) do inly love
So much o'er-weening that does mortal prove)
Look not ascue, nor turn the Head aside
(As if you could no Praise but yours abide)
At these just Praises (Relickes of the Dead)
But learn by them to be so honored.
,, Envy does leave the Enui'd at the Grave;
That Fort from Envy should the Virtuous save:
Then (o) exalt these Lauds, unless you will
Be rather pitied, then enuide still.
Poets (I grant) have liberty to give
More height to Grace, then the Superlatiue:
So has a Painter license too, to paint
A Saint-like face, till it the Saint out saint.
But Truth (which now mine Art to shadow strives)
Makes license larger by the grace she gives.
But yet,
To say thou wast the Form, (that is the soul)
Of all this All; I should thee misenroule
In Book of Life; which (on the Earth) they keep
That of Arts fountains have carowsed deep,
Nay, so I should displease and wrong thee both:
For, unjust praise thou can not chose but loathe,
That lothed'st it here; then there, more (past compare)
For, he is the Soul of All by whom they are.
But I may say, (and none the same gainsayes)
Thou art the soul of this thy World of Praise!
Whose soul did animate thy small-world too
To be the soul of all that here I do.
Often have I seen thee, (nay I see thee yet,
Whose face and manners I shall never forget)
When as thine ears had heard, or eyes had seen
Ought that to Virtue had offensive been,
Thy face and breast with that fair blush overflow
Which Modesty (not Bashfulnesse) does owe.
In these bold Times it is held a Trick too fresh
Of vnbred Indians, so to paint the flesh
For any cause: but, this is but the effect
Of Impudence, the Times souls chief affect.
No Parts (i^ laudable) at Court required,
But they attired thee in thy state retired:
Yet thou so modestly did act them still
As that the light'st seemed grave against their will!
What shall I say? in thee was naught so small
That was not greatly praised and loved of All!
This shows thy Mother true unto thy Sire,
Whose worths, in love, set all the World on fire!
Thou, his true Daughter, likewise do the same,
While thou go through Oblivion by the flame!
The Soul a twofold action has; that is,
Original, and Isntrumental; this
By Nature does the like produce; but that
( Mere Intellectual) does not generate.
Though Nature yet, could not so high aspire,
Thou, in thy spirit, wast like thine honored Sire
By special grace of Heaven; for, in your Birth
Such Planets met, as decked and ioyde the Earth.
But o! too soon the earth quite lost that Joy;
And in that loss found infinite Annoy.
Such is the staylesse state of Things below,
That do but vanish while they seem to grow!
Beneath the Moon, all is but like the Moon,
Constant in nothing but in changing soon:
And so will be while they remain beneath;
Resting from changing only but in Death:
As when the Whirle-windes (in their wheeling play)
Pursue their Turns, till, in their Center, they
Return into themselves: so, Nature goes
On in her Course (which first from form arose)
Until this World of form be dispossessed,
And Nature in the Chaos, takes her rest.
That Time runs round, by this dark Riddle's bright;
A Father has twelve sons half black, half white,
And every son has thirty, which still live;
And when their sires decease, they them revive:
So sire and sous still die, but die in vain;
For, still the thirty them revive again,
And yet these thirty every Month do die;
Yet every Month they live immortally!
Thus, by a Year (which evermore does raise
Twelve months (like sons) and each month thirty days)
Time turns over All, and All does ouer-turne,
Till in the later day himself shall burn;
And then Eternity shall take his Room,
In which is nothing past, nor yet to come;
Wherein the subject of my Song still is
A glorious Angel in the height of Bliss!
Atheist, stand far from this her sacred shrine:
For, thou art foe to all that is divine:
Thou do believe where ere her Corpse consumes,
There perishes her soul, which never assumes
The same again: but (o her flesh shall rise
(As does her fame) above both death and skies.
And why shoudlest thou the Resurrection doubt,
When Clouds of Proofs so compass it about?
Some write that Swallows drowned are in the Deep,
In Winter; and, in Summer, rise from sleep,
The Fly in Winter, dies; in Summer, lives;
And, being drowned, warm Ashes her reuiues.
The Vrchin of the Sea, in pieces rent,
Re-water'd, ioynes, and lives incontenent!
Each Grain that rots before the same does spring
Is a true figure of this real thing.
Each Plant, which in the Winter (seeming) dies,
And springs in Summer, shows men, dead, shall rise.
Say a man famisht, into Air were past,
Yet Air shall yield what it receives at last
As well as Earth, and Seas shall yield their dead;
Though on them (vanished) Worms or Fish have ^ed.
At first, they gave, what they did not receive;
Then, what they take, shall they not rather give?
He that, but with a Word, made Man of naught,
Can he not raise him, dead, now he is ought?
If no: his Arm wants wonted power, and length;
Or else wants knowledge to employ his strength.
But in the Almighty none of th^se appear,
That knew our smallest Portions ere they were.
If Nothing were the point from whence did rise
Creation, it may be the Point likewise
Of Resurrection; but it Something is
That shall be raised: the easier then is this.
Say Men eat Men, through some hard exigent,
And them converted have to nutriment,
Yet shall their Excrement (how ere unmeet)
At last yield up their Relickes pure as sweet!
For, at that Day each man shall put on fresh
Flesh of his own, and not another flesh.
And though he fed of others, that shall be
Restored the owner, be it he or she.
And Beasts of prey, that often on Men do feed,
Do die; and of them Flies or Worms do breed.
Those Flies and Worms are often food for Fish,
And they again come often to our dish;
All this may be: and so a Man may go
Through Beasts, Fish, Foul, and Vermin too and fro,
And never rest, though he be dead, till he
From that base Progress lastly raised be.
Yet he that w^ll knew All ere ought was made,
Knows where what ere created is, does vade;
And, can, but with a thought re-gather it;
And make it in the proper figure knit!
For, if it were worthy Gods Creation erst,
To make Man subject to a fall at first;
It Is far more worthy for his powrefull hand
To raise him, dead, eternally to stand:
For, Death is but a sleep: and as a Man
Can wake Men, sleeping: so, the Almighty can,
Raise up the dead so much more easily
As matchless Power does pass Infirmity.
If Heaven be just, and there be Providence;
Then we shall rise when we are fallen from hence:
For, if the good, in this Life, find but Woes,
And no Joy in the next, most cursed are those.
Nay, most bruit Beasts more happy are than they
Who here do most of all Watch, fast and pray.
The Rod of Moses turned to a Snake,
Shows God of one thing, can another make.
And shall he not (so, fail us in our trust)
Restore man to himself, when he is dust?
O Heaueus fore-fend, we should once so conceive;
For, God can neither alter nor deceive!
Our Bodies are his seed; Churchyards and Graves
Are all his Seed-plots, where his Seed he saues
By sowing to corrupt, to rise again
Most incorrupt; and so, by loss, to gain.
In the Cadauer, some have thought some Bon^
Retains the Seed of Resurrection;
Which kept from rotting by the Almighties force,
Should raise, at last, the Worme-consumed Corse!
Some say, that in the Teeth that Seed does lie,
Since Earth, Air, Fire them hardly putrify.
But we believe this Seed (and do not doubt)
Is not within the Body, but without:
For, when the Trumpe shall sound, the dead shall peep
Out of their graves, as newly wakt from sleep.
By that great Power that, there, asleep them laid:
Then in that Power that Seed is solely staid.
But, some do say, our Bodies cannot rise
From Earth to Heaven, for that above the Skies
No nasty Body can remain; because
Such to the Center Nature ever draws.
Fond men! they know not, then our Flesh shall be
Not changed in Substance, but in Quality.
Our Bodies shall be Heavenly; so they shall
Agree with any place Celestial.
Our blessed Saviors Body, once like ours,
(Save that it could not sin) those heavenly Towers;
Do now enwall: then; in that Heaven of bliss,
Why, by his Power, may ours not stay with his?
That Water hangs in the Air who does not know?
And by the Lodestone heavy Iron does so.
If Nature can do this; what can persuade
Gods Power is less then Natures, which he made?
No, no, his Power, that does all power comprise,
Can flesh refine, t'inhabite (so) the Skies!
By which high Power and his diuinest Grace
There rests my Saint, as in her proper place.
Her Soul there rests; and in those heavenly Bowres
Her Body shall, when it shall rise with ours.
Which while (too short a while) it soiourn'd here,
It did celestial to all Eyes appear.
Then, may a little mending make it fit
For Heaven, that was so heavenly out of it!
Thou wast (rare Maid) alive, s' Angelical,
That, dead, thy dust is Metaphisicall.
If some shall muse why I contemplate Thee
Among his Praises that most praisefull be,
Let if suffice them, it was of purpose done,
To praise thee, Star, for light had of this Sun,
Within the Volume that includes his praise
(That naught includes) so his in thine to raise:
As when we laud the light the Sun does give
We praise the Giver in the Gift; and strive
(When most we praise the Taker) to renown
The Giuers praise, for gracing so his own:
So, and none otherwise, I praise the Grace
Appearing in the Soul, Limbs, Eyes, and Face
Of Natures Maister-piece this goodly Maid;
Of whom all good, can never ill be said.
If so much heavenly Grace, and Gifts of Nature
(As Virtue, Beauty, rarest Form and Stature)
Should not be graced by them they good; then I
May say, the bettered are the worse thereby:
For, still th'ungratefull for a Benefit,
(Though bound) are free from Honesty and Wit.
And though the virtuous, for their Virtues sake,
Look not for praise; but strive it to forsake,
(To keep them humble) yet each virtuous Wit
Should honor Virtue for selfe-benefit.
And since Posterity does light receive
To run to Honor by the Lines we leave
From Virtue drawn; we should be drawing still
The Lines that (drawing) lead up Honors Hill.
The Highest Power and Grace, by oath, has vowed
To honor them (among the multitude
Of Men and Angels) that are good; then she
That was so good, of both must honored be!
Celestial Maid, if from the heavenly Sphere,
What Mortals do, thou can or see, or hear,
Be not displeased that my vntutor'd Pen
Should teach thy praise, to teach all Maids and Men
The way to Honor: nor, that in its Mouth
(That often does fable) it should take this Truth.
I was thy Teacher, though (unworthy) I
Might ( old) learn of thee ( young) to live and die.
Yet since it is the Oblation of my zeal,
Which I do offer for the Commonweal
In thy dear Memory; thou will (I hope)
Acquit me from Presumption; since my scope
Was but thy glory, and the Peoples good,
Which in great light, go right in likelihood.
I must confess a Priest of Phoebus, late,
Upon like Text so well did meditate,
That with a sinlesse Envy I do run
In his Souls Progress, till it all be DONE.
But, he has got the start in setting forth
Before me, in the Travel of that WORTH:
And me out-gone in Knowledge every way
Of the Souls Progress to her final stay.
But his sweet Saint did usher mine therein;
(Most blessed in that) so, he must needs begin;
And read upon the rude Anatomy
Of this dead World; that, now, does putrify.
Yet greater Will, to this great Enterprise
(Which in great Matters solely does suffice)
He cannot bring than I: nor, can (much less)
Renown more Worth than is in WORTHINESS!
Such were they both: for, such a worthy PAIR
(Of lovely virtuous Maids, as good as faeire)
Self- Worthiness can scarce produce, since they
Lived like Celestial Spirits, immured in Clay!
And if all-powerfull Love can All perform,
That in it has rare Matter, or like Form,
Then should my Lines have both so'accomplished,
As from the Grave to Heaven should draw the Dead:
Or, with her Taper-pointed-beaming Name,
Nail her to Heaven, and in Heaven clench the same!
Hold Muse, no more: (thou have too large a scope,
To prove thy Pinnions: for, the Heavenly Coape
Infolds no more) and take thy leave anon
Of Her thou never shall leave to muse upon.
Thou may be tired; but never can fly about
The Inside of her praise, much less the out.
Then stouping here, with reverence, grief and love,
Bid her adue; and, with that bidding, move
Thy self to tears; but, if thou can not so,
Show thy self willing by the dryest woe:
For, never had I greater cause of grief;
Since while she lived, I joyed, in painful life:
But now, am left all solitary-sad
To wail her death, whose life made Sorrow glad!
O! had it pleased the Heavens, by their Decree,
To Have made my Pupil learned to have did of me,
(And mine example) I had been at rest,
And she live blessed long, to dye as blessed.
I, like a withered Pine, no fruit produce;
Of whom there is no Care, no hope, no use.
I burden but the Earth, and keep a place
Of one (perhaps) that should have greater grace:
Oppressed with Cares that quite crush out the Sap
That feeds my Life; now thrown off Natures Lap.
I solely sit, and tell the saddest hours,
That ever yet impeached vital powers.
Obscured by Fate, yet made a Mark by fame;
Whereat fools, often, shoot their Bolts, in game.
Yet, live as buried (that I learned of thee
Dear Pupil) while the World goes over me.
Praying for patience still to vnder-ly
The heavy weight of this Worlds injury.
Often have I been enbozomed by Lords;
But all the warmth I found there, was but Words.
And though I scarce did move, yet scarce they would
There let me lie, though there I lay acold;
But, as I had some biting Vermin bin,
Out must I, moved I but for warmth therein.
Or else so lie, as I were better out;
Since there I lay as dead, yet lived in doubt:
In doubt I should have nothing but a place
In the outward Room but of their Idle Grace.
In doubt black mouths should blot me in their Books
That make few Scholars; and in doubt my Hooks
Would hold no longer to hang on (o Grief!)
This hanging's worse then hanging of a Thief!
An Halter loone abridgeth bale and breath;
But hanging on men's sleeves, is double death.
To hang in hope of that which doubt does stay
Is worse then hanging till the later-DAY.
Doubt stays that meede that merit hopes for, often,
Lest Meede should but make Merit look aloft;
Or, quite leave working, since it has no need;
Therefore the great do still withold this Meede:
For, to them selves they say; If we should fill
The well-deseruing-empty (working still)
They would but rest: than, well we will them entreat
Yet keep them hungry still to work for meat.
Fate, but to State this privilege affords;
And but the mean, without means, work for words.
Yet work they must, since Air the great do give:
For, if they have their hate they cannot live.
Their Love does little boot; but o their breath
Blows down, in hate, a poor Relict to death.
These miseries I ran through, and did try
These dear Conclusions but in misery;
Hoping for that which but my hopes deceived;
And me of hope and life, almost, bereaved.
Till I (to stand) from these was feign to fall
To serve two Lords that serve me, now, withal:
The one immortal, the other mortal is:
Who serve my turn for what my life does miss
Which, for it is still amiss, still misseth that
Which makes men gracious, and (so) fortunate:
But he, who knows all, knows (perhaps) it is best
For me to live with little, in unrest:
For, never since I first could move, had I
A better life than those that (living) dye.
I never yet possessed one day of joy
That was not lined or hemmed with some annoy.
The Kingly Preacher in his weal found woe;
But I in thwarts, for those alone I know.
These made me old in youth: for, Sol had run
Scarce thirty years before my days were done;
And to his course ere five more added were,
Black Days (like Nights) in gray had did my Hair.
Yet never Cross on me so sad did sit
As this dear loss; whereof this benefit
To me acrewes, that (now) each pressing woe
Stands far without this, and this keeps them so.
I say I greatly grieve; yet seem to feign:
For, great griefs never greatly could complain:
That is, when Sorrows flood the Banckes does fill,
It noiselesse runs, and smoothly glideth still:
But if the Current once the Brimmes get over,
Twill roughly run; or, stopped, will rage and rore.
But, o, that tyrant Time will silence me
Before my griefs are vtter'd as they be:
Farewell then, my griefs Cause, who wast the effect
Of all the joy my life did well elect:
Farewell, in Him, on whom who sares is well;
And, while I live, I Will be the leading-Bell
That shall thy lowdest Peales of praises ring
Which in the Clouds shall never leave ecchoing!
Or, be the Trumpet of thy Fame to fill
The Aetheriall Lofts with Strains more lofty still!
That when Times wings his Funeral flame consumes
Thy Fame shall soar with fair vnsinged Plumes!
An Epitaph on the death of the right virtuous Lady Liegh; sole Daughter of the
same right Honorable, Lord Elesmere, Lord Chancellor of England: which Lady
deceased the third day of April, Anno Dom. 1612.
HEre dead she lies; who while alive she was,
was Graces Inn; Wits Home, and Virtues Rest:
Whose WORTH was of true Worthiness a Mass:
yet well proportioned for her humble Breast.
A Wise and Mother! as it is hard to say,
whose loss was great'st, her childrens, or her phoares:
To either wisely kind; to each a stay;
that made one, love; the other, love and fear.
To her all-honour'd Sire, she was as dear,
as she was virtuous; which was as the blood
In his Hearts Center; which to him is near;
yet dearer held his flesh in one so good!
Who did (as live she did) in grace and peace,
more laden with good-deeds then idle-dayes:
Leaving her worth (for worthiness increase)
for Wives unborn, to imitate and praise.
Who had at once, two Husbands; yet she lived
of Wisely truth a constant Paragon:
One Husband heavenly was; who has deprived
the Earthly of her, for himself alone.
Yet, yer he had her, bought her with his Blood:
But, with her, bought a World of Womanhood!
Then, maugre Time, and Death these Lines, tho weak,
May lead all Times all good of her to speak!
Here Muse, now close the Paper-tombes of these
Two virtuous Souls, and Bodies; Aunt and Niece.
with this,
A good Name is better then a good Ointment: and the
day of death, then the day that one is borne. Eccles. 7.3.
The Picture of an happy Man.
HOw blessed is he (though ever crossed)
that can all Crosses Blessings make;
That finds himself ere he be lost;
and, lose that found for Virtues sake.
Yea, blessed is he in life and death,
that fears not Death, nor loves this Life;
That sets his Will his Wit beneath;
and has continual peace in strife.
That strives but with fraile-Desire;
desiring nothing that is ill;
That rules his Soul by Reasons Squire;
and works by Wisdoms Compass still.
That naught observes, but what preserues
his mind and body from offense:
That neither Courts nor Seasons serves:
and learnes without experience.
That has a Name as free from blot
as Virtues Brow; or, as his life
Is from the least suspect or spot,
although he lives without a Wife.
That does (in spite of all debate)
possess his Soul in Patience;
And pray, in love for all that hate;
and hate but what does give Offense.
Whose Soul is like a Sea, too still,
that rests, though moved; yet, moved (at least)
With love and hate of good and ill,
to whaft the Mind the more to Rest.
That singly does, and doubles not;
but is the same he seems; and is
Still, simply so, and yet no Sot;
but yet not knowing ought amiss.
That never Sin concealed keeps;
but shows the same to God, or more;
Then ever for it sighs and weeps;
and joys, in Soul, for grieuing so.
That, by himself, does others mete;
and, of himself, still meekely deemes;
That never sat in Scorners Seat;
but, as himself, the worst esteems.
That loves his body for his Soul;
Soul, for his Mind; his Mind for God;
God, for himself; and does control
CONTENT, if It with him be odd.
That to his Soul, his Sense subdues;
his Soul, to Reas'n; and Reas'n to Faith:
That Vice in Virtues shape eschewes;
and both, by Wisdom, rightly waigt'h.
That rests in action, acting naught
but what is good in deed and show;
That seeks but God within his thought,
and thinks but God to love and know.
That all unseen, sees All, (like Him)
and makes good use of what he sees;
That notes the tracts and tricks of Time,
and flees with the one, the other flees.
That lives too low for Enuies looks;
and yet too high for loathed Contempt;
Who makes his Friends Good-men, and Books,
and naught without them does attempt.
That lives as dying; living yet
in death, for life he has in hope:
As far from State, as sin, and debt;
of happy life the means and scope.
That fears no frowns, nor cares for fawnes
of Fortunes fauorits, or foes,
That neither checkes with Kings, nor Pawnes;
and yet still winnes what Checkers lose.
That ever lives a light to All,
(though often obscured) like the Sun:
And though his Fortunes be but small,
yet Fortune does not seek nor shun.
That never looks but grace to find;
"nor seeks for knowledge to be known:
That makes a Kingdom of his Mind,
wherein, with God, he reigns alone.
This Man is great with little state,
Lord of the World Epitomized:
Who, with staid Front, out-faceth Fate;
and, being empty, is sufficed:
Or, is sufficed with little; since (at least)
He makes his Conscience a continual Feast.
This Life is but Death.
Though Fire by warmth cheers life; great heat brings death;
though good Air life detaines; bad, life defines:
Though Water stays our thirst, it stops our breath;
though fruitful Earth does feed; the barren, pines.
Too-much o'er-fils; too-little, feebles life:
Wealth wants not Cares; and Want, wants all but Cares:
Solenesse, brings sadness; Company, but strife;
and sudden Joys do kill, as well as fears.
Mean mirth, is rational; extreme, is mad;
no good so good, but here it is mixed with ill:
Nay, too much goodness is exceeding bad;
yea, bad, if blind it be, is true Good Will:
And, save the Highest, our highest gain is loss;
Then, life's but death where al things are so cross.
True Wealth.
THat Grace that neither wonders, grieves, nor joys
at Fortunes utmost, seeking but to find
What Bounty (still in action) best imployes;
nor wailes the want that beggars not the Mind:
That neither, grieuing, sighs; nor, ioying, sings:
that shines most glorious, in most gloomy days;
Pleased with the state her own endeavor brings;
that droupes not with defame; nor swells with praise:
That scorns Disdain, disdaining naught but vice;
and Greatness rates by Goodness: doing naught
But good for ill; and that for avarice
of goodness only; by her only sought:
That Time and Wealth well spent, does not deplore:
This is that Wealth, without which Wealth is poor.
An Angel-like Man.
HE which (prouokt) endures, as borne to bear;
and looks alike in greatest weal and woe;
That so loves good, that ill he naught does fear;
and ebbes in Mind when Fortunes most do slow.
That bounds Desire with less than he enioyes:
(for, only nothing's less then Nature needs)
That holds all Virtues dear; all else but toys;
and, meekely, scowres Prides rust, from his bright deeds.
That Is better than he seems, yet seems the best:
but, without scandal, seeks to seem the worst:
That, quelled with Crosses, thinks him highly blessed;
and, for the Bliss of all, would dye accursed:
In sum: that would do all that All should do
For love of All: this Man's an Angel too.
A sick Minds Potion for all in Tribulation in Body: or for the saving of
their Soul.
THou that do joy or grieve beneath the weight
of his dear Cross, who did on it for thy sake,
View and reuiew these Lines with more delight
then Patients do the Potions which they take:
How ere they sense displease, they wholesome be;
so wholesome, as they often whole do make:
So may this Potion work the like in thee:
My Muse desires to make it grief expel;
And, all she seeks, is but to take it well.
Yet this I do as often the sick do talk
of Health; not for they have, but would have it;
So, I exhort to Patience, though I balke
her Way; and only wish the same to hit:
Yet, as a sick Physician soon may find
a Potion for another Passion fit:
So may a sick Mind cure a sicker Mind:
No Mind more sick then mine; yet well I know
What Is good for Minds so ill; and, that I show.
The Soul that sins, vnplagu'd, wild quickly grows,
as Trees vnprun'd; and, but sour fruit produce;
The heavenly Planter then, no cost bestows,
but it abandons as unfit for use.
Why weep'st thou then, sad soul? what thou endur'st
a blessing is, no beating for abuse:
Or, if it be, since it thy self procur'st,
Thy patient bearing this thy Sourge (or Cross)
Does make it score less; nay, thy Score does cross
Regard not then thine anguish, in the Rod;
but, in thy Fathers Will what place thou have:
If thou will share the pleasures of thy God,
then, of his Cup thou must, with pleasure, taste.
The Ox assigned for slaughter well is fed,
and lies at ease; while others labor fast,
And still are yokt, tired, pricked, and punished.
"Not all that stroke are friends, nor foes that strike;
"but strokes that maime from either, we mislike.
The Wounds, a friend does give, are sweeter far
than suger'dst Kisses of a fraudfull foe:
The first, often make; the last, more often mar;
for, Surgions Bands do pinch, to solder so:
Who binds the Mad, or wakes the Lethargicke,
how ere he seems, thereby, t'awake their woe;
Yet, he to both's a friendly Empericke.
" Severity is Mercy oftentimes,
"And Mildness cruel, that increases Crimes.
To weane us from this World, an unkind Nurse,
God (only good and wise) annoints her Teates
With Gall of troubles, spights, and what is worse;
and as a Mother well her Child entreates;
But makes her Servants use it ill; that so
finding of all, save her, but blows and threats,
It may to her, the much more willing, go:
So, God permits that All should us molest,
That we may fly to Him, and love him best.
The flatt'rings of the World, the Flesh, the Fiend,
are but the kisses of worst Enemies;
And though the Fiend to Heaven seems to ascend,
like Jove's own Bird that nestles in the Skies;
Yet is he but a greedy Bird that towers
to Heaven, while on the Earth he nearely pryes,
To watch his Prey, whereon forthwith he poures:
For, he but seems in Virtue to excel;
And flies to Heaven, to bear his Prey to Hell.
Out of close-Prison, and much closer Chains
many do travel; but, their Iourneyes end
An endless Kingdom is; whose greatest Pains
are endless Joys; these sincke, but to ascend,
Unlike the Fiend that mounts, to fall more low;
and, ruin that, on which he does descend;
But, low these stoop, to shun an Ouer-throw:
"To bear high Sails in Tempests, is to have
"Our Keele turned up with every Gust and Wave.
If Crosses heavy be; o yet (at least)
they make the Soul as sober as discreet:
If we be fellows of our Lords unrest,
we shall be of his rest and comforts sweet:
He wounds; but h^s are wounds but of a Friend,
that in no fortune once from us will fleet;
And lanceth but ^o cure, and make us mend:
"I^ is a Pain, that is free from all annoy,
"To die with torment still to live in joy.
He that had seen just Joseph in his Chains;
in Sackcloth Mordocheus (his Iyb^t nie)
Susanna going to her mortal Pains,
would have bewailed their infortunitie:
But, had he known that Joseph should be rid
from Gives to Rule; and Mardochs ieopardy
Conclude in Honor, as Sasannaes did,
He would have though them blessed in great mishap,
since so great Comfort was the after-clap.
So much the Fiend shall tempt, when thou do dote*
as shall enforce ^hy mind to mind her m^sse:
If Christ we serve, Affliction is our Coa^e;
his Cross, our Badge, to make us known for his:
His nakedness, how we should cloth us, shows;
his Gall, how we should feed on Agonies:
B'his hanging on the Cross, how to repose:
And by his Death (wherein all pains were ri^e)
How to esteem the pleasures of this life.
Worlds- weale's our woe; and yet we will not see it:
young Toby walked securely in the Mire:
But at the River when he washed his Feet,
a Fish was like to swallow him entire.
He that, on pain of life, must watch the Foe,
wakes best when he is neer'st Afflictions fire;
But, on the Bed of ease he does not so:
In this Worlds hell, if ease be good for ought,
It Is Poesy; yet too much makes it naught.
Besides, the better mind, the worse is tempted:
Pirates to charge the empty Ship, forbear,
But richly laden, and from fear exempted,
they charge it home, and give it cause of fear:
Even so the Fiend, while we are void of Grace,
lets not our Voyage, but our Helm does steare:
But when we take in truest Goods apace,
With Storms of troubles then he seeks to reaue
Us of our freight; and, over Bourd us to heave.
Of Joseph, Benjamin was loved best;
in his Sack, therefore, Joseph's Cup was found:
So does the Cup Christ drank of, ever rest
with those to whom his love does still abound:
On Beds of Roses lies Lasciviousness,
which Virtue hates, since she corrupts the sound;
But Virtue lives, too often; in all distress:
For, she respects not Fortune; nor disdaines
To lie with those, that often lie in Chains.
When God had praised Job, the Fiend straight praid
that he might prove him with Affliction:
And when the heavenly Voice, of Christ, had said;
This is my dear, and wel-beloued Son,
He, in the Desert, was, soon after, tried.
"They find most Lets that most aright do run;
"And they left Rubs that most do run aside:
"But, straight to run, dispight each spightfull Let,
"Does Glory gain, while Shame the rest do get.
When holy David did his People count,
a great Mortality his Coasts did scowre:
But when Augustus did this Sin surmount,
taxing the World (by his usurped Power)
He never was with least Punishment annoid:
So, Jonas, fly'ing, a Whale did him devour,
While Pagan-Passengers a Calm enjoyed:
But, though the Whale did Jonas (so) enjoy,
He swallowed, but to save, and not destroy.
And as a Scarre a Son takes in the Face
in his Sires quarrel, though the Face it marres;
Yet it procures the Fathers love and grace,
and so gets glory by such graceless scarres:
So, God desirous more to have us kind
than comely Children, thrusts us in his Wars,
As we were but to fire and sword assigned:
He takes more pleasure in the great'st annoyes
We have for him, then in our ghostly joys.
Each Danger, for our Mistress, vnder-tane,
seems most secure; and pleasant, deadli'st pain:
The Wounds both for and from her (though but bane)
seem honied-sweet; and loss, for her, is gain:
The colors that she likes; we most do love:
her words, mere Oracles; her spot, no stain;
Her actions, Patterns, ours to shape and prove:
All her perfections past Superlatiues;
And imperfections, least Diminutiues.
And shall we do and think all this, and more,
but for a shade of Beauty; and endure
Nothing for Beauties Substance? nor adore
the CREATOR but in the Creature?
O! it is a shame that Reas'n should be so mad
in men of mind: for love (if it be true)
Will most affect what is rarest to be had.
"The Object of true Love is greatest GOOD;
"If less she loves, it ill is understood.
With our Souls Eye, if Christ, our peace be viewed*
true love shall see a Soule-afflicting sight;
His head with blood (that thorns do broach) imbrude;
his Ears, with Blasphemies; his Eyes, with Spite:
His Mouth, with gall; his Members all, with wounds;
his Heart, with grief; and all in all unright:
Yea, so unright, as Justice quite confounds:
Yet, man's Ingratitude does griue him more
Then all these Plagues, as manifold as sore.
And, o, for whom does he the same endure?
for Man, begot in filth; in darkness, formed;
With throws, brought forth; and brought forth most vnpure,
whose child-hood's but a dream, with pains enorm'd,
His youth, but rage; his manhood, ceaselesse fight;
his Age, mere sickness, all his life unsure:
And, worst of all, his death is full of fright.
This, this is he, for whom Heavens God endures
All shame and pain, that pain and shame procures.
We Are no where safe, where we may fall to sin;
in Heaven, nor Paradise; with men much less:
In Heaven fell Angels; Paradise within,
the first man fell, through whom, all men transgress:
In the World Judas, from his Lord did fall:
so no place can defend from Wretchedness,
But he that place confines, and holds up All:
For who from worse to better falls, he may
From better fall to worse, without his stay.
If Crosses trauerse not our Comforts, then
we ought to cross our selves as many did
That were Men Angel-like; or, Gods with men;
who hardly lived, in Dens and Deserts hid:
Fed little, and slept less; in Sacke-cloth clad;
to mind them that to mourn they here were bid;
So, chose fo^d, place and suite as suites the sad:
To sing in Babylon, being Abraham's Seed,
Is to forget our Bondage and our Creed.
Heaven is our native Home, our Canaan;
Earth's but the shade of Death, or vale of Tears:
Then mirth in place of moaue, but kills a man:
at point of Death he is mad that Music neares:
Therefore those Saints, (discreet, sad, ^ob^r Souls)
rejected all that Sense to Life ende^r^s;
And live (as buried quick in ^aues) lik^ Mo^es.
"I^ Weapons l^s^e do w^und th^n s^arpe^t praise,
"Les^e hu^ts lamenting, then the Sirens ^ayes.
And as Men longing, at Noonday, to see
the Lamps of Heaven, descend into a Well
As deep as dark* that so their sight may be
the more contracted, smallest Stars to tell:
So, pious m^n, that feign would fix their Eyes,
still on the Stars (the Saints in Heaven that dwell)
Descend (in Earth) to low'st Obscurities:
"For, to a loving Soul all labor's sweet
"That tends (although in Hell) her Love to meet.
Low is our Way; but, our Home most sublime:
if home we wou^d, th^n this low Way is best,
Which yet, grows steep ^omewhere, and hard to climb;
yet, Love o'ercomes it, and eternal Rest:
Vain pleasures are like Gold thrown in our Way;
and, while to gather it, we stoop, at least,
It let us us, and our Judgments does betray:
But if on Heaven our minds be altogether,
Nothing shall let our Bodies going thither.
Which way go you says Christ to those that stray?
I am the WAY* and whither will you wend?
I am the TRVTH: or else where will you stay?
I am the LIFE: that is, your Iournyes end.
Now if this Way do lead over steepes and plains,
If this Truth teach us, rising, to descend,
If this Life be not got but with our pains,
Then, woe to them that laugh, since weep should al;
And blessed they that weep; for, laugh they shall.
We should be, therefore, like the Egytian Dogs,
that drink of Nilus running, lest they should
By staying much, to drink like greedy Hogs,
the Crocodile might have them, so, in hold:
Nature does teach them, reas'nlesse, what to do:
then, shall not grace work much more manifold
With humane Creatures, that divine are too?
It should: then, we are mad, or reason lack,
to quench our thirst of having with our wrack.
What is it to have much more than Nature needs;
but, to have more then Nature well can bear:
Like one that is deadly drunk, or ouer-feedes,
whose excess makes his Death excessive cheer!
Enough, then, should be better then a feast,
since more is mortal, howsoever dear;
For, Nature cannot well so much digest.
"Much less then little (only) makes her grutch;
" Enough maintains her better than too much.
Besides; in us, Sin is more odious grown,
then in the Devil: for, his was but one sin:
Ours, numberless: his, yer Revenge was known;
ours, when we knew it; and might favor win:
In Innocence created, sinned he;
but we, when to it we had restored bin:
In malice he, of God forsook; but we
when GOD recalled us to his saving- Grace:
He damned, we saved: yet were in worser Case*
For we were saved in possibility;
but he condemned; so, could not saved be:
He sinned against one that him did straightly tie;
but we (worse f^ends) against one that made us free:
Against one he, that doomed him second-death;
but, we against one that did for us: so, he
Sinned less than we; which Hope quite banisheth,
Did not the time we live in, stir us ( thrals)
To call for Grace, that comes, if grief but calls.
He that of Sin, does know the large extent
and Hell of Hels the Soul incurres thereby,
Shall little feel his Bodies punishment,
though he, in life, a thousand d^athes should dye:
Which borne with Patience, for his Saviors love,
quite abrogate his passed impiety:
And future sins and pains from him, does shoue:
"Immortal pains, extreme in quality,
"Annihilate all mortals quantity!
Our Faith, in the beginning, thin was sown
in the afflicti'on, shame, and death of Christ:
And then with Martyrs Blood it was ouer-flowne;
nor, can it grow (or prosper) to the highest
Without showers, ceaseless, gushing from their wounds:
then, what art thou, that soon thy Faith deni'st
For fear of death, that but thy Judge confounds*
O! I am he, the frail'st of flesh and blood,
That lives for ill, and fears to die for good!
Yet for Christ it is more glory to be crossed,
then of him to be crowned an earthly King:
The last may be, by chance or Treason, lost:
but, from the Cross, immortal Crowns do spring:
To be in glory may proceed of Grace
without the glorifide his meriting:
But well-borne Crosses alter (quite) the case.
" Virtue consists in doing hardest things;
"And, vicious Fools have too too often been Kings.
Who suffers straight, has but one victory;
but, he that always does encounter pain,
And yet o'er-throwes the strong'st Extremity,
is crowned every day, and still shall reign:
And what is Death but our best earthly friend,
which kills our Flesh, our deadliest enemy?
So, friendly is both to and in the end.
Then Crauen, why do I so fly his force,
That saues me when he makes my corpse a corse!
For, if the pining of the body be
the pampring of the Soul; than, must this friend,
(That, with his pains makes virtuous Souls to fly
where they are pampered without mean or end)
Be still embraced, no^ fled: but, o frail flesh
this dying doctrine does but thee offend,
That hold'st it most erroneous, fond, and fresh:
Thou can not poise these treasures of the Sp'rit;
for, they are weighty and, thou art too light!
Thou must have all that may thy Senses charm
with sweet, as most effeminate, delights;
And fly'st from Death to honied pleasures swarm;
yea, follow'st them in their unconstant flights:
Austeritie, nor can, nor will thou brook,
since it quite mortifies thy lively spirits;
And, for thy life, still put'st thee to thy Book:
But, thou do long for all that makes thee light,
As well within, as (gaudy still) in sight.
Mean while thou burn'st to naught with flames of sin:
for, as the Lightnings flash, although it spares
The painted sheath, it melts the Blade within;
(which is the thing more worthy) so it fares
With sins pernicious fire-flash: for, it leaves
the goods and body sound: but, unawares,
The Soul, more precious, it of life bereaues:
"But, who to spare a Thing of naught, will spend
"Gods Goape (his Soul) he is mad, and cannot mend
Many (though Princes) poor, are in their store;
in Honors, abject; malecontent, in mirth:
Their flesh, self- frailty; their spirits, basely poor;
their Soul's the sinck^ of all the sins on Earth;
The Moths of Mankind sores of Sou'raigntie;
vnhappily-happie in their base-high Birth;
Who live like Monsters, and like Devils dye:
"The rich, possess; the meek, the Earth enjoy:
"For they have most, that have the lest annoy.
Look in the Graves, survey the Emperors,
Kings, Dukes, and Worthies of the Ages past;
Then look on those whom life and death obscures;
(poor Beggars) tell me then, who is first, who last;
Who rich, who poor, who fair, foul, high, or low:
but, if thou can when burnt be diverse Woods,
Their Ashes well distinguish; then, may thou
distinguish of their bodies, states, and bloods.
Then wherefore weigh we so our Flesh (misled)
That Is light as vanity alive, and dead?
And life, at best, is but a golden sleep,
lined but with silver, or more earthly dreams;
Or else a Tragedy (that moves to weep)
of ceaselesse troubles, and most dire Extremes;
A passing but from life is life; for, still
in stay'ing, it goes; yet unlike Water-streames,
That, running, stay alike, by Natures skill:
Streams, running, rest the same, and not the same;
But, still unlike, does burn our vitall-flame.
Things future, are beginning endlesly;
Things present, ever ending; and Things past
Quite dead or done: for, while we live, w^ dye;
and, dead, we live: so life is first and last:
Then, better dye to life, than live to death;
for, mortall-life (in Death) but time does waste:
And Death does gain of ^ime he shorteneth:
Who, for our good, our bodies still assailes;
And frees our Souls by ruining their Iayles.
It is but Natures necessary wrack:
then let us make it voluntary, that
Is necessary; and still bow our Back
under the burden of our common state
With all alacrity; and give to God
his own; which were most damned to alienate,
Since he, of Dolor^, paid for it, his lode.
Then, it were Sacrilege not to restore
That which he made and lent, and bought, and more*
Death's dreadful but to those that know him not;
to those that know him well, he is not so:
The Old, before their faces him have got;
the young, behind; while he does all o'erthrow*
Acquaintance with the Wars, estrangeth fear;
they dread not waves at Sea, though high they go,
that used are to them, (though they all orebeare)
And with them fight in front, or in the rear:
Then, not to fear Death, is with him to be
Familiar made; and, bring Sense under lee.
The Pilot, while he is his Ship to guide,
sits at the Stearne; for, there he most preuailes:
And, so the virtuous (maugre wind and Tide)
when through this stormy Sea of life he sails,
Sits at the Stearne; that is, life's hinder-piece;
where he, in Tempests (bearing lowest Sails)
Conducts it safely to the Port of Peace:
"To bear high sails, and still forbear the helm,
"Is Ship, and Freight (so) quite to ouer-whelme.
Death is the door, whereby we must go out
of straitest Bonds to freest Liberty;
Then as the Prisoner that of Death does doubt,
yet waits the while for his delivery,
Most joys in sitting at the Prison door,
that, when it is opened, he may instantly
Get out, t'enioy his freedom, as before:
So should our Thoughts be fixed on life's last step,
To which we soon may iump, but not o'er-leape*
Thoughts mortifide the ashes are, wherein
the fire of Virtue, being raked up close,
The longer lasts, and greater heat does win,
to kindle courage in our cold dispose,
That when Death comes, and those shall be vnrak't
we may rejoice our flame so freely goes
Unto her Sphere; then should it not be slack't
In Chimney of our flesh, where it does lie,
Like to be quenched with our iniquity.
And, as a flood that from a mountains top,
does (rolling) run, with strange* as ceaseless, noise;
And over many cr^ggy* Lets does hop,
till in the Val^ beneath, it rest enioyes:
So, fares it with our life; which we begin
with ceaseless outcries, for our felt annoyes;
Then down Times hours we run, through lets and sin,
Till in the end we rest in vale of Death,
To which we blow our selves by spending breath.
Then Death's our rest* for, since the same has past
through life's pure Veins, or rather Lords of life,
Of the least bittern^sse it has no taste;
but, freshest sweetness therein still is rife*
It is the virtuous peaceful Paradise;
but, to the vicious, it is a World of strife:
For, naught is plagued in Death, but mortal Vice:
Then he may well be styled a Martyrs Peer,
That virtuous Death does rather seek than fear.
Death to a Thiefs's compared; who, if he finds
the Man, he means to rob, upon his guard,
He speaks him fair; else him he binds and blindes:
so, Death is kind to those for him prepared:
But, cursed to them, that, careless, spend their breath:
for, all that watch for him, he does reward
With endless Life; the rest, with double death:
But, they that dye for Virtue or good note,
Though he o'er-throw them, yet they cut his throat!
And why should his worst look more irke, or fear
a Man resolved that he can dye but once:
Goliath bought a little Stone as dear
as Sampson did the House that chrusht his bones:
And, from a Chair to fall, the Neck can break
as well as falling high, as Thunder-stones*
And, all is but one Death, (be ^t strong or weak)
Deaths sharpest sting, the Heart but enters in,
Which dies with that, and so it will with a pin.
Why grudge we then, to endure for endless life
that, for vainglory, freely we endure?
Repine we not to dye in damned strife,
and, grudge to dye to make our life secure?
Is Death so sweet, when it the Diu'll commands;
and when God wills it, is the same to sow'r?
What manhood's this, whereon* now, manhood stands?
O ougly valor (if it valor be)
To flee to death; yet, fearing, life to flee.
Live well, and, so, dye well, perhaps, we may;
but live still, and not dye, we never can:
Life is not short, that, soon, goes well away;
and, longest life Truth calls but a spanne:
He dies old (though young) that well does die:
and Life well lost is better then ill wanne;
For, so to win we lose eternally:
Then, what can counteruaile eternal loss?
Nothing that is; no Patience bears that cross.
If we for virtue do our life forgo
our Pitcher's broken over the fountaine-head,
From whence, what filled it, came; and, where does flow
the Aqua vitae, that reuiues the dead:
Our liquor is not lost, but run into
the proper Fount, by Nature thither lead,
And heavenly Grace assisting Nature too:
Our life's a war, where patience guards from loss:
Our Captain, Christ; our Standard is his Cross!
But, seems God long, thy labors to content;
the more forborne, the more will be thy meede:
He takes on Intrest what before he lent:
and, takes delight t'o'er-guerdon each good-deed:
If in our Vs'ry, then, we wish delay;
fear we the Lord of All should fall to need,
That, on his Bond, we dare not give him Day?
And, shall we trust a Merchant, that may break;
More than that King, of whom all Kings do seek?
Admit thou should be rackt to strain the Truth,
(though Racks are made the truth to gain not strain)
Yet, if thou her believe, let never thy mouth
deny it for the cracking of a vein*
We owe so much to Truth* as should we pay
the real debt, to us should naught remain;
No, not our lives, which must, for her, away:
For, God and Truth are Relatiues. Not so:
For, God is Truth; then, for him All must go.
If Truth-pretending Turks or Infidels
should on our Plagues, which we for her endure,
Triumph; and make our Pains so many Hels,
alas* (poor Souls!) they (so) do but procure
Their own perdition: for, that God we serve
is God of vengeance; and the same will pour
On Good-pretenders that so ill deserve*
"To be for truth reprocht; yea, p^agu'd, or slain,
"Is to be glorious, free from Death and pain.
The red-hot It'n into the Water thrown,
thunders therein, as if it did it harm,
Yet, so, the force of burning's ouer-throwne;
the while the Water, cold before, is warm:
Like Thundrings Tyrants use, in every Age,
who, though against the Truth themselves they arm;
And with the blood of Martyrs quench their Rage,
Yet all their triumph's nothing but the noise
Of their own quenching, and the Martyrs Joys,
Then, if they show us Honors, Gold, or Iemmes,
t'intice us to their Faith; they shall but show
The Lion Chafe, which (chasing) he contemnes:
and if with Torments then, they us pursue;
The Salamander they but threat with fire;
which makes her rather to rejoice then rue:
So, that the worst they can do, we desire:
Then, through the Red Sea of our blood, thus shed,
Unto Heavens Holy Land we soon'st are led.
Saints on the Earth resemble Babes dead borne;
that are no sooner borne, but borne they be
Unto their graves; so, straight to Ashes turn:
but Tyrants (Viper-like) do live to ^ee
Their own Confusion; and the death of those
which they have martyred: so, from death, made free;
And, manumiz'd from this Worlds mortal woes.
The first, are borne to dye, to live in joy;
The last, to live, to dye in all annoy.
When Thieves an house do break, to rob by night;
(since it is a Work of darkness) first, they will
(That they may not be known) put out the light;
and so the good are handled by the ill
Lights of the World the Good are said to be;
but bad-men ( Sons of darkness) put out still
Those lights, lest men their darkest deeds should see;
For, all that evil do, the Light do loathe:
So, love they darkness; and, do darkly both.
Unto the light it is no reproach at all
though Bats and Owls abhor it; nor, is it
Disgrace to Wisdom, if but Idiots shall
condemn the same for Polly: they want Wit:
To judge of Wisdom, which is too too bright
for men to look on that in darkness sit;
To judge of coulors, blinde-men have no light:
The fault's not in the coulors they are so;
But in their Eyes that can no coulors know.
Far sweeter are the Tears of them that mourn,
then is their laughter that in mirth are lost:
All crosses by the virtuous so are borne,
that most they joy in that which grieueth most:
Like Roses mong the Thorns, their pleasures are
most sweet, when as they are most sharply crossed;
And, being at the worst, they best do fare!
But, put the least cross on a sensual Soul,
And it will (blaspheming) grudge, nay, cry, and houle.
The greater Ox the yokes worst part does bear,
(that is, the heauiest) Christ (that is, thy God)
Thy yokes most heavy part with thee does wear,
that so thou may, with him, the lighter plod
Through thick and thin: for him thou can not do
that he did for thee; he feels the Rod,
Yet he does all in us, and for us too:
"Mates in afflictions, make Affliction less:
Then, if Christ bear, with us, naught can oppress!
This life is but a lie; true life's not here;
it seems, but is not: so, it is not true:
Than, for a lie, or what does false appear,
let us not lie to God, or break our vow
We made in Baptism; but to cleave to him,
although for it, it might (perhaps) ensue
That we to him, in our own Blood should swim*
That water's strong; it will not let us sincke,
And, to engrosse sure Deeds, the only Inck^!
The life of David was but Tears and moan;
but Salomon^ was joy and Mirth through-out:
Yet David (sure) is saved: but, Solomon
whether he be, or no, Believers doubt.
Poor Lazarus lived here in dying-plight:
Dives in all that reueld with the Rowt
Of honied Pleasures, and extreme delight:
But he that lived in death, in joy now lives;
And he that joyed in life, in death now grieves.
As sweetest Wine does soonest boil our blood:
so, this Worlds favor works us most annoy:
The Water of Detraction then, is good
to mingle with it, lest we should be coy:
For, being gracious in the great Ones sight:
when Cunning clawing makes us doat, with joy,
We Are best remembered by the tongue of Spite:
"So, foes do often make those, that friends do mar;
"As many live most warily in War.
He that should pass a Foord, that swift does glide,
(so to prevent his giddiness of Brain)
Should fix his sight upon the further side;
not on the Water, and himself sustain
By one more strong, that, as his guide, should go,
lest that the Current, running so amain,
Should ouer-whelme him by an Ouer-throw:
Then, rest on Christ, and fix thine Eyes on bless*
while thou go through all Torrents of distress.
Through fire and water we must pass, before
we can arrive where nothing can distress:
Our flesh in both should purged be more and more;
for, in the p^r'st it is full of filthiness
In double kind: and, makes clean Souls the while
^o loathe their Lodge, so full of slut^ishnesse,
Consorted with all vices that defile:
" Pride, Envy, Wrath, Lust, Hate, with all amiss,
"The Speci^s are, and Flesh the Genus is.
For, flesh is earth; so, in our souls it sets
foul thoughts: (as earthly, as voluptuous)
The World as vain as c^rious thoughts begets;
the Fiend, malicious thoughts and envious:
Who on the flesh for help does much rely,
since household enemies may soonest hurt,
She being here freedeniz'd specially;
and we in bondage toiling here in dirt:
Then, does the World rely upon the Devil
To make flesh love the World: and, so, all evil*
So these procure us Worlds of Enemies:
if Avarice be quail'd; Lust, seconds her:
If Lust be foiled; Ambition straight does rise:
If she be down; then Anger wageth War:
If it be cooled, Pride, Envy and the like
give fresh encounters in this mortal Jar;
And all, with all their might, our ruin seek:
Then, where the fight's so fell and ceaseless too,
Were not for grace, most would themselves misdo.
Then, if we weigh our flesh how frail it is,
how full of all disease, in life, how dead!
In Death, how foul! (as naught so foul as this)
how then can we be light with so much Lead?
Or how can we be pleased such filth to feed
as in our Nature naturally is bred;
And, whence so many Prodigies proceed?
Then if we may be rid from such annoy,
But with one death, it is the life of Joy*
And what is Honor but a lie, like life?
for, as a Ship at Sea, with swelling Sails,
By winds, that for her peace are still at strife,
dancing upon the Waves with merry Gales
Allureth every Eye her pride to praise:
but when to the Haven she comes with her auailes,
She Is by the Searcher sacked, or Custom paies:
So they, in life* that are most honored,
Are often most dishonored being dead.
And likewise, while some sail on Surges high
of puft-vp vanities; and still ensue
The Tide of Times, armed with Authority,
are praised, and followed of the worldly Crew:
But if, by grace, they do themselves withdraw
into a virtuous life: then, straight their due
Is searched or sacked, by Custom, or by Law:
O th^n how blessed are they that most are cursed
(For their so blessed retiring) of the worst.
The Libard bears to man such mortal hate,
that in his face he flies when him he sees:
Therefore they use a man to figurate,
and show i^ him, at which forthwith he flees,
And piece-meale tears it; so, his wrath t'asswage.
showing thereby how ill with man he'agrees:
So Satan and his Members being too weak
To tear our God, to spoil his Pictures seek.
As Grapes unto the Wine Press all do come,
that come to Virtue in Faiths outward House:
They shall be crushed with many an heavy doom
of Justice n^m'd, but most injurious:
But, though their Pressures squize out all their blood,
yet in Gods Seller shall it find a Room,
And there made Rose of Sollace, sweet and good:
Then, let us still be pressed so pressed to be;
For, running loose, we soon run to our lee.
The more huge Billows beat upon a Rock,
the more they break; and so, to froth are turned;
The while the breaker seems their spite to mock,
that hurt themselves, not him, that so they spurned:
So, let the spightfull spurn us while they will*
our Rock stands sure; while they are ouerturn'd,
Whose blows, for us, Christ bears, or breaks still!
So, hurt themselves they may, but never us;
Since still, in him, we are victorious.
Yet it is too true, some wise and wicked too
(if possible the wi^ked may be wise)
May, if a King will do what they bid do,
undo a Realm with Legal Tyrannies;
And all the guilt thereof shall still be gilt
with guilefull gloss of Conscience, most precise;
Till all be as they would, though all be spilt:
"For, that is but spilt that stands but on the fall
"Of sacred Virtue, that vpholdeth all.
To carry Pagan hearts in Christian breasts,
is no new thing, though many (new) do use it:
"On fairest pretense the foulest purpose rests:
yet Beautie's good; but foulely some abuse it:
The heart of man so many windings has,
that for a Maze of skill none can refuse it;
Since hard it is to turn to every path.
O Christ! were thou on Earth as once thou were,
How would thou, now, behypocrit man's heart?
Such fair pretenses we may well compare
t'Egyptian Temples; fair, but most profane:
Garnisht with Gold, and Columns, rich as rare,
in the outward Rooms: but, if a view be taken
Of the inward, where their God is still confinde,
some lothed Viper, full of deadly Bane,
Or Snake or Cockatrice, we there shall find:
So, in such fair Pretense we (often) see
The Devil himself, as GOD, adored to be.
With Christ's plain Coat to hide vice (which enormes)
is to disgrace the Owner: or it is
The Devils virtue, that himself transformes
t'an Angel bright, to do the more amiss:
But, every Age has groaned with this Disease;
yet never Age, for that, was eased of this,
Groan while we will, it will us still displease:
Then make w'a virtue of necessity;
And, what we needs must bear, bear willingly.
For, these; and like afflictions, still must prove,
and purge our manners from the dross of blame;
From Earth to weane, to God to increase our Love;
like Smithes Forge-water that augments the flame:
And, pleasure is most pleasant unto those,
that have been least acquainted with the same;
As heavenly Joys are after earthly woes:
And, Griefs grow senseless in a virtuous Will,
Or rather sensual; for, they ravish still!
If in our foot, much more if in our Head,
a thorn be thrust, our Heart, nay, Soul will grieve:
Then fly we pleasures, as they us have sled;
and rather wish for pains that ease might give:
Then since our HEAD is thorned over all,
if we his Members be, shall we be fed
With honied pleasures while he tastes but Gall?
God shield we should: then let us only joy
In his sowre-sweetest Cross, and his annoy.
Moses did see him in the midst of fire,
and fiery Thorns: and in the mount among
Lightnings and Thundrings: Daniel did aspire,
to see his Throne, which fiery wheels did throng:
Then shall we look for more Prerogative,
than had these friends of God? then him we wrong
T'expect what he, in Justice, cannot give:
For, we must see him as the others did;
Else may we seek him, but he will be hid.
For, as the Sires delight to have their Sons
resemble them in favor: so it joys
Our heavenly Sire, to see us (wayward Ones)
like him, in patient bearing all Annoyes,
Which, for our good, his grace on us inflicts:
for, when we bea^e what beauty quite destroys,
(The wemmes and wounds of all his sore conflicts)
In his fair Eyes we are most lovely, then;
And foul'st, when fairest but in the Eyes of men.
We see a Dog, that but with crusts we feed,
will in our quarrel fight while he can move:
And Servants which we hire for little Meede,
will ne'erthelesse die often for our love:
Then shall we Christians be less kind then Beasts*
or thankful less, than those we hire for need
To him that gives us all that Faith requests?
O no: no, no, it were too great a blame
The dignity of Manhood so to shame!
The Flower of Iesse did most sweetly smell,
and came to perfect growth upon the Cross:
The fruit of life could not be gathered well
without sharp Thorns that stooke unto it close:
And Gall was tasted, in a deadly fit,
by the best Taster; who, by his life's loss,
Wanne Life to all that dye in him, and it;
And, till he rose from Death, he did not eat
The Hony-combe; but, fed on sower meat,
The Waters of Affliction are the streams
whereat our heavenly Gedeon still does try
Who are ^it Soldiers for his Wars Extremes;
and seu^rs such as on their Bellies lie,
To drink as thirsting that they, full, may rise;
from those that, for their mee^e necessi^ie,
Reach out their Hands to take what does suffice:
"Great Wealth and Virtue no agreement have,
"Since Virtue makes it serve her as a Slave.
Though Prisons, of themselves, be Satan's folds,
wherein, for slaughter, his best Sheep he keeps:
Yet may the Cause make them the safest Holds,
(yea, Heavens of Saints) for, tho the Linnet peepes
(When she is encag'd) at every loope and Chincke,
as longing to be gone, and often weeps
That she is restrained; yea, leaves her meat and drink;
Yet in the Cage she is from danger sure
Of Fowlers Snares, and Kites that would devour.
But those in Patience that their Souls possess,
(while they, in bonds, do Tyrants wrath assuage)
The sweeter sing, the sowrer their distress,
like well-taught Lynnets used to the Cage,
There learn they sweeter Notes than Nature gave,
when they abroad were in their Pilgrimage;
New exercise of Virtue, there they have:
Where may we sing with Quires of Angels then,
More free, then when most fast from mortal men?
Then out of Prison go we, when we be
put into Prison, so the cause be good:
For, Liberty is but Captivity;
that (lightly) makes more loose frail flesh and blood;
Kings Courts; yea, Heaven it self must yield, with awe,
t'a Prisons glory (though defiled with Mud)
That keeps Gods Servants safely for his Law.
"A Princes Presence makes a Cote a Court;
"And, that Pris'n's Heaven, where Saints and Angels sport.
The Coriander-seede, in pieces cut,
each piece brings forth as much as all would do:
And so a Martyr into Prison put,
and there first b^uiz'd, then, cut in pieces too,
No drop of blood, no piece (though turned to mould)
but it has force the Devil to undo;
And works more (often) then the Owner could:
For, if in private Iarres effused Gore
For vengeance cries; his can do that, and more!
Of all parts of a Tree the Root seems worst:
for, it is deformed, and most offends the sight:
Yet, all trees virtue thence proceeds first,
stem, branch, and leaves, flowers, fruit; yea, life, and might
The Root alone may challenge as her own:
for, by the same they are both borne and nursed,
Which in the Root (as in the womb) was sown:
So some like Roots, be'ing ragged in the Eye,
Dying for Christ, makes Christians multiply.
Some Trees there are, that, if their Rind be rent,
cut, pricked, or braiz'd, a precious Balm it bleeds,
In sight and savor fair and redolent:
but neither yields, till outward it proceeds:
So, Martyrs when their Flesh is gasht or torn,
out flows the Balm that cures their own misdeeds,
And others heales, that (so) to Virtue turn:
This balme's so sweet that it the World perfumes,
Whereby the Pagan Christ's sweet Name assumes.
The Roses sweetness, if untouched it be,
soon with the leaves does wither quite away;
But by the Fire when it is stilled, we see
it yield sweet Juice that hardly will decay:
Nay, more; the Leaves so baked into a Cake,
do long make sweet both where they lie, or lay;
And all that neighbors them, most sweet do make:
The leaves, so parcht, delighting still the Nose,
Immortal makes the Sweetness of the Rose.
So, Martyrs sweetly live, with Brambles keen,
since in their conversation they are pure;
Yet few can see it, since they live unseen:
but still from worldly Comforts make them sure,
Bolt them, nay, bray or burn them if you will
then will their virtue sweetly all allure:
And Heaven and Earth with divine savor fill:
Had they, by Nature, did, their leaf nor iuce
Had not been half so sweet, nor meet for use.
Dark is the Water in the Airy Clouds,
yet that, the Rose and Lilly brings to light,
Mantling the Earth with all that Nature shrowds
within her bowels yer the Waters light:
What are these Clouds (of which the Psalmist sings)
but Clouds of Witnesses (as black as bright?)
Grave Martyrs that give Truth true witnessings:
Their Blood the Water: and when out it poures
The Time looks black, but Saints spring with the showers.
With blood the Churches Bud came forth at first,
as earnest of the fruits she was to bear;
Who was no sooner'spoused unto Christ,
but in their blood her Infants drowned were,
To show her future throws in bearing young:
your years (sweet Lambs) could not believe nor fear;
But yet your flesh could dye to right Faiths wrong:
Thus did the Church as soon as she was wed,
With chastest blood forgo her Virgin-hed.
Then to our blood the Gates of Heaven fly open;
and, with our blood Hell-fire's extinguished:
Our Bodies blood does scowre our Souls like Soap;
and with our blood our Body's honored:
The Devil shamed, and God glorifide:
for when, in Truths defense, the same is shed,
It makes our deeds most glorious in it did:
The seed of Virtue, and the bane of Vice
Is blood so shed: " No price to'a bloody PRICE!
The resurrection of Truth, Faith and Fame,
did flowrish most when soakt in Martyrs Blood:
Whose Palms with weight grow higher, and their flame
does wax more strong, the more it is with-stood:
Their Spice, by pounding, yields sweeter sent,
and Lets to Truth are borne down with this flood:
Which let abroad, does grow more violent;
And, while it runs, it rores, and after cries
For vengeance on their Foes, Truths Enemies.
With Tyrants Thundrings, Errors Cloud is cracked,
th'inclosed light of Truth's disclosed so;
And showers of blood (that then for Truth are wrackt)
makes Martyrs more and more on Earth to grow:
For, still their Side by God, himself, is backed;
they ( Sampsons) with their Death, do quell the foe,
And most torment him, when they most are rackt:
then, good Cross (blessed sheep-crooke) Saints still keep
to Christ, whose Hook thou art, to catch his Sheep.
For, as a feate Embroderer, that has
a piece of Velvet brackt, t'embroder on,
So draws his Work, that he, to hide the scath,
embroders richliest in that place alone:
So* GOD upon the Velvet of our flesh,
all torn in time of Persecution,
Couers the Bracks with Beauty fair, as fresh:
So, that the other Parts are beautifide
By those rent parts, by GOD so glorifide!
And as the Paper-mill, of rotten Rags
taken from the Dunghill, by still mauling it,
Makes so white Paper, as the filthy Iagges
may now infold the purest part of Wit,
Or purest things that come from Heart, or Hand:
so, we by Martyrdom, are made most fit
(How ever base) in glory still to stand:
And made more apt (divinely) to comprise
Gods glorious Graces, and his Rarities.
Though th'vpper heaven does turn (by violent sway)
the lower, out of course, from East to West;
Yet, of themselves, they wheel the other way:
(for, they, by Nature, turn from West to East)
So, though from the East, where Truth begins to shine,
her Foes would force our Faith, or course, at least,
To Errors West, where Truth does still decline;
Yet must we stir, as Grace and Nature moves
Unto the East, where God our course approues.
A Martyr's like a Dye, which though it fall
this or that way, it falls no way amiss;
It flat will lie, or cannot lie at all;
so, Martyrs lie with Truth, where ere she is:
They will lie level with the Earth; nay, more,
In, or above it lie, or stand for this;
Hang, burn, or starve, all is one; they feel no sore:
Then when God throws at all, with them, to win,
At every throw, he draws some others in.
Abel he cannot be that is not taught
true patience by the malice of a Caine:
And happy he that like a Cole is caught
out of Afflictions fire with God to reign
While he is bright, and glowes with Charity:
for, whether to be white or red, in grain,
The Church were best, is uncouth to discry:
The Churches flowers the Rose nor Lilly want,
But both adorn, and make her triumphant.
The martyred Body of our Lord and God
is the main Rock from whence his Saints are hewne:
For, from his flesh they rent are with the Rod;
and by the rentings of the Rod are known
To be true flesh of his torn Flesh, and so
to be his Types, by which him self is shown
To Heathen-folke, that him desire to know:
"O! it is a glory past the height of FAME,
"To be like Christ in sufferings as in name.
The ancient Romans used, their force to try,
t'incounter Bears and Lions; and the Scarres
That came by savage Tuskes, they valued hye;
and piercings of their Paws so many Stars:
If in vainglory they such Dents endured;
what should we do in Christ, our Captains, Wars,
Be'ing of true glory, for our fight, assured?
We should (with Patience armed) encounter death;
And, for that gain, with torment, lose our breath.
Shall Saints fear Men, whom Angels ought to fear;
for, Saints shall judge the Angels; and, the F^end
Has cause to fear them; for, they rule do bear,
over his Legions; yea* his Forces rend:
The World should likewise fear them since the Saints
shall, with heavens Vmpier, judge it in the end;
Than he that at his threats, or torments, faints
Can be no Saint; but must be Judged of them
(A Coward) to foul shame, and pains extreme.
Elias must not fear, nor fear disguize,
to let the Mantle of his flesh to fall,
To fly in Coach, ^lame-wing'd; to Paradise:
Gedeon must break his earthly Pots, since all
Their Light's so seen to put his foes to flight:
Joseph must leave his Cloak, or else he shall
Be moved to wrong his Master in his right:
" Life leads to Care; but, Death to Comfort leads:
"Then Death, in Syons cause, in Sion treads.
At Sea, decays the Sailer; in his Tent,
the ventrous S^uldier; in the Court decays
The virtuous Courtier; Justice, in Judgment;
true Faith, in Friendship; Skill, in Arts Assaies;
In Manners, Discipline: so, we, alone,
(that, dying, live in these too nightly days)
Under the ruins of the World do groan:
All is quite or ^erlesse; which does portend
The World, with us, is even at an end.
And o what should I say, when Courage makes
the Cause nor good nor bad; for, Falsehoods Friends
Have did in Errors cause, at flaming Stakes,
as stout as Martyrs in their constant ends?
Witness that Legate, sent from Powers beneath,
who late in Smith-field, Error so defends,
That he out-fac'd Truth, men, flames, dread and death:
And Anabaptists there for Error stood,
A^ stout as those that, for truth, lost their blood.
But, Legate, though thou can no answer yield,
yet let me question thee as many do
Question the dead for Error which they held;
tell me, who gave thy false Faith Courage too,
That thou for Error should so stoutly burn,
for Error that must needs thy Soul undo,
If, on the Coals from it, she did not turn?
Can Satan counterfeit our GOD so nigh
In's Gifts, that men, for him, should stoutly dye?
But thou might answer; Faith, though false it be,
yet, if the Soul persuaded be it is true?
Upon the Heart it works morrally,
as Faith does, which to Heavenly Truth is due:
This made the Priests of Baal their flesh to wound;
