and many Indians sense of pain subdue;
Yea, burn with those, whose Faith, they hope, was sound:
Then not to suffer much; nor Constancy
Proves Error, Truth, which fire's too cold to try.
Then Truth must try her self by Reas'n and Faith;
but, where Faith bids believe, Reas'n still must be
Obedient to believe what ere she says;
though she say, Three are One, and One is Three:
A Maid's a Mother, that a Man had wiu'd:
true God, unmade, made true Man, really:
And that the Dead shall rise, as here they lived:
All this, and more, of Faith, must Reas'n believe;
But God (the Fount of Reas'n) this Faith must give.
Death is the worst of Ils, yet best to those
that dye for Faith well tryde: and who they be
The Conscience of the Dyers never knows,
if with the Rules of Faith they disagree:
Then God knows who are his: and Men may know
that all are his, his ^reest Spirit does free
From life, by death be it violent quick or slow:
A Saint as Man, may sear and faint in death,
As Christ did (dying) yer he yielded Breath.
Let this Cup pass, was Terrors proper voice,
yet vtter'd by our Saviors sacred Tongue:
Our flesh (he took) annoi'd, did make that noise,
fore-feeling it should be with Torments stunge.
My God, my God, why have forsaken me?
unto our Flesh entirely did belong;
Then may true Martyrs in Death drouping be
With sense of pain; but God, that gave them strength
To stand to him, through him, preuailes at length.
For, it is not hard when Gods soft comforts cheer
our Soul to suffer, torments to endure;
But when such favors are turned all to fear,
and in distress of Mind to hold us sure
To God, and for him all annoyes to bear;
that is a Miracle performed by Grace,
Past Natures best performance; and is dear
Unto the Doner; then, who does the same
Goes straight to glory through Afflictions flame^
For, Works of Justice we should rather do
than those of Grace: now Justice wills that we
In Truths defense should dye, with torment too,
though Grace, to us, a stranger seem to be:
" Obedience far excels Sacrifice;
the first, is duty, in the highest degree;
The other, in our Wills Devotion lies;
Then courage in our Death is no true Sign
Of life elsewhere, without the Cause divine.
For, through Vainglory some in Death have seemed
as bravely resolute, as Saints have bin:
Nay, often the first have been the better deemed
by outward-sight, that sees naught within:
Leaena being but a Curtezan^
tyring her Tortures, though she did for sin,
Spat out her Tongue, that to accuse began:
And many more, of like sure, so have did:
Then, by brave dy'ing, plain Truth's not iustifide.
But dye they how they can that dye for Truth,
they stoutly dye, since they dye willingly;
But much more they that dye in sportfull youth;
and though Deaths ougliest face may daunt their eye
When they behold him; yet, if they endure
that fear, and pain, which after they must try,
They stoutly dye, though saint be all their power;
Nay, more they do, since they so little can;
" Flesh is but mire, the Mind does make the Man.
But see what ends the Tyrants erst have made,
that of Gods Saints made ceaselesse Butchery:
Nero, the chief, that first did them invade,
in his own blood his murdring hands did dye,
And while he bled his last, he (crying) said;
Foulely I lived, and dye more filthily:
Thus, for his pains, in paining, he was paid:
Domitian, by his Servants being slain,
For doing like, the like reward did gain.
Fell Maximinus, with his Sons, was brought
to self same issue: Decius, with his Frye,
Incurred the like: Valerianus, caught
by him that swaid the Persian Monarchy,
Was cag'd in Iron, more fast then Lions are,
who, in the end, being flaid, did wretchedly;
But Dioclesian worst of all did fare:
For, he fell mad; ^o, made himself away,
While fire from Heaven his House did level lay.
So of the like, in life, and their Degrees,
I^ might count many dire and awful deaths;
All drank Gods vengeance Vials to the lees:
and, in their blood overwhelmed, they lost their breaths
For, God vnstings such angry Waspes and Bees,
since each their Stings in Saints too often sheathes:
" God burns his Rods when he has paid his fees:
Yet Stings of spite, in The Head of Power, with wit,
Can sting the World to death, if Heaven permit.
But howsoe'r the Almighty throws his Rods
into the fire, when he his Ire does cease;
Yet, often the scurged fall to greater ods
with Goodness, than before: The Churches peace
Makes her more loose then when she is bound to fight
(incessantly) with foes that her disease;
"For, they live wrong that rest to much in Right,
" Metal (though Silver) resting long vnscowr'd,
"Will canker; or, with filth, be quite obscured.
For (ah) this Witch (the World) with pleasing charms
so lullabies our Sense in soft delights,
That though we be, upon our guard, in arms,
yet we are taken in our Appetites;
And made to serve the Devil, and our Flesh
in strictest Bondage; while their Parasits,
Sinne-soothing Pleasures, do our Sense refresh,
To serve them with the more alacrity;
So, ^lee lets Grace our Sense to mortify.
A Parable.
Wee' are like a Man chaste by a raged Bull,
who in his flight into a Well does fall;
And, in the fall (by chance) he lights full
upon a Tree, that there grows in the Wall:
And, resting there, there sets his Souls delight:
but looking better on the place, withal,
He spies two Mice, one black, the other White,
Who still the Root of this his rest do gnaw,
And more and more asunder it do saw.
Then, underneath he looks, and there espies
a gaping Dragon threatening to devour him:
And at his feet four striving Serpents rise;
yet, looking up, he spies (what does allure him;
And makes him deem, he is from dangers free)
a little Honey (which he ever tryes)
Cleaues to a branch of that untrusty Tree:
For which, these dangers he neglects; and still
That Honey sicks, yet never can lick his fill.
The Moral.
The Bull, is Death; the World, the Well; the Tree,
our time of life; the white Mowse and the black,
The Day and night: the striving Adders be
the Elements, that strive us still to wrack:
The Devil, the Dragon: and, the Honey is
our whitest Pleasures, that are lined with black;
And, black within, for loss of Glories Bliss:
Who, therefore, would not deem that man were mad
That in such dreadful dangers can be glad?
What comfort can we have then, in a place
that is by the Prince of darkness governed?
Where every thing is in a cursed case;
and, by Gods foes and good-mens, peopled:
Where Pains be ri^e, extreme, and infinite;
but Pleasures few, and false, frail, dull, and dead,
Which, at the best, at least, do vex the sp'rit:
Where Plentie's full of peril; Want, of woes;
And (in a word) where all, that ill is, flows.
Then, cast we off these pleasures, that but cast
a mist before our Eyes, and mock our Sense:
But let us hug those pains, and hold them fast,
that bring eternal joys for recompense:
Now, if this Potion work not in sicke-mindes,
at point of death is their Intelligence;
Nay, Death the power of all their forces binds:
In few: Great things by greatest minds are sought:
The small but seek for shades, the shells of Naught.
To attain a quiet Life.
WHo would in quiet spend his life,
must shun the Cause of strifes Effect;
And yet with Vice still live in strife:
so, Strife retain, and it reject.
1
Hold no Conceit against that Conceit
the King maintains; unless it be
Against that Faith, whose form and weight
with TRVTH, well tried, does still agree.
2
Find never Fault, but when the same
concerns the Honor of the Highest:
Or else the Kings; to hear whose blame,
is blame which often to Death is nigh'st.
3
No Wager lay: for, that but stirs
the Losers heart to hate and ire;
Which often enflameth Ciuill-warres:
then, give no Fuel to this Fire.
4
Nor make Comparison: for it
is odious; and, works like effect:
Why should thy Will, t'aduance thy Wit,
another Wit or Worth deiect.
To praise thy self, is but Dispraise,
unless Spite wrongs thine Honor (known)
If others Shame thy Glory raise,
let their Shame raise it, not thine own:
For, it is but Shame, to glory in
another Shame, because we yet
Are free from blot: but Praise we win
by hiding both our praise and it.
This is the way to earthly Peace;
Without which grows all strifes increase.
A Cordial to cheer the Heart under the Cross of Confinement: written to a
great Lord, once a perpetual Prisoner.
WHile yet thou lie in Afflictions fire,
more bright to make thee, and increase thy worth,
From mine Inventions Mud, I send this Mire,
to cast upon the flames, if they break forth.
Then, deigne t'accept (vnhappy-happy Lord)
this Muddy Stuff, my creeping Muses Meat;
The rather, since some ease it may afford
in plaistring Patience if it scorch with Heat*
For, greatest Spirits do greatest Passion feel
in bearing Crosses, though but small they be;
But under great, great Men do weakly reele,
though greatest Men from weakness should be free.
But thou (to thy true glory be it said)
do cross expectance, bearing so thy Cross,
As those that are by Hands of Angels staid;
so, draw'st much winning out of little loss!
For, Liberty to lose, or terrene trash,
(the Mind being free) is better lost than found;
Which often, on Waves of Weal, their Owners dash
on Rocks unseen, which either part confound.
Now be'ing thus staid, thou can not rise to fall,
Fortune has bruiz'd thee, but on Safeties Base;
That now thou can no longer be her Ball,
to strike thee in Life's Hazard, for her Chase.
Now may thou sit securely where thou art,
and see (unseen) the Worlds Reuoluings still;
And how men live by industry, or Art;
and what events ensue the greatest skill.
So sitting, bound to Safeties Shore, at ease,
thou may with ioyfull-Sorrow freely see
How other Folk are tossed on Dangers Seas,
as they that bear the highest Sails still be.
Now on the top of some proud Billow borne
as high as Heaven (while Billow-like they swell)
Then, by a Crosse-Sea is that Billow torn
be'ing at the highest; so, straight they sincke to Hell.
And they that have the Wind and Tide at will,
each Moment fear the Wind may turn about;
And so their good is never free from ill,
because their Hopes are ever bound to doubt.
But now thy Will (familiar with thy Cross,
all Storms of Passions being ouer-blowne)
Has ever Calmes that never threaten Loss,
that more then now, thou never did hold thine own.
Now Mischief cannot see thee, though she would
look never so narrowly to glance at thee:
For, thou art hid in Britains strongest HOLD,
where safe thou holdst thy self and thy degree.
And some that Life immurde, would have to chose,
although, as Monarchs, they might all control:
As greatest Charles his Empire did refuse,
and shut his Body up, t'enlarge his Soul.
For, that great Priest of Hyppo held but right;
who rather out of Hell his GOD would see,
Than be in highest Heaven, and miss that sight;
then Bondage, with that Sight's divinely free!
And long I wish (great Lord) thou may be so;
(though short I wish thy Troubles) and that God
That has perhaps t'vplift thee, brought thee low,
will make a Staff of that that was thy Rod.
The Hearts of all, in his all-holding HAND,
he wields at will, and Patience will requite;
Then, thy Commanders Heart he may command,
(in time) Sunne-like, to fill thy Moon with light.
Then, when thou have regained this Comforts Sun,
thou w^ll may say (as some have said of yore)
"The had been undone, had thou not been undone
since then thy Moon shines fuller then before.
For, even as when the Moon is at the full,
she from the Sun is most remote we see:
So, in thy Wane (perhaps) this Sun may pull
thee to him near, to light thy Heart, and thee.
That so it may I Will pray; and pray that thou
may Grace attract by virtue of thy Grace;
Meek Patience can the Heart of Highness bow,
and make self Wrath to show a cheerful Face.
For, when our lives do please the Lord of Life,
he will make our greatest Foes our greatest Friends:
Then shall our Troubles cut the Throat of Strife,
and make our peace to make our pains amends.
Long life is promised those that live aright,
then may thou all disfavors ouerliue:
" Patience o'er-comes what naught o'ercomes by might,
even God himself, and makes him to forgive.
But say the worst should hap (which hap's the best)
that thou should live and die in state confinde,
Thy state's most blessed, since so little blessed
with Freedom, that to Earth enthrals the Mind.
Yet Liberty thou have as large and free
as highest Virtue (Angel-like) does crave;
For Men like Angels love with Christ to be
in's blo^dy-sweat alone, or in his Grave.
And, if that fellows in Affliction make
affliction less, thou have thy fellow- Peers
In worser plight, whom Death did even forsake,
that they might live with thee to ease thy Cares.
"A good-mans state scorns Pity howsoe'er:
for, though it be engulph'd in deep'st distress,
Yet his high Virtue him aloft does rear,
that no Calamity can him oppress.
And though he be coupt up in Little-ease,
his spacious Mind to him a Kingdom is;
Wherein he wanders Worlds that most do please^
for, Heaven and Earth holds that great Mind of his!
While in his Conscience Theater is played
the Comedy twixt his Souls Spouse and her,
How can his Soul but wander all unstaid
through worlds of joy, although he cannot stir!
When as a Caesar (in all liberty)
bathing in Pleasures, or more sanguine Streams,
Upon the Rack of Conscience bound, does dye
extremest Deaths, in midst of Sports extremes!
O did thou know some poor spirits Rauishments,
when as (entranced) they feel unbounded Bliss,
Crowns thou would loathe, as crossing those contents,
and let the Cross quite break thy Back for this!
It is not; no, it is not high estate
has highest Pleasure; but it is only those,
That, for those Pleasures, fading pleasures hate:
"but they in Hell, no other Heaven suppose.
While outward Comforts compass us about
in Griefs pursuit, we to those Comforts fly;
But when they break the King, we straight run out,
to find more sixed joys, or (ioylesse) dye.
Then is that sour Affliction highly blessed
that more Perfection brings (like Ligatures,
That hurt to heal, and wring but for our rest
so, they are blessed whom Walls from wrack immures
We Prisoners are within Heavens outward Walls,
and are, by Nature, all condemned to die;
To Death we must, when Death (our Heads-man) calls,
some to dye gently; some, more violently.
And though our Prison be as wish we would,
and may, perhaps, therein go where we will,
Yet (like the stricken Fish) we are in hold;
and are in hold to him that sure will kill.
But here is the ods; those in close-prison pend,
are there with Death much more familiar made;
So that, in favor, he their griefs does end
for endless joys and peace which cannot fade!
But, those that (loosely) have the World at will,
do take their swinge, as Fish (if hanged) desire,
Till they be tirde with Pleasure, paining still,
then gently come to Hand; so, to the Fire.
And see how those that (care consumed) do climb
at Sternes of State (still menaced to be riu'n)
How public Toils engrosse their private time,
that they can scarce a Moment spare for Heaven.
And public Persons (if they mighty be)
the public state, and theirs, they still must eye:
So, to their Souls they scarce have time to s^e;
which, so neglected, often unawares do dye.
Then (noble Lord) if in thy self confinde,
thou art most happy thus confinde to be:
And since our Bodies do but Iaile our Mind,
while we have Bodies, we can never be free.
Then, if thou weigh'st the volubilitie,
of Time or Favor, Fortune, or this ALL,
Thou will but loathe their loose uncertainty:
for, hardly Ought does rise, but soon to fall.
Who ruled this Realm three thousand years agone?
so many ruled it since, that none does know.
"A Plow-mans blood, in time, ascends a Throne;
"And Royal Blood descends unto the Plow.
Yet, that King knows not from the Cart he came;
much less that Carter knows he came from Kings:
"But Times vicissitude is Fortunes game,
whose Rest puts up and down all eathly Things!
Then if we look on Life; how frail is that?
resembled to a shadow of a Dream;
To smoke, t'a thought, to naught, t'I wot not what,
far less then naught, that can so much as seem!
A Grape-seede one, an Hair another spils:
some Smoke does choke; mere nee^ing some destroy:
Some other Choler; and, some laughter kills:
some fear; and (which is strange) some die with joy!
So that when our last Grain, is running out,
no Grain so small but turns our flesh to dust;
Be we as Giants, strong; as Lions, stout;
all is less then nothing, then to naught we must.
The Grave (too like a Ierfe) does naught but fill
his greedy Panch, and straight out-straines the same:
Then fill again, then strains; then fill it still,
till it all Flesh consumes that Nature frames.
One rots therein to give another place;
a second to a third, and so, forth on:
Till Earth yield up her dead; and she embrace,
her funeral flame to lea^e Corruption.
Then, since that Life and Flesh so soon decay,
why should our flesh with life be long in love?
This world is but an Inn; this Life, a Way;
(a wrackfull way) that Wisdom lothes to prove.
Which has, of yore, made Kings to quit their Crowne^
the lighter, so, the Way of life to run:
Directed, by the Cross (over Dales and Downs)
in private Paths, the public Toils to shun.
We (like to Fools or Babes) for Bables long,
wherewith we hurt oursel^es and others often;
Yet straight we whyne if they from us be wroong,
our Natures, toward Folly, are so soft.
But our all-wise-celestiall-louing Sire
takes, or keeps from his Children which he loves,
All that may harm them, though they it desire,
but gives, though it displease them, what behoues.
Some long for State, and what is that but strife,
more full of trouble, then it is of State;
(With dangers mixed) a simple Hell of life;
which none do love, but those that rest do hate?
Some love to bear the most imbrued Swords
before the Majesty of Victory;
And what are they but Butchers made of Lords,
that (like fiends) Lord it over Calamity?
Some hidden Arts do openly desire
and seek for knowledge only to be known;
But knowledge such, is light but of Hellfire,
to see (with Eve) such Prides confusion.
Some fame affect, and for it venture far,
seeking by Sea and Land the same find;
"But Fame most follows those that flee from her;
and, often who meets her, she o'erthrowes with wind.
In summe^ both all and some (o strangest Case!)
have hurtful humors; which (if not restrained
By him that is the Lord of power, and Grace)
all would to naught, where Grace should never (...) gained.
Then, if our Flesh and Sinne-corrupted Bloods
could rightly feel, they well might see that Hand
That made us, will not mar us with his goods;
unless our selves his powerful Grace with-stand.
Our dearest Sense is Sight; yet if the same
offend that Grace, we must pull out our Eyes:
Then must we Limbs, of less account, vnframe,
much more Things less, if they against It rise.
"The greatest Cross is never to be crossed;
"the Way to Heaven is by the Ports of Hell;
"The Waters most corrupt, that least are tossed;
"and their account exceedes, who most excel!
He Is rich enough for Virtues choicest friend,
that neither needs to flatter, nor to borrow:
To lade our Backs with Baggage till they bend
(wandering in stumbling-wayes) augments our so^row.
" Abundance is a Burden to the Soul,
and strongest Souls can hardly it abide:
For, Men that, being mean, could Pride, control,
be'ing mighty made, are most controlled by Pride.
Nature's suffiz'd with Nothing, in respect
of that our Wolfe-like Appetites require;
And they as Naturals great-men reject,
whose Souls have not the power of great Desire.
"But greatest Men have not still greatest Grace;
(ah would they had, then should thou soon be free
From thy restraint) and all desires are base
of Greatness, that with Goodness disagree.
I wade too far (perhaps) in Dangers Deepes,
that may o'erwhelme the rash, though never so tall;
But Truth's my Guide; and, Care my Footing keeps
on double Duties Ground, and firm in all!
Yet wot how ill it stands with Policy,
to fancy those whom Times disfavor most;
Since Fancies such, acquire but Jealousy
(if not much worse) of those that rule the Roste.
For, Wisemen shift their Sails as Wind does shift,
and, but whom Fortune favors, favor none:
For, if Kings have with Fortune been at shrift,
they leave them to their Penance post alone.
But I conceive it a prodigious Sin,
(like that of Judas ( Peter's I would say)
Who left his Lord when trouble he was in,
which (in effect) is merely to betray.
I fear not Justice, since she does command,
that we should love our friends in spite of Fate;
And, to the Alter, with them go or stand,
though we might (therefore) be o'erthrowne with hate.
Then Justice warrants me in what I do;
and I will do but what selfe-Iustice would:
That Is love my Liege, obey and serve him too;
yet love that Lord that likes me as I should.
Let him have never friend that leaves his friend,
in show of sound affection, in distress:
And let highest Wit to lowest Hell descend,
that weighs ought more then some friends heaviness.
Let those that wait on Fortune weigh the Times
in Scoles of greatest Sculs, I, little i,
Do little weigh the ways how other climbes,
since I would live as longing well to dye.
Beyond my Birth, has Fortune been my foe,
she never yet so much as smiled on me;
No force, since I my self the better know;
and see the World while me it does not see.
Fear they her frowns, that care but for her fawnes;
I fear, nor care for neither: (being white
With Cares and Fears) for my Grave open yawnes
to swallow me to save me, from her spite.
Enough (great Lord) my Proheme is a Feast,
whereat my Muse does surfeit with sowre-sweetes;
Hard to receive, and harder to digest,
where love and rashness, Rhyme and Reason meets;
But if they meet with Grief that meets with thee,
I grieve with joy: for, thou art fast and free.
A Dumpe, or Swans-song.
ALl in a gloomy shade of Sicamour,
that did his leaves extend (like Shields) to bear,
The Beams of Phoebus, darted in his power,
at those that underneath them shrouded were)
I me reposed, while my Thoughts did range
here, there, and every where, where thoghts might roame;
So, by their change at last, my latest change
became their Subject, with my latest Home.
And when, with Travel, they themselves had tired,
I likewise tired with life (that stirred them) too,
Thus flasht I out, (with sacred fury fired)
and my thoughts Bottom thus did I undo.
Why long I longer here to live in death?
for life, if mortal, dies all the while:
Be'ing but a puff, but of the weakest Breath;
yet, blows me ( Weakness) into strong Exile.
As soon as borne, was I condemned to dye;
since when, Time has but executed me;
Yet life prolongs in dying misery;
so, yet I am as those that dying be.
To him that gave me life, a death I owe;
which, since I can, I must, and shall repay:
His Powr's as great to take as to bestow;
then will I pay him, though I quite decay.
I, dead in Sin, his only Son he slew,
to please his Justice, and to make me live:
Since me he bought, I Will give him then his due;
which had, I have much more then that I give.
Death soon will rid me from this life's annoyes,
( Annoyes that naught can rid, save death, from life)
And put me in possession of those Joys,
that are as far from end, as free from strife!
And were not madness to repine, that I
had not had life when Eve did Adam wiue?
Then, it is but all alike to live, and dye;
as it is, Not to have lived, and not to live.
Then, life IS not, that not immortal is;
for, mortal life is but Deaths other name:
Nor is that Bliss, that is not fearelesse Bliss;
nor glory, that is subject still to shame.
The Days of Heaven are datelesse; since the Sun
that makes them such, does neither set nor rise;
But stands (as it shall, does, and still has done)
fixed in the Noone-stead of ETERNITIES.
Here one's the ruin of another Day,
while (like a ne'er-suffized Grave) the Night,
Does bury both in silence; yet, does prey
upon them both, till both play least in sight.
" Death is the door of life: so, would I live?
then, through this door to life, I needs must go:
For, through this door Death, LIFE it self did drive:
then, since LIFE did for life, I must do so.
Two only had the privilege to wend
another way to life that mortals were,
But it was in fiery- Charets, to this end,
that Fire should flesh refine, yer it came there*
There, where all joys, united, are of force
(for, force united, stronger makes the same)
The spirit and flesh (both ravished) to divorce,
and melt their powers in loves eternal flame.
What Lets shall let me then, from Paradise?
Mountains of Gold, and Rocks of rarest stone,
Crossing my Way, I (trampling) will despise,
if thither Hope but go with me alone.
This WORLD's a Vale that ceaseles tears do spoil;
and make it so a Bog, or loathsome Lake:
Then who but Swine (that pleasure take in Soil)
will here (if they can choose) abiding make?
Heaven is my Home; the HIGHEST, my Father is;
his SON, my Brother; Angels, are my Friends:
Then while from Them I am, I am amiss;
and, lightly, miss the Means to so good Ends.
My Body's but the Prison of my Soul;
which straits her more, the more that Prison's free:
Time's but the Rock that up my Life does roll;
and Earth the Place where Heaven spinnes it and me.
Here must I fight till Death for endless Life;
"The Chariot of my Triumph then, is Death:
Then (as I would be free from endless strife)
to mount this Chariot I must spend my Breath.
The ground whereon I tread's the ground of Grie^e;
so that each step does grieve me: for it is
A Sanguine-field, that bears Hurts, in chief,
crossed with sinister-bends; and All amiss.
Then here to be, amiss is to be borne
in Dolors Field, to every foul Disgrace:
O Death then help my Souls house to adorn;
and let thine Arms be mine; for, life's are base.
Am I not dirt and dust? then, marvel is it
if I, but with a thought, be that or this?
A shadow by some substance, does subsist:
but, all my substance, but a shadow is.
The Sun does rise and set; the Moon does hold
a constant course in most unconstant state:
The Earth now quick with heat, then dead with cold,
does show their plight that It preambulate.
Then o ye Saints (whose Bellies being rife
with Waters both of life and grace) be ye
Pure Aquaducts, by, life, to bring me life
from the Wellhead, that fill may you and me.
The Grave (though wide it gape) dismayes me not,
since it is the Gate of glory, rest, and peace:
And though therein my mortal Part must rot,
yet thence it springs with much more fair increase.
If the last breath we call our Bodies death,
then may we call the other Breathings deaths;
Since Life and death do come and go with Breath,
we have as many deaths, as we have breaths.
Yet, twixt this life, and that we death do call;
this ods there is; while life does last, we dye:
But when Death comes, we die no more; but shall
by dying well, live well immortally.
O then, look how the Labourer for Night;
the Pilot, for the Port; and for the Inn
The Poast does long: so does my tired spright
(by death) still long for Life, and rest therein.
Death is my Hope: than fear not I his knife;
Fear is his Sting; but, Hope has pulled it out;
The mortall'st Wounds, immortal make my life:
then, better dye in Hope, then live in Doubt.
If Death be painful; then, is pain sustained
before, or, at the Article of Death:
But not before: for then, but thought is pained;
and at the instant it is but rest of breath.
So that in Death, is rest without disease:
then Death be kind, and rest my life in thee:
While others, (that do cast such sums as these)
these Ciphers sum, decyph'ring thee and me.
And, Ciphers cast life's Ciphers to, and fro,
that I their number (seen) may multiply:
Take naught from Naught, and naught remains; and so,
the sum of All is less then vanity.
Ciphers (not Numbers) call I them, because
they run (sans number) roundly to the Grave:
At which my Muse, being now arrived, shall pau^e;
referring these to those that Science have
To cast up life's account; and to forecast
the stricktnesse of Life's great account at last.
ESSAIES.
The Fool has said in his heart, there is no God. Psalm. 14.1.
THat GOD IS, no Man ever made a doubt;
if doubt some did, they did it not as Men:
For, faithless men, by mere sense, GOD find out:
what are these senseless God-deniers then?
They are not Fiends; for, they have humane Souls;
and Fiends confess, with fear, there is a GOD:
Much less, not Angels, Beasts, nor Fish, nor Fowls,
for, these praise God! What then? Even their own Rod.
Who do themselves, tormentingly, confound;
hardening their Hearts; and, so, plague Sin by Sin:
Yet joy in that which does their Conscience wound:
is it possible such Creatures ere have bin?
It Is possible, for, such there be, God wot,
That know not God, because God knows them not.
No pleasure to the pleasure of the Spirit.
TWo Joys there are, whereof, the one is not;
of Flesh the one, that other of the Spirit:
The Spirits joy is real, active, hot;
but, that of flesh, is vain, cold, dull and light.
How then can they be two, if but one Be?
one is; but is, but merely in Conceit:
Which in Conceit, is forgde by Fantazie;
and whatsoever is forgde, is but deceit.
Yet in this mere deceit, most men conceive
most pleasure to consist; and, it to buy,
They most essential perfect pleasure; give:
so, make their Judgment give their Sense the lie:
For, Sense could never tell, by her Receipt,
That such Joy Is, that is but in conceit.
Vanity of Vanities, all is but Vanity. Eccles. 1.2.
A Mortal Eye can see but mortal Things;
and whatsoever is mortal, is but vain:
Then all we see is vain, though Crowns, or Kings;
yet Men will lose themselves, the same to gain.
And yet, thy ween, they win by so great loss;
o corrupt Judgment! Men made to be lost:
Who will all Vices (hatched in Hell) engrosse,
them to retaile, to get but Care, with Cost.
It Is said, Light gain does make an heavy Purse;
but, this light gain does make an heavy Heart:
To gain all blessings, with Gods heavy Curse,
is too light gain for such an heavy Thwart:
For, who does purchase All at such a Price,
Does buy but extreme Vanity with Vice.
Fly vain Pleasures, as Pains intolerable.
IF Vanity be All, and All be vain,
how scapes he from this All, that is All in All?
It is, because He ever does remain,
the Cause of Causes metaphisicall.
Since fiends immortal are, not vain they are:
for Vanity is but the Instrument
Wherewith, in sport, they do this All ensnare,
to bring the same to Be as they are bent.
And, so, unbend their being, and, distort
the even Compass that became their Form:
So Vanity them backward bends, in sport;
and, Satan still, in sport, does them deforme:
O then let All that would be bent aright,
Beware these sports, that do distort them quite.
To the good, the Worst falls out for the Best.
THe Cross and Crown, on Earth, our value try;
as Crowns alone, in Heaven, our virtue crown:
In Earth, if crowned, we swell in Heart too hie,
and, under Crosses we lie basely down.
But yet, if Grace do Nature ouer-sway,
and that a Cross, or Crown, alike we bear,
A pride of Grace, our Nature, will bewray;
so, in our Nature, Sin, though dead, does steer.
And yet this Pride does humble us the more;
for when we mind it, grief does us pursue:
So, is our Sore still cured by our Sore;
for, still we heal as we our grief renew:
This, is a Work of Nature; that* of Grace;
And this and that, runs with us all our Race.
To the Lady Anne Glemmam, upon the death of her noble Father.
TO lie down under Crosses, is to lie
in our Confusion: for, that is Cowardice;
And has no taste of true Humility:
then, such prostration is an abject Vice.
Nor is it the Way from Crosses to be free
to sink beneath the Cross, which weighes the more
The more we, under it, so, humbled be;
but HE that bore your Sins It stoutly bore.
Yet is the Cross imposed to humble us;
nor, is it removed, till we be humble made;
How much more low, so much more glorious;
(so as the Cross does us not ouer-lade)
Then if we meek be made, we win by loss;
And cut a Crown of Glory from the Cross.
That to sin finally maliciously, is irremissible.
THe oftener Sin, the more grief, shows a Saint;
the oftener Sin, the less grief, notes a Fiend:
But to sin often, with grief the Soul does taint;
and, often to sin with Joy, the Soul does rend.
To sin, on Hope, is Sin most full of fear;
to sin of malice, is the Devils Sin:
One is, that Christ may greater Burden bear;
the other, that his Death might still begin.
To sin, of frailty is a sin; but, weak:
to sin, in strength, the stronger makes the blame:
The first, the Reed, Christ bare, has power to break;
the last, his Thorny Crown, can scarce vnframe:
But, finally, to sin maliciously,
Reed, Crown, nor Cross has power to crucify!
That mortal Life is a mortal Plague.
THis Life, of ours, is called Life most amiss;
which may be termed, more truly, life's disease;
Whose perfect'st Pleasures are opposed to Bliss,
and, greatest pains grow from her greatest ease.
One, has the Plague, we say, and he will dye,
that yet may live; then much more may we say,
That One has Life, and Death he cannot fly:
for, Life's a Sickness mortal every way.
Does mortal life, then, bring the mortall'st death?
then, no Disease so mortal as it is:
A Plague of Plagues then, is our mortal breath;
yet mortal Men would still be plagued with this:
Though Life be Plague of Plagues, yet this desire,
Is the highest Plague, whereto no Plagues aspire.
Too much Honey breaks the Belly.
SWeet honied Life, thinks one, that Honey draines
from bloomes of Helebo^e (this vain Worlds-wealth)
Which, though It breaks his Belly, yet his pains
seem, to his Appetite, true Signs of Health.
O bewitched judgment! Senses r^ft of Sense!
deem ye that sweet, that yields Eff^cts so sour?
That spoils the Will and soiles the Intelligence?
and Soul and Body, quite, in dung, devour?
Yet, those whose Spirits are turned to grossest flesh,
nay, those whose flesh seems turned to purest sp'rit
Are thus bewitched; which Sweetes their Sense refresh;
who sting like Waspes, if them they lose by might:
If thus they fare by whom the World is led,
What meruell though in sweet Sin It be dead?
The Fool makes a mock of Sin. Pro. 14.9.
WHo laughes at Sin; for Sin can hardly weep:
who ie^ts thereat, is mad, or misbeleeues:
Then, since the World still laughes at Debt so deep,
it shows it madly sins; and never grieves.
Sin is a Stinger; and who feels it not
is mortifide, not to, but in foul Sins:
Then, does the World, in Sin, but stincke and rot;
for, it feels not when Sin ends, or beginne^.
If it do stincke, what Nose can it abide?
if it do rot, what Eye, or Taste, or Touch
Can be content by them it should be tried?
Then only Hearing hears it without grutch:
And that is because the Dead, in silence, cry
Cave to those, that, living, are to dye:
Then they that love it, in such loathsome plight,
Have neither Sense, nor Reason, Flesh, nor Spright.
The World is in a desperate plight, for which Christ refused to pray.
But if the World be dead, God owes it not;
for of the living, He is God alone:
And, if not Gods, it is the Devils Lot;
which bounded is with the chief Cornerstone.
From that Stone upwards, all to God belongs;
and from it, downwards, all the Devils is:
For, God, being just, the Devil never wrongs;
but; lets him have his due; as He has His.
Then, to be Gods, is still to rest thereon:
but who can rest there, that God does not stay?
Then, since the World refused this Corner Stone,
God, for the World, refused but to pray:
O woeful World, how can thou merry be,
That so forsook'st him, that so leaves thee?
That all Heresies are grounded on the infallible Scriptures erroniously
interpreted.
EAch Bible-bearing Sectarie will say
he is in the Truth; and proves it by her Word:
Thus, is the Word of Truth wrencht every way;
and made a Text that Falsehood does afford.
Yet, Truth's but one, though Falshood's manifold;
and when Truths Saints, with her Word, do conspire
To find her out; that Truth embrace we should,
though we should mount to her in Coach of sire.
No Exposition of the Truth is true,
but what Truth makes her friends, alone, to make:
Who know Her best, and what to Her is due;
but, foo^es, wise in their own Eyes, both mistake:
For many Eagles Eyes have better sight
Then one blind Bats, that hardly see the light.
That the Eye is Sinnnes Burning-glasse, working upon the Heart and Soul.
AS Sinne's most conversant with outward Sense;
so is she most familiar with the Eye:
For, she sits in the eyes Concupiscence
as in her Throne of greatest Majesty.
From eyes, to eyes, Sin does, in triumph, Tilt:
(a fiery Serpent, clad in silver Rays:)
The end of whose Carr^ere is, where her guilt
makes black the Soul with Dolor and Dispraise.
Heuah first sinned: but, ere her Heart, her Eye
did Sin commit; and all the lustful Crew
Melt in that Sun, like Ye, until they dye:
yet, like dead Flies, those Rays, their lives renew:
Then, since this Sun exhales such Humors ill,
We must with Sorrows Clouds, eclipse It still.
To take is to give; for a Gift, Liberty.
Our Mouths run over with false Superlatiues,
in praising him, though bad, that did us good;
Which are naught else, but true demonstratiues
of the Corruption of our basest blood.
For, Pride can give as much as Charity:
and Tyranny as much as Mercy can:
But, who appl^uds or Pride or Tyranny,
do Monsters praise, the Mind does make the Man.
Then shall we take no good gifts of the ^ad?
I say not so: and yet, I say, who will
Take Kingdoms of the Diu'll, are worse than mad;
for He does good to no man, but for ill:
Then since, for Gifts, M^n give their Liberty,
Such Gifts bind Takers often to villainy.
Again, of the same.
ANd yet blind Justice sees her Sword to sheath,
if any offer her a Sheath of Gold;
Nay, high and hardy Hands often sincke beneath
the abject Gift and Giver, to uphold.
There Is naught more free than gift: yet naught more ties
the Hand, that takes them, to the Gi^^rs will:
And yet (^h woe therefore) some, god^y-wise,
take Gifts, as good: and give, as gifts were ill.
Were I a Devil, yet were I liberal,
(in this damned Age) I would be served with Saints:
For, if I sent bright Angels to them all,
they would adore me, maugre all restraints:
But, were I GOD, if Gold were not my Friend,
Pure Saints, in show, would loathe me like a Fiend.
To him that has, shall be given; and from him that has not, shall be taken
away, that which it se^mes he has. Mat. 25.2^.
HIs Word is Truth, that said, to him that has,
shall still be given; and, from him that lackes
Shall be withdrawn that which he holds, with scath;
so, this World makes men, made; and marred men, wracks.
Who least does lack, has most bestowed on him;
for GOD and M^n give richest gifts to Kings:
So, he that does in all abundance swim,
each Hand to him much more Abundance brings.
What means this Mystery so mystical!
what! World, begin'st thou now, with Age, to dote,
That Thou do give some All; some, naught at all:
and, sinck'st some, sincking, to make swimmers flote?
I know now what this means; but, this I know,
It Is too much to be loathed, to be too low.
Of the Neglect of Art and Virtue. To mine ingenious, dear, and
well-accomplish'd Friend, Mr. John Sandford, of Magdalen-Colledge in Oxford.
Go, forlorn Virtue, into Eden go;
and, with Leaves, hide thine outward nakedness:
Though it is their shame, not thine, that made thee so;
there make Work for the Press, far from the Press.
Tell Times to come, how much these Times neglect Thee,
in Lines as far from death, as free from dread:
And, make their Ofsprings blush that do reject Thee;
yea, live in shame when their Shames Cause is dead.
With open Hand to All, thy Largess throw;
though All are too straite-handed, unto Thee:
Make them, them selves, and thee, aright, to know;
that in thy shining Lines they, both, may see:
But, if they will be blind, and both still wrong,
Eden still keep, and sing a Sion Song.
Ye have made a mock of the counsel of the Poor. Psalm. 14.10.
THe Wisdom of the Poor, is still mispriz'd,
since by their Wealth, Men's Wits are valued:
Speak he like Solomon, he is ill advised,
say some, that understood not what he said.
But if he be an Isis-bearing Ass,
and speak what he himself, nor none conceiues,
His Praise shall, as his Folly does, surpass;
and speaks, as he no place for answer leaves.
Yet had some Kings, some Beggars in a place,
where he might not be seen, to use their Wit,
(If it were Sovereign, though his state were base)
this Sovereign would, of him, perhaps, beg It:
Which when He vents, the Peoples voice is than,
This is the voice of God, and not of Man!
Against Fortune Tellers, commonly called, Wisemen.
Since all our Knowledge from our Senses comes,
which often mistake, then must our Wisdom needs
Mistake, as often as Error ouer-comes
our Knowledge, that from ^rring Sense proceeds.
Then all our Wisdom must be most unsure,
as are the grounds from whence the same does grow;
Yet some great Wi^^ m^n har^ly can endure
that GOD should know that, which they do not know.
And, since they know, they know not as they ought;
more then they ought they seem to know, at least;
These are the^ Wi^e-men that by Fools are sought,
to s^ew ^u^nts to come to give them rest:
On whom they do^ bestow a Wise-mans ^ee,
Which th^se wise Seers, only, do foresee.
Sinful Curiosity had rather be acquainted with the Devil, then with God, or
his Saints.
Have any made a Cou'nant with black Hell,
and are Familiar with infernal Sprights?
They shall be sought to, wheresoe'er they dwell;
for, many Souls desire to see those sights.
But lives Elias (most familiar
with GOD and Heaven) where great ones most frequent,
He lives as in his fiery C^ach he were;
for, none comes near so mere an Innocent.
Thus does the Devil Lord it over the Air,
and those that most do prize It; while his slaves
Are more sought to then Saints or Angels fair,
though such Fiends bideing be among the graves:
Then, what so senseless, as the World, to take
Delight in Diu'ls, and in Hell, for their sake?
Again, of the same.
WEre Bacon, and that Vandermast alive, ( Air)
(if live they did where Men might draw but
They, with a mischie^e, would much more than thrive;
for, they would smothred be with Men's repair.
Some Bladuds would inuest them with their Robes,
nay, Crown them too, to learn them but to fly:
That so they might but glide about the Globes,
to be admired for Iack-dawes quality.
So much Men, singularity affect,
that to be singular, (though but in Toys)
They Will fre^ly give what they do most respect;
so much their inward Man, loves outward Joys:
Nay, Men to Hell will creep from out the Croude,
Ere they will be drowned in the Multitude.
That Persecutors of Truth, are their own Tormentors.
IF Virtues Sons be plagued with Vices Brood,
(since they, by Nature, still do disagree)
It Is for the vicious plague, and virtuous good;
which both shall here, or elsewhere, shortly see.
If our good life, our Enemies increase,
that bad increase, in that great good, is drowned;
Who fight against themselves, but for our Peace;
and, through our Weeds, their Hearts and Souls do wound!
Our Savior, through his Death, did Death subdue,
to make us conquer by enduring strife:
Then, what though They, to Death, do us pursue,
when, through our Death, they dye to give us li^e?
But with such proofs, none but such Saints are proved
That of his Judge, in death, was feared and loved.
The righteous, in Joy or Grief, Life, or Death, GOD keeps as his Treasure.
FEll Malice most of her own Poison drinks;
for them she plagues, do sippe but of the top;
But she of that, which to the Bottom sinckes,
to work in Her Perdition, without Hope.
Impiety and Plagues are of an age;
being burnt, not in the Hand, but in the Heart:
For, who against the good do battle wage,
shall perish through his power that takes their part.
And, if, for virtue, Men are made away,
GOD takes for sacrifice, their sufferings:
But when, by course of Nature, they decay,
he then receives them as Peace Offerings:
So that in life and death, the righteous rest,
As the Apple of his Eye, as safe, as blessed.
That men's Devotions toward God and Goodness, are most mutable.
Naught in our Life endures so many Turns,
as our Devotion; off, on, in, and out;
Now, cold as Ice, and by and by It burns,
scarce in one mood, while we can turn about.
If good we hear, perhaps we think thereon;
but be it ill, ill (past perhaps) we mind:
Thus, roll we ever, like a thriftlesse stone,
till Death us stay by force, or Course of kind.
From Sin to Sin, as Flies from sore to sore,
we still do shift; the best Men, Men are still;
The worst are worse than Beasts, to kill, or store;
for, they are leave in good, but fat in ill:
Then blessed are they, that neither fat nor leave,
Have rolled to Rest, but with the Golden-meane.
To my most honored and approved best Friend, and Alye, Sr. Fran: Louell,
Kn^ght. God takes the Will for the Deed.
ALthough we do not all the good we love,
but still, in love, desire to do ^he same;
Nor leave the Sins we hate; but ^^^ing, move
our Soul and Bodies Powers their forc^ to tame;
The good we do, GOD takes as done aright;
that we desire to do, He takes as done:
The Sin we shun, He will with Grace requi^e;
and not impute the Sin we seek to shun!
But good Desires produce no worser Deeds;
for, GOD does both together (lightly) give:
Because He knows a righteous Man must needs
by Faith that works by Love, for ever live:
then, to do naught, but only in desire,
Is Love that burns, but burns like painted fire.
The Virtuous, live well for Virtues sake; the Vicious, for fear of Punishment.
THree things, in Judgment, have observed bin,
to wo^k with wicked ones, shame, grief, and fear:
And yet without shame, grief, or fear, they sin,
till Judgment (strict) begins to appear.
If Judgment then, have force t'extort these three;
they have no Judgment, that will not prevent
This sore Extortion, with an easy fee;
that is, live well; if not, then, well repent.
But are these two performed with such ease?
Gods Y^ake is easy, and his burden light:
And such as cannot well away with these,
can neither live well, nor repent aright:
But if they cannot, much less can they bear,
What Judgment will extort, shame, grief, and fear.
That there is no peace to the Wicked.
THe Wickeds rest is like the raging Deep,
whose smoothest Peace is rough intestine War:
With whose Alar'ms they often start in sleep;
whose Heart Strings, with such fretting Stops, do jar
Yet as the Sea seems calm, as other Brooks,
till Winds arise, wherewith they rage as mad;
So, often the Wicked-man as smoothly looks
in prosperous state, as he whom GOD does glad.
And in this plight, he Saint it can as well
(at least in show) as can the holiest Saint:
Yea, can (for glory) in Good-workes excel;
and, Piety in Word and Deed depaint:
But when Afflictions flawes begin to blow,
He plays the Devil both in Deed and Show.
That a vexed Conscience is the only Hell on Earth.
THe Bodies rest does most disease the Soul,
that is diseased with Sins sorest Sting:
For then the Thoughts about that Prick do roll,
and to the Soul an Hell of Horror bring!
The Mind then looking into Fancies Mirror,
sees nothing there but Sin that sits a-Broode
On grimme Chimaeraes, and sights full of horror,
so to confound the Mind, or mad her mood.
For, when Sin only fronts the Fantasy;
that Glass reflects these horrid Forms, by kind,
Then, when the body most alone does lie,
these Monsters muster most about the Mind.
O! Plague of plagues, when Sense nor Reas'n can spy
A Real Object from a Fantasy!
Fantasy a great Comforter, or Tormenter.
Give me Jobs Botches, Naamans Leprosy,
nay, give me All that plagues the outward sense,
Rather then Terrors of the Fantasy,
crawling from out an Hell of Conscience.
And give me all the Hels the Damned endure,
(this Hell alone excepted) I will be
Able to make them Heavens, in Conscience pure,
through Operations of the Fantasy.
Asleep, awake, in Company, alone,
past-vnderstanding Peace, and Joys past Joy,
In our Minds Kingdom, then, themselves enthrone,
to ouer-sway the Pains that Flesh annoy:
Then, is this Peace and War, true Heaven and Hell,
Where Pain and Pleasure do themselves excel.
A wounded Conscience, who can bear?
SAlues, Sores do cure; and Medicines, Maladies:
Friendship, Oppression; W^sed me, poor Estate:
Favor, Restraint; and Tim^, Captiuiti^s;
Good life, Reproach; and loving Manners, Hate:
But, these, nor ought else that are ^^st, or best,
(except the Highest grace) h^u^ p^w'r to cure
A wounded spirit (with Sadness st^^l oppressed)
but It does Death out-liue, and Hell o^^ dure.
Were our Meat, Manna, our We^des, Salomons;
Monarchs- our Friends; and Eden, our Freehold;
Our Guardes, G^^ia^s; our Seates^ highst Thrones;
our Houses Silver, hung with Pearl and Gold:
All these, and all what else can Sense delight
Do rather kill, than cure a wounded Spirit.
Death makes Things appear as they are.
Envy and Anger have some Wise-me^ killed;
(though in those Passions we hold no man wise)
As favor and base flattery Fools have spild;
for, with them both, we Fools do Nestorize.
But when these moodes are, with the Parties, dead,
then, were they Fools, who were so wise while-ere:
And, They most wise that Fools were reckoned;
thus, Death does make Things, as they are, appear.
Flattery adornes Men's Fortunes, not the Men;
and Envy, not their Persons, but their Fames
Does seek to wound: so, it appears then,
that Wise nor Fools have here their proper Names:
But in the Font of Death they do receive
Their naked Names which their true Natures give.
God and Conscience tells truly what we are; and are not as we seem.
WHat thou art, ask thine Heart, and it will show;
or, ask a Foe, that Conscience makes to lie;
But ask thou no^ Self-love which cannot know:
no^, ask a Friend, which can no ^ault epsy.
If we could see our selves, then should we see
that we are nothing less then what we seem;
Yet, some seem far worse than in Deed they be;
and therefore All, this Some do not esteem.
For, we know nothing wholly, but in part,
and, understand but, what we know, by Sense:
We see the Face, but cannot see the Heart:
then shows betray our best Intelligence:
This makes all wise men, that such Secrets know,
To win the World; a Shadow, with a Show.
That Truth, being One and still the same, is made by wicked m^n to countenance
Falsehood, which is manifold, and still unlike.
WHen Peace and Truth do jar, Peace is not peace:
then, Peace, in Truth, is that we should ensue:
Now, for this Truth, what Wars and Iarres increase,
these Times do ^eele, and Aftertimes may rue.
Yet, no Man's so unjust, that will auerre,
he fights for Falsehood; but, for Truth and Right:
So, just, some say, is every unjust War:
thus, Truth is made to countenance each Fight.
Who ever yet for Heresy has did,
but says, for Truth he dies, and so believes?
Or, what Sect says not, Truth is on their side?
so, Truth is made a Devil, that deceiues:
But Truth is God, unmade, who, in the end,
Will damn them all, that make him such a Fiend,
That we are naturally bent to Ill, but supernaturally to Goodness.
TWixt Sin and Grace, I tossed am to, and fro,
as mine Affections please to bandy me:
From Grace, to Sin I fly; but, back, I go;
and yet I go as one that feign would flee.
Nature does move the Wings of my Desire
to Sinne-wards nimbly; but, not so to Grace:
For, then she limes them with my fleshes mire,
that I am forced to pass an heavy pace.
Yet still I stir those Wings, and seek to break
fail fleshes Bands; too strong, for me (too frail)
Who though, sometimes, I fail of what I seek,
yet seek I what I find, and never fail:
For, none seeks Grace that has not Grace in hold:
Then, Seekers find, though often less then they would.
Abuse is familiar with humane Flesh and Blood.
MInding this World, I muse at what I mind,
though It unworthy be of Mind or Muse:
I muse that Men are to It so inclinde,
since It minds naught, but how Men to abuse.
From high to low Abuse does proudly reign;
from which the Priest, that leads all, is not free:
The Holy hold the Holy in disdain,
if with their state, their states do not agree.
Virtue, or Vice are held or good or ill,
as, in this World, they thrive or ill, or well:
For, Vice is honored more then Virtue still,
if Vices Manors, Virtues do excel:
If Manors good, do what good Manners ought,
(That Is make men great) great men are made of naught.
That it is far better not Be, then to be Ill.
THe World (the Womb where all misdeeds are bred)
breeds in my little- World such great offense,
That my Soul, great with Sinne's delivered
of Grief, that gauls my bleeding Conscience:
The Midwife Flesh, that did the same produce,
gives it the Nurse, cursed Nature, it to feed:
And fattens It with full-Breasts of Abuse;
so, Grief grows great, with Natures gross misdeeds.
O Nature, Nurse of my Souls foul Disgrace!
o World, the Nurse of that Nurse (grounds of grief)
Why do you give me being, time, and place
since you do worse then kill me with relief?
For, that reli^se that does but nourish Sin,
Makes our Case worse, then if we never had bin.
Sin and Grace cannot dwell in one place.
IF Faith believed that Creed that Essence gives her,
then would she give the Soul what that does give:
Faith's made to know, and do that which relieues her;
for, by her active knowledge she does live!
But often the Soul (though Faith be still her Ghest)
makes Sin her Steward to provide her Food:
How then can Faith such banefull Bits digest,
which but contaminate her vital Blood?
Can Faith and Sin (if they be full in force)
dwell (as if friends they were) in one weak Heart?
No: one will other from the same divorce;
for, Sou'raignes part with life, ere Lordship part:
Then, want of Faith, with gross Sin is supplide;
For, Nature vacuum could never abide.
In rainy-gloomy Weather.
THis Weather's like my troubled Mind and Eyes:
the one, being sad; the other, full of Tears;
And, as Wind often the often Showrings dryes:
so, Sighs my Tears dry up, and kindle Cares.
Sighs please, and pain the displeased painful Heart;
they please in giving vent to Griefs vp-pent;
And yet the Heart, they ease, they cause to smart:
so, Griefs increase as Sighs do give them vent.
But were my Mind thus sad but for my Crimes,
and mine Eyes turned to Tears for cause so dear;
Or, did my Heart for that since often- times,
my Sighs, my Tears, my Sadness blessed were:
But it is, since Hope, my Ship, through Fates crosse-waue,
Now grates upon the Gravel of my Grave.
Our Wits are unable to please our Wills.
THis Life is but a Laborynth of Ils,
whose many Turnings so amaze our Minds:
that out of Them our Wit no issue finds,
But what our Sense commands, our Wit fulfils.
Yet Sense (being tired with deceitful Joys
that fleet as soon as felt) prouokes the Wit
to cast about those Turns to pleasure it,
Which finds new Pleasures lined with old Annoyes.
So, that when Sense and Wit are at a Stand
in quest of Pleasures vain variety,
they are so cloid with their sacietie,
That Will is wearyed with her own Command*
Thus, in this Life, or Laborynth of Ils,
We toil our Wits in vain, to please our Wills.
To my most dear, and no less worthily-beloued Friend and Pupil, Henry
Mainwarring Esquier, with the truely-noble and venterous Knight Sr. Henry
Thynne, accompanying, into Persia, the meritoriously-farre-renowmed Knight, Sr.
Robert Sherley, Englishman; yet, Lord Ambassador sent from the great Persian
Potentate, to all Christian Princes, for the good of Christendom.
HEroicke Pupil, and most honored Friend,
to thee, as to my Moitie, I bequeath
Half the other half; beginning, at mine end,
to make (I hope) me triumph over Death.
My Son (sole Son; and, all I ever had)
unto thy Care and Service I commend;
So, make me Sonnelesse, till you make me glad
with your Return from this Worlds further end.
The Absence of so dear a Son as thou,
must needs affect thine honored Sire with Grief;
But, for thy good, he does his Grief subdue:
so, do I mine, by his, since his is chief:
Then, with my Son, take thou my Heart and these
Celestial Charms, in Storms, to calm the Seas.
Rob not the Poor, because he is poor; neither wrong him in Judgment. Pro.
22.22.
THough Words with Wisdom richly be attirde,
yet, if their Speaker be not rich withal,
They will be rather scorned, then admired;
or thought, through Enuyes spite most criminal.
But Words pronounced by Authority,
(though no Authority does them approve)
Are held for Oracles of Deity;
and, quoted, as rich Scriptures, Truth to prove!
Thus Wisdom rare, without a Fortune rich,
is a rich Gift that gets but poor regard:
For, Wisdoms lowest voice, or highest Pitch,
if her Pipe be but mean, can never be heard:
For, the Worlds Ears, though wide, no voice can hear
That comes not from a Pipe, as dear as clear.
To my worthy* witty, long-approued, and beloved Friend, Mris. Joyce Ieffreies.
Let us hear the end: Fear God, and keep his Commandments: for this is the
whole duty of man. Eccles. 12.13.
MAny a weary Winter have I past
since first our eyes strange- looks did interchange:
But now (dear Friend, that is; as kind, as fast)
Time, in Life's Reere, or vital- Powers does range.
My Lays of Love, are now turned all to Psalms
and Hymns addressed to Heaven; which my young days
Did most offend: Then, now, I crave this Alms,
that thou, for me, on These, our God wilt praise.
When Time, and Thwarts have taught the humbled- sp'rit
that all, save love and fear of God, is vain;
By Grace and Nature we take most delight
in pains, which may prevent eternal pain:
Then, if thy Will do match thy Wit (dear Friend)
On these Feet may thou travel to that END.
The CONCLUSION.
LO here an end of these our Muses Flights,
which aimed at Man's End, or chiefest GOOD;
But if too wild she were, in heavenly Heights,
let her be made to know it, by the W^ood.
If her Disire too high has made her rise,
(though less than GOD contents not that Desire)
Of Christ, and of his Church she mercy cries;
and, humbly, stoupes to what they do require.
Yea, prostrate, she does fall; nay, vailes her Neck
under his Spouse (the Church) her sacred Feet;
Submitting all her Soarings to her Check;
and ready to reform what she thinks meet:
If she has rightly fled, GOD made that Flight:
If not, she prays the Church to make it right.
But, knew I ought offensive to her Ear,
My Tears should blot It, yer It mended were.
FINIS.

under the Blacke-bell within Temple-barre. 1612.
